THE BED PAGE.
A CANADIAN POET
I.
Collected Poems of Wilfred Campbell
I (Lond., Fleming H. Revell Co.; 6s. net)
J- represents the life-work of one of the most
important Canadian verse-writers. It is a
bulky volume of 350 pages, containing some in-
teresting material, and a great deal that is com-
monplace. The first thing an Australian reader
notices, is Campbell’s occasional duplication of
O’Dowd’s mind and motive. “ Soul,” for ex-
ample, imitates the philosophy of “The Silent
Land.” Campbell commences :
Wind of the wide world’s mantled thought,
About the vague vast blowing ;
This truth my wayward heart hath caught,
That being hath more doors than thought,
And life is more than knowing.
And he may be supposed to continue (omitting
a line of his stanza to enforce the parallel, and
omitting here and there a stanza):
The creeds of darkness or of mind
Are but the scaly bark
That slips from off the oenturied rind,
Amid the crannied dark.
And deeper than the builded theme
Of priest or book or seer,
There lies that life, that subtle dream
That wakes the upward year.
And greater than all thoughts that fall
Prom page or poet’s song,
That dim impulse behind it all
That finds no written tongue.
This impulse in each being rife,
Deep hidden in each man,
This inward, mystic flame of life,
The blessing or the ban :
It is the master of all thought,
All impulse and all dream,
And builds or ruins, base or not,
The blackness or the gleam.
It gives through some weird inward need
The centuries’ impulse birth ;
And weaves in subtle dream or deed,
All tragedies of earth.
It kindled Homer’s golden song
Of elemental man,
And lurks behind the fateful throng,
From Christ to Caliban.
It is that greater self behind
All earth’s confused gleam,
That leads men up by stairways blind
To heaven of their dream.
At all earth’s altars it hath knelt,
.Sought God ’mid stars and dew,
Wherever craving ages felt
The agony of the few.
All sorrows, passions, all delights,
All hopings, all despairs,
All earth’s old splendours, ail her blights,
Her ruined starward stairs ;
Her songs, her battles, her grim blades
Forged in her caves of dream —
Yea, all that glories, all that fades,
Was cradled in its gleam.
Greater than mightiest thoughts of mind
That measures life by rule,
It soars in dreams of God, behind
The Plato or the fool..
In every common hour of life,
In every flame that glows,
In every breath of being rife
Man feels more than lie knows.
Earth’s child of science counts the stars
Upon'God’s garment’s hem :
He plumbs the seas, the heavens’ bars,
liebuilds the rarest gem ;
But blind as night to that within,
That demon, god, ,or elf,
That universe of dreams that spin,
That heaven or hell in “ self ” :
That something subtle that outweighs
The mightiest lore of man ;
That master of his dreams and days,
Behind his bliss or ban :
Which lifted Shakespeare from the clod,
Yet spake in Caliban ;
That god in man, or man in god.
That dreamed since life began.
That outsoared Shelley’s lark in flight,
Beyond all dreams we know ;
T.iat. knew with Milton music's might,
Or Paganini’s bow.
That same dim impulse Saxon, Celt,
Mohawk or Tartar knew ;
That in old Shylock’sagony felt
The tragedy of the Jew.
We have mutilated the verse to show the like-
ness—but the likeness is there. If O’Dowd did
not write this, who did? Campbell, apparently.
And he continues :
Wind of the wide world’s winnowed dream,"
About the vague vast blowing ;
Beyond our futile taper-gleam
Of priestly creed and poet’s theme,
God’s tides of night are flowing.
Man feels the present, feels the past,
As one born blind may know
The sUn, the earth, the rain or blast,
Or those dread phantom shadows cast,
His brother men who go.
But round about the dreams we are,
In caves of wind and fire,
Where mind is cabined ; soul afar,
Doth rise eternal, star to star,
To heights of God’s desire.
In other verses, such as “ Genius,” Campbell
approaches so closely to O’Dowd that one can
hardly distinguish thought from thought. In
“ The Wind Dancer” he exhibits in his own way
O’Dowd’s metre :
He is the master of the mirth
Of field and stream and tree ;
And of the dreamers of the wood,
The lord of revels, he.
Till Summer and her dream depart,
And leaf and gleam be done,
He holds the whole world’s laughing heart,
This dancer in the sun.
There are other poems in which the methods
of the two are almost identical. One has never seen
a nearer parallel between writers so far removed
in life. Possibly it is another argument for Our
Imperialistic Destiny.
A LIMB OF THE LAW
JC. WITT was a Lond. barrister in
m good standing, who—before dying
recently from the effects of a 'bus
accident—emitted a very shrewd and
cheerful book of legal anecdote and com-
mentary earned by thirty years of travail.
Life in the Law (Lond., T. W. Laurie)
appeals especially to lawyers, but an intel i-
gent laity may read it with interest anc
profit. The author’s clear, strong mind
reflected in his style, and he never proses
maunders. Once he touches Australia, bu;
the touch is not consoling :
In my early dayswe had fifteen incendiary fir
in one year in the village, and Cottenham w
twice destroyed by fire. No one was prosecutec
for these crimes, and the offence of arson diec
out when the discovery of gold in Austral a
tempted the most desperate of our neighbours no
leave us for their own and our good.
Well, probably “sturdy Etruria” wa
founded in much the same way.
J*
Witt has some reflective passages on th
attitude of counsel to witnesses. What h
would say of the tone adopted in some Syd -
ney police-courts is easily to be imagined.
In the good old days a learned counsel of fero
cious mien and loud voice, practising before Mi
Justice Maule, received a "magnificent rebuke from
that famous judge. No reply could be got from
an elderly lady in the box, and the counsel ap
pealed to the learned judge. “I really canoe .
answer,” said the trembling lady. “Why not,
ma’am ?” asked the judge. “Because, my lore,
he frightens me so.’”®\ “So he does me, ma’am,
said the judge.
Yet—
It is by no means difficult for a witness to tun
the tables on counsel. My friend, the late Mr
George Williams, did not like giving evidence ii
horse cases, and he was rather too candid a man
for the work. But, of course, there were occasion
on which, as the busiest man in his profession, h
could not help himself. He was called to prov
that a horse was a “ roarer.” Serjeant Ballantin
was on the other side, and in his best Mephi
stophelian manner he said to the witness, “If you
say that my client’s horse was a roarer, just rep
resent to. the jury the sort of noise he made.’
“No,'’ said Mr. Williams, “you see, that is no
my business. Now, if you will be the horse anc
make the noise, I, as veterinary surgeon, will de
termine whether you are a roarer or not.” This
concluded the cross-examination.
Of “ business men ” as witnesses Witt has
a poor opinion :
Business men, in spite of the frock coats and
sober neckties which they invariably assume out
of respect for the Court, and in assert,on o;
their own respectability, are very seldom goot
witnesses. Nearly all money-makers are stupid
If anyone denies this proposition, let him recal
ttie dinners at which he has been present with
successful seekers after wealth, and the utter
dreariness of those functions. Tne reason is that
money is made by concentration of the faculties
on that one pursuit. But although these people
are stupid, they are not at all conscious ot their
deficiency. They regard cross-examination as a
duel, in which they are to pit their brains against
those of counsel. They are therefore on the alert
to guess at the drift of the questions, and to make
their answers fit in with what they suppose to be
the theory, of their case, with the result that at
the end of half an hour they are in a hopeless
tangle. It never occurs to them that if their case
will not admit of the truth being told it must be a
bad one.
Sailors also admit of explanation, if not of
excuse:
bailors in collision cases have earned a very
different character. The late Mr. Britchard was
marshalling the evidence to be adduced on behalf
of his ship, and worried the witnesses in a vain
effort to reconcile their statements, until at last
the captain said, “it is of no use to bother any
more about it. Just jou writedown what I and
the mate and the crew are to swear to, and I w’ill
see that it is done.” This was. too much even for
the nerves of an Admiralty solicitor of forty
years’ experience.
Lord Hannen once held forth to me on the sad
lack of truth.among his nautical witnesses...
I offered him an explanation, which I venture to
think is the true one. The sailor is loyal to his
flag. He has signed articles for a voyage on a
ship. He gets board and lodging and wages from
the owner. His captain is his superior officer,
whom he lias to obey and to help in scorm and
stress. In a damage suit the purse of the owner
and the character of the oaptain are put in
jeopardy. His plain duty is to save both, and he
thinks that duty is just as binding on him in a
court of law as in a tempest.
A keen comment on the “relationship”
difficulty :
“If,” said a learned counsel, “I get a brief
from my brother’s firm everyone says, ‘He only
got it because the solicitor is his brother,’ and if I
don’t get the brief then everyone says, ‘What
sort of a counsel must he be when even his own
brother will not trust him with the case.’ ”
As to the expert who is always with us
or against us :
Public morality would be advanced if all expert
testimony were abolished in courts of law and at
arbitrations. Ihe Admiralty system of assessors
ought to be universally adopted. Suppose that a
man is injured and brings an action to recover
compensation, the court ought to name a surgeon
to examine the plaintiff one week before the trial,
and to write out a full report to be delivered to
the judge, with a copy for the counsel on either
side. The surgeon who has attended the man
could, of course, be called to state the facts, but
he should not be allowed to depose to the’con-
dition of the plaintiff at the time of the trial, or
to offer any opinion as to the probable date of
recovery. The same rule should be applied as
regards building, engineering and all other cases
where it is now the practice to adduce expert
evidence. Tp call three surveyors on oath t
appraise a property on behalf of the owners £
£lO,OOO, and then to call three surveyors on beha
of the company to swear that its value is on!
£3OOO, is a scandal of high degree. The conflic
of surgical opinion is not so bad because th
element of uncertainty is less great, but there i
quite enough to justify a reform in this direction
Mr. Justice €ave inspires a bright page o
two. It was he who, when dealing with th
law as to beer-drinking on Sunday, spoke o
a bona-fide thirst as the true test of a bona
fide traveller.
One of his finest efforts was when the counse
for the plaintiff had called four witnesses withou
advancing his case at all. The learned Judge
said, “These are very bad witnesses, very bac
indeed. Have you got a good one ? ” “ Oh, yes
my lord,” said the counsel. “ Then call him a
once,” said the Judge; “if you keep him any
longer he will go bad like the rest.”
Witt’s opinion on betting has some loca
relevancy :
Personally, I consider that backing horses is
waste of time and energy, but where is the harn
of it? The losses in betting incurred by mechanics
artisans, and labourers, in town and country, are
absurdly small compared with their useless anc
pernicious expenditure in drink. As for crime
every young rascal spoofs the magistrate wit!
lamentations that he has been tempted in to stealing
through backing horses, because lie knows per
fectly well that he will get a short lecture insteac
of a long sentence, and half the bankrupts who
prefer not to state the truth as to what has become
of missing money tell the registrar that it has
gone on the turf. In my early days thero was
plenty of big betting, and I was not brought up
near Newmarket without knowing what was
going on, but it was among very few people.
Now betting is universal, but in small amounts.
Education and the cheap Press, and nothing else,
have brought this about.
The humbler classes of readers whose lives are
dull find profit and pleasure in the study of these
journals, and horse-racing forms for them a subject
of interest and conversation, gives them some-
thing to puzzle over, and is fine exercise for the
memory. However, if Parliament thinks that
betting is very awful, and desires oo try its hand
at a little more empirical legislation, lawyers will
not grumble, and I think that I may venture to
say that there will not be one single bet less made
in consequence.
On the virtue of Parliament :
The Common Law of England, tempered with
Equity, constitutes the highest expression of
human reason and morality. The laws made
from time to time by Parliament are, for the most
part, opposed to good sense and morality.
There must be a Parliament. Its primary
office is to prevent arbitrary power in man or
multitude, to hear grievances, to determine after
what manner and by whom the country shall be
ruled. History teaches that these duties have
been discharged splendidly. Every man of right
mind does his best to secure that Parliament shall
go on with this work. Other nations follow its
example, apd strive to emulate it.
Lord Palmerston understood and upheld the
dignity and authority of Parliament. Hd also
appreciated its follies. He never, with the notable
exception of the Probata and Divorce Acts, in-
dulged in legislation if he could help it. He
knew that statutes, as a rule, do much more harm
than good.
Why does every session bring forth a crop of
Acts altering the general law of the land ? The
answer is that no Government can afford to pro-
duce asking's speech without a list of projected
statutory reforms. It would be like a public
dinner without a toast list, a race meeting with-
out a card, a play without a programme. Think
of the Opposition. “ Here is a Government with-
out a policy. Where are its measures for the
reform of abuses? where is its construeti velegisla-
tion ? what heed does it give to the crying needs
the people ? ” Away with it!—and away it would
go.
There have been many statutes of extraordinary
beneficence, but they have been-Acts to rSpeal
wicked old Acts. For example, Acts to relieve
Catholics, Nonconformists, .Jews, and Quakers
from pains, penalties and disabilities imposed in
bad old times by malevolent kings and yet more
malevolent priests; Acts to get men out of
prison, who would otherwise have been kept there
by cruel despots and yet more cruel creditors ;
Acts to prevent rich men from fixing poor men’s
wages, or regulating their dresses, or determining
the prices of goods in defiance of laws that will
not be defied. All these are Statutes to knock
down something already put up, and only prove
the folly and injustice of older law makers.
J*
Witt’s book is emphatically quotable, for
every page is packed with sense and wit ;
and the solidity of the performance gives it
a permanent value. With this, as an ex-
pert lawyer’s opinion upon the multiplication
of laws, quotation may end :
Modern legislation is based upon the theory
that an official, whose interest in work l>egins and
ends with his salary, can do our business better
than we can do it for ourselves. All this of course
means curtailment of that individual liberty,
which is the most precious of human rights. We
ca t n boast that we put down the Stuarts, that we
have survived the"oligarchy of the early Georgian
Whigs, and the absolutism of the later Georgian
Tories. These were political triumphs, and j we
are right to be proud of the men who achieved
them.' But our political liberty will be of small
value if we are condemned to the loss of personal
and civil liberty, and if Parliament goes on
unchecked in its present course of legislation, we
shall have to get up a new revolution, hang all local
officials to the nearest lamp-posts, and make huge
bonfires of rules, orders and bye-laws throughout
the country.
A SCHOOL-HOOK.
A New Primer of English Literature, by T.
Tucker and W. Murdoch, is published by Wb
combe & Tombs (Melb.). -To \y*ite such a boo
is a difficult task, and the authors have earnec
their groarf of satisfaction at completion. T
object of the ideal primer, they say, “would I
simply to provide an intelligent bird’s eye vie
of the whole stream of our literary history
Upon its level, and within its limits, their wor
attains this object. It is not “written down”
the childish mind, and rightly ; for good ed
national practice follows psychological theory :
exciting interest with the new and in stimulatin
faculty with the untried. Conceivably, in Au
tralian schools, a shorter and.simpler treatise ma
be interposed between the reading-books and th
book; but such a treatise must be barren of a
but cardinal facts and ideas, and A New Prime
supplies the subject's imperative need of expan
sion. It has a definite place in the scheme o
inquiring scholars aged from twelve to fourteei
and it takes its place admirably. The authors
conform to conventional standards; but the
critical spirit moves freely and brightly, and th
practical obligation to compile has not preventec
them from supplying original aspects. A jus!
appreciation of Chaucer, for example, can
drawn from a score of sources beside his writings
and, for text-book purposes, there is nothing t
add to a familiar verdict; yet the authors here,
often elsewhere, manage to deliver the verdic
with a new' voice, attractively. (There wer
thirty pilgrims to Canterbury gathered at th
Tabard, by the way—not twenty-nine; for th
story-teller was going too.)
d*
The weakness of the book is to stop shor
at the uncultivated court of Queen Caroline, o
thereabouts; and the note to the very inade
quate chapter on the “Victorian Period” is
confession of weakness. The authors have n
warrant to presume the student’s acquaintanc
with recent writers ; and in any case, that know
ledge is what their book exists to supply. “Th
world has not yet made up its mind as to what th
distinctive characteristics of the period reall
are ” ; and if that be so, there is all the moreneec
for the authors to make a beginning of the task
They should help in the work of decision, anc
should refuse to follow in the rut and accep
other people’s choses jugies. It is precisel
in regard to reoent and contemporar;
writers that a “ new primer ” is wantec
for there are many old primers to reiterate th
praise or blame of the old authors. At the breac
of honour and difficulty A New Primer fails a
lamentably as the rest.
The book is well and readably written ;thoug:
one objects to words like “ liberal-mindedness
and as far as it goes, has few shortcomings tha
cannot be justified. In the reviewer’s opinion
a section should have been devoted to the Percy
ballads and their kindred; and the influence o
Maepherson’s Ossian should have been stressed
The second James Thomson certainly should have
been mentioned among Victorian poets. There
is a curious blunder, at p. ICO.
Every one of the novels of Tobias Smollett is picaresque
n form—that is to say, the incidents have no connection
with each other or with the conclusion, but are simply
string of adventures happening to the same person.
The Spanish gusto picaresco, or picaresque
fashion in fiction, has nothing to do with the form
of the story. The picaresque novel, from Laza
rillo de Tormes downwards, is simply a narrative
of roguish life and adventure (Span, picaresco,
roguish, knavish), and the English transference o;
subject and description is correctly suggested by
the authors on a previous page (79).
J*
We suppose it is still too soon to hope for a
primer of English literature which shall deal only
with what is worth children’s knowledge, elimin-
ating the always-accumulating mass of scholastic
lumber, leaving historical detail to advanced
students, and employing the brief available year
or two in transfusing the beauty and the glory.
Andrew Carnegie tells stories of Boerntrager,
one of his partners in the steel-making industry:
Boerntrager had always one speech. It was short and
straight to the point. “Gentlemens,” said he, “what
we want is this—get prices up, and costs down ; and
every man must stand on his own legs.”
Boerntrager’s mind was so centred upon his work that
he could not talk upon any subject without using the
language of the steel-mill. For instance, soon after his
marriage to a most estimable lady of short stature,
Carnegie met him, and offered congratulations.
“ Did you get a perfect wife, William ? ” he asked.
“Veit, Mr. Carnegie,” replied Boerntrager, “maybe
she might have been improved if she had got von more
pass troo de rolls.”
Viticulture in New Zealand (with especial refer-
ence to Amer. vines) is a fully illustrated manual
to prove that Maoriland can make our Chianti, if
not our champagne. We hope it can. The
author—Romeo Bragato, Govt, viticulturist—has
written a sensible, practical, useful little work.
The Religion of Ancient Scandinavia and The
Mythology of Britain and Ireland are two more
volumes in the lucid series of “ Religions Ancient
and Modern” (Lend., Constable; ls. net). The
latter, especially, will warm the old instincts of
Welsh and Scottish and Irish-Australians. One
feels to grasp theTuatha de Danann, for example,
as one never grasped those cloudy personages
before.
“Agricola” thus:
I am descended from a long line of blacksmiths, tracing
from my grandfather, in an unbroken line for 400 years.
I, myself, was reared entirely away from the “forge,”
except for an occasional visit t.o the local smith when our
horse wanted shoeing. Yet, whenever lamin a smithy,
watching the blacksmith mend a plough or some other
implement, I instinctively hand him the next tool he will
require. This habit is so marked that on several occa-
sions the blacksmith has accused me of having worked at
the trade. -
Impressions of a Victorian Abroad, also Con-
fessions of a Naughty Boy in the Fifties, by J.
Bear (Melb.; J. T. Picken) includes reminiscences
of early Melbourne and elderly impressions of a
trip to Europe. Thalatter are commonplace and
tedious ; the former readable and pleasant, though
the gossip holds nothing of importance.
A.G.S.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Karachiensis Re “Sheelah’s” remarks
on the keen smell capacity of the small red ant
(B. 24/5/’O6), I differ from him. The ant, red,
black, or otherwise,' is not possessed of anything
extraordinary in the way of smell power. Brother
ant happens, quite promiscuously, to come upon
a tit-bit—dead by preference. He takes a bite
just to see if it’s worth the. subsequent trouble. He
lingers a little while round the object to admire
it, and if it’s more than he can carry or
pull himself, he makes tracks for" home.
That ant, as he goes, keeps pressing
his caudal appendage to the ground, or
anything else he is travelling over, and leaves
on it a microscopic trail of a peculiar ant fluid
all the way to his nest. Any other ant coming
across this trail at once stops, picks up the
“ scent,” and runs along it till he finds the object.
Meanwhile, ant No. 1, the original prospector, has
arrived at his nest. In a second you will find <
hundreds of ants pouring out and going off in a
regular line along the trail. But let anyone break
the trail by running a damp cloth over a portion
of it, or strewing it with cigar-ash, and that
marching army will be thrown into confusion. '
The ants wiil scatter in all directions trying to
pick up the trail again, and, if they cannot do so
in a short time, they will scoot back to the nest.
And yet the object they are looking for may be
only a yard away, it may be large enough to.
attract the attention of even an idiot ant,
and it may be pouring forth a perfect cataract of
flavor.
“San”: Your precious “Quambat,” or
“ Dingbat ” (any name is good enough for him)
(B. 16/8/’O6), is an aboliar. This is how I found out.
I have a neighbor who has been having his house
made from a Gothic to a Queen Anne villa. His
men take their lunch out in the garden near my
fence. What more natural than that my half-
grown St. Bernard should root a hole under the
fence, and sit in front of the row of workmen, in
various ways urging his claim to a share
of the tucker. At first the men seemed
friendly to the pup, and gave him scraps. Later,
they got tired and threatened to poison him.
So I kept him on the chain. One day he escaped
and I saw one of the wretches throw him a large
piece of steak, which was swallowed at a gulp.
Madly I rushed down the garden path and called
the pup. He came. I followed “Wombat’s”
system for restoring the apparently poisoned.
Seizing the pup by the tail, I began to slowly
swing him round my head. My word, he was
heavy. The men, hearing the rumpus, lined the
fence with a row of grinning heads, “ ’E’s doin’
Sandow, Bill,” said one to his mate. “Wonder
’ow the pup likes it ? ” said the laborer’s assistant.
This made me so wild I gave an extra swing
and the pup’s head went bang against a post.
He gave one gasp, and all was over. He
never got sick, and the next day he was just as
dead as he was the day previous. And the steak
hadn’t been poisoned after all.
In 1901 the Queensland Parliament gave
Private Enterprise the chance it says it is always
craving for to build a railway, and open up the
country, and show how much better it can do
these things than the State can. The line (Nor-
manton to Cloncurry) was to be finished and
working, and supplying a great object lesson,
inside five years. The five years have expired,
and, as usual, Private Enterprise hasn’t put a
pick in the ground, so the little deposit it put up
will probably be forfeited and the bargain declared
off.
Woman’s Fate.
Our Master, Time, amid his wealth,
Counts to each man a golden store ;
Some spend in haste, some hoard in stealth,
. But unto no man grants he more.
Coins newly-minted, red and white,
He counted into my cupped hands ;
Ah, but they shone so big and bright,
To answer all demands.
Where’er I went I heard the cry,
“ Spare me one hour ! ” “ And me a day !”
“Give of thy riches!” How could I
Speak churlishly and tell them “ Nay ” ?
Here, from my grasp bright hours they caught,
And there, I flung a careless day ;
For worthless dues, in gifts of naught,
I cast my wealth away.
And then Love met me. Royal, he,
And generous. “Keep thy gold,” he said,
“ My moment spans eternity ! ”
But in chat moment he had fled.
My all I laid before him there;
“ One moment yet !” I cried. In vain.
I saw his bright plumes cleave the air,
I met him not again.
And then Life met me. Some were free
To take unpriced his wondrous wares ;
Some he denied. Alas, from me
He took fine gold for petty cares.
Before my time he left me old,
Bankrupt in gladness, hope and health !
My nerveless fingers scarce can hold
The remmtnt of my wealth.
Oh, slow decline ! Oh, small distress !
How to endure ye? Fain would I
Rather, for all I yet possess,
Breathe one full breath—and die.
QUEENSLAND.
A.W.
The Recluse.
We dream among the nodding daffodils
And big blush roses in a garden old,
While sunset on the great eternal hills
Burns red and gold*
The brook that murmurs on its winding way
Is music, floating light as a caress
Through green arcades’ where rosy sunbeams play
In loveliness.
The calm contentment of a summer sea
Sighs drowsily from far deep coral caves.
We hear at times in rolling melody
The wash of waves.
I think I see your sordid nightmare town
At times, from out the haven of our rest—
A hideous study in a bilious brown
Of man unblessed;
Of bartered lust, of swains too poor to wed ;
Of god in garret, sot in gilded sty;
Of fat men feasting off the great unfed ;
Hung all awry.
I have no stomach for the poor man’s crust.
A cup of tears is but a bitter draught.
Your life a threadbare theme of “ dust to dust,”
A joke outlaughed.
I crave no tear-stained gold, no purchased smile;
No rusty crown, or painted blandishment.
In lotus languor on my garden isle
I rest content.
VIC.
FRANK HUDSON.
The Traveller.
He started out to brave the world
With high-held head and gallant mien,
When morning clouds were looped and furled,
And all the new wet grass was green.
-A buoyant, bright and.joyous youth,
He whistled down the morning wind ;
He saw the glowing peaks of Truth
Ear-off, in cloudless skies defined.
To climb their heights was his whole thought—
To rest upon their summits sure.
To hold his heart unsold, unbought,
Unspoiled, unstained, and ever pure.
Right well equipped for night and day
Was he, that youth of bearing fine ;
He had some dreams wherewith to pay
For bread and meat and wayside wine.
But sadness tames the soul that sings,
And time transformed the glowing boy;
He saw so much, and heard such things,
. That anger took the place of joy.
The dust and heat of many a mile
Made dry his throat with drouth accurst;
He paused at tavern doors to smile,
And ask for wine to wet his thirst.
But, since he had no gold to pay,
They gave him greeting scant and cold ;
“ We are not witless folk,” said they,
“ To trade in dreams instead of gold.”
He found the heart of man a rock,
Untouched, though many tears he shed ;
His dreams were but a worthless stock
That would not buy him daily bread.
He saw the courts of gilded vice
Where thronged gay rogues and painted dames,
And all the world with cards and dice
Sat smiling at its cut-throat games.
He longed for power to stay or stem
The tide of lies that rolled far spread—
For Gabriel’s trump to summon them,
And Michael’s sword to strike them dead.
And so untired from youth to age,
Inspired by Truth and armed with Right,
The world beheld this dreamer wage
His vain, preposterous, foolish fight.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

Model 1905 Self-Loading Rifle
This rifle is a six shot, hammerless take-down, made in .32 and .35 calibers,
the cartridges being of the modern smokeless powder type. The recoil of
the exploded cartridge ejects the empty shell, cocks the hammer and feeds
a fresh cartridge from the magazine into the chamber, leaving the rifle ready
to shoot upon the operator’s pulling the trigger. The self-loading system
adds much to the pleasure of rifle shooting, either at target or game. The
list price of this model is $28.00.
These Rifles For Sale By All Dealers.
WINCHESTER REPEATING ARMS CO., NEW HAVEN, CONN., U.S.A.

The Advantages of Kutnow’s Powder.
SIR JAMES CRICHTON BROWNE said that a prolongation of
life is not desirable unless that life be vigorous and happy.
Many of the quick-killing diseases that involve but brief
disability and suffering, like typhus and typhoid fever, are decreasing,
while slowly degenerative diseases, involving protracted invalidism
and much misery, like Bright’s disease, diabetes, and cirrhosis of the
liver, are mounting up. Society has always had to pay for every
mistake it makes, except when the habit of hygienic correction is
cultivated. PROFESSOR LAWSON TAIT, M.D. recognised the
importance of removing the debris of undigested food the morning
after the banquet, and not only prescribed Kutnow’s Powder in his
hospital and private practice, but used it personally for years with
unqualified success. The secret of health is often found in the
elimination of uric acid, and this is easily achieved by the use of
Kutnow’s Powder. It cleanses the stomach, rouses the liver, flushes
the kidneys, and clears the bowels. By this simple means the errors
of excess in diet and drink are corrected, and the poisonous debris
removed from the system.

Pbiob 6n.
“ First, the speech should evidence the
quality ot the speaker’s mind. Here, the
keeping of the body In a healthy state was
necessary.” —Bruce Smith, to the C.A.
debaters last week.
Bruce : “ Now, my lads, you have here an
example of the perfect mind In the
perfect body.”
HER HATED RIVAL.
In a recent Sydney Divorce Court, plaintiff deposed that, since the new
Liquor Act, her husband used to bring home a demijohn of beer and spend the

lluiv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin, ” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY. SEPTEMBER 13, 1906.
DIVORCE.
The suggestion, which has been made, to step
backward in regard to the N.S. Wales divorce
law gains more importance from the fact that, in
the natural order of things, the Australian Parlia-
ment will shortly deal with the question of
divorce for the Commonwealth. At present the
N.S. Wales Act is the most liberal in Australia ;
that is to say, it gives the most generous facilities
for amending matrimonial mistakes. If it should
be decided to retrace the steps taken in that
direction in N.S. Wales, the decision will, with-
out a doubt, profoundly affect the delibera.
tions of the higher legislature when it comes
to shape the law for the continent. That would
be an unfortunate result, for the N.S. Wales
law represents the minimum freedom of divorce
compatible with justice and common-sense.
It is true that there are many arguments which
can be advanced against it on grounds of religious
sentiment. But these deserve no consideration
in matters of secular legislation. If, for instance,
the Roman Catholic and High Anglican view were
taken of divorce, there would be no legal means at
all of breaking the marriage bond ; and the im-
morality of loveless unions, and their consequent
irregular amours, would go unchecked.' Other re-
ligious views, whilst not so extreme, would suggest
much more strict limitations than exist at present.
All respect should be given to these opinions when
they are honestly held, and a secular divorce law,
however liberal, grants them that respect by leav-
ing its provisions to be taken advantage of or not,
just as the persons concerned choose. The
Roman Catholic whose marital relations are
unhappy is not forced to seek relief. If
his, or her, religion preaches the propriety of
putting up with the evils of an unhappy married
life, instead of seeking a remedy, there is no com-
pulsion. But it is not logical that those who
do not have such religious scruples .should yet have
to suffer for them in others. The M« '.thodist, s or the
Unitarian, or the Agnostic, who does not regard
it as a pious duty to continue in a marriage which
is stained by adultery, or made horrible by
drunkenness or violence, should not be kept to a
bond which he does not consider right or moral,
because some other religious person’s ideas on the
subject are different. A liberal divorce law is
fair to all religious opinions. Those who have
conscientious scruples against taking advantage
of its provisions are in no way forced to do so.
Even in the case of “ mixed marriages,” and a
divorce being sought by one party, who does
believe in divorce, against another party who
doesn’t, there is no real wrong done. The non-
believer in divorce had a right to consider that
eventuality before making the contract; and, after
making it, should have avoided giving such offence
as justified divorce proceedings.
* * * *
The particular section of the N.S. Wales
Divorce Act against which protest is now being
made in some quarters, is that which allows a
divorce to be obtained on disobedience to a decree
for “the restitution of conjugal rights.” It is
allowed in matrimonial jurisdiction for one party
to a marriage to sue for an order to restore what
is the essence of the marriage contract. If the
husband refuses “conjugal rights ” to the wife, she
may, on establishing the fact, get an order from the
court, directed to him, to restore those rights.
And the husband has the same remedy in the
case of the wife’s refusal. Should that order be
disobeyed the Court does not ventui'e to attempt
to enforce it, but accepts the disobedience as valid
ground for a decree in divorce. In effect, Mr. B.
refuses to be any longer a husband to Mrs. B. ; so
their marriage is annulled. It is an eminently
sensible law, since, when a marriage has ceased to
be a marriage in fact, it should cease to be a mar-
riage in law, and the parties to it should be
allowed, if they so choose, to try for conjugal hap-
piness in other quarters. But the objection is
made that the provision enables “collusive”
divorces. Mrs. B. is thoroughly tired of Mr. 8.,
but shrinks from the social sin of seeking consola-
tion with Mr. C. Mr. B. is just as convinced
that he cannot be happy with M'rs. 8., but
hesitates to form an irregular alliance with some-
one else. The law now allows them to get
the relief of divorce without committing adultery,
since one or the other can “ refuse conjugal
rights, ’ can disobey the decree for their resti-
tution, and a divorce follows. What grievance
is there in this ? If both parties to a marriage
are convinced that it is a failure, what is the use
of keeping them bound together, or of refusing
them freedom from the bond except at the price
of a social sin on one side or the other ? In the
majority of cases, if the unhappy married couple
are resolved on disunion and find the “conjugal
rights ” way barred, they will probably take some
other path. I here is a I’alais Hoy a] farce in
which husband and wife, over the morning chop,
agree that they must be divorced, and amiably
discuss which one of the two will commit adultery
and thus open the gate to freedom. That sort of
thing is hardly what the religious objectors to the
liberality of the N.S.W. Divorce Act aim at; but
it would be the natural consequence of the success
of their agitation.
• * • •
It is useless for the secular law to lean on a
religious sentiment which does not exist. If a law
seeks to enforce an idea of sex morality which is
alien to the general sentiment of the community
it will necessarily fail. And so long as the law
does not, in allowing freedom to all, force freedom
on those who do not want it, there is no valid
reason for any complaint. The religious can go
on observing their old rules and exposing their
votaries to all the dangers and evils of irregular
alliances; those who believe in the divorce law
can take advantage of its provisions. The State’s
business is not to interfere with anybody’s
religious convictions, but it is its function to
encourage happy and fruitful marriages; and part
of that encouragement consists in providing for the
rectification of the percentage of errors which
necessarily occur in the making of matches. In
this regard it is worth notiug that it is mostly
the childless marriage which comes to the Divorce
Court. Fruitful marriages are more rarely sun-
dered.
» • • »
Those who profess to see in reasonable divorce
facilities a reversion from monogamy argue
against the facts. The general tendency in
civilised countries is rather to strengthen the
monogamic system. Civilised man, following an
indoor occupation, is less ardent than his open-
air forefathers. Civilised woman tends—through
her different type of education and for other
reasons not easily discussed—in the other
direction. The original sex disparity in
mankind which made polygamy a natural
institution is rapidly being effaced by civilised
life, and the effect is to buttress monogamic insti-
tutions. Another influence working m the same
direction is the increased complexity of life,
which makes the “charm” naturally attracting
a man and a woman a very much less simple mat-
ter than of old. The primeval courtship, when
a man looked over the figure of a girl, saw that it
was good, and proposed to her with a club, has
vanished. Nowadays the average civilised man
demands in a woman whom he wishes to make
his mate a whole train of qualities which appeal
to him ; and the woman, on her part, is scarcely less
exigent in her demands. The result is a general
rule of careful courtship, and of stable marriages.
Two people wdio have mated after very careful
mutual examination usually come to the sensible
conclusion—even if they find their marital life
hardly up to the ideal they had formed—that,
anyhow, they have probably done as well as was
possible, and that a change would most likely be
for the worse. Which attitude represents, on the
whole, the truth. Marriage is rarely the perfect
union sung of by the poets, but in a civilised com-
munity it is generally a fairly happy partnership
of two people, who have, by living together,
acquired many mutual tastes; who are bound
together by their children; and who find pas-
sion fading away very comfortably into
quiet affection. There is little fear in civilised
communities of any divorce facilities leading to a
general weakening of home life. A proportion
of the idle rich will always desecrate and abuse
marriage, as they do all other social institutions. A
section of the desperate poor will, from the other
pole of society, adopt the same anarchist attitude.
But the great body of the people in all civilised
countries is becoming, through education, increas-
ingly ethical and increasingly common-sensible,
and, therefore, to an increasing extent, mono-
gamic. Divorce facilities will do nothing to dis-
courage that tendency. On the contrary, by
giving relief to the victims of mistakes, they will
‘assist in establishing the stability of marriage.' A
man or a woman denied relief, and tied by the
law to an unhappy home, is apt to be driven to
desperation, and may become the means of de-
stroying the happiness of other homes if not given
a second chance of an honest family life.
WORKS FUNDS, SURPLUSES AND
OTHER SWINDLES.
As the politicians put it, N.S. Wales had a
surplus for the year to June 30th, 1906, of
£896,124. But in matters of finance the poli-
ticians of N.S. Wales are not always to be
trusted; and Joseph Carruthers, Treasurer, is
not to be trusted above the average. To the bad
tricks he took over from his predecessors he has
added some ot his own. He knows the virtues
of a surplus for advertising purposes ; and there
are few things easier than to “ fake ” a surplus.
All that has to be done is to convert enough
assets into cash and use it as revenne, and on the
other side charge enough expenditure to capital
account. The rottenest business in Sydney
could produce a good profit and loss balance in
that way. The trouble in a private concern
would be that the fraud would have to end in a
few years when convertible assets ran out and
new capital refused to come in ; but with a new
country to play with, the fraud may last a cen-
tury. Yet there is an end to even the biggest
estate, and there is a limit to the tax-bearing
capacity of even the richest people.
* * • *
The figures quoted by Treasurer Carruthers
had already been certified to by the Auditor-
General. But the Auditor-General supplied some
particulars to read with the figures. It is true
that the alleged revenue was £12,291,367, and the
alleged expenditure on revenue account was
£11,395,243. But consider these items :
Auction sales of land £82,953
Sales on Newcastle reserve 1,041
Miscellaneous land sales 11,587
Conditional purchase deposits, instalments, &c. 767,323
Other net land-sale proceeds .. .. .. 113,867
Sales of timber (timber licenses) 40,710
Royalties on minerals 46,271
Sale of Government unclassified property .. 11,012
Sale of land, Martin Place and Market-street .. 14,625
Fire Insurance Fund 2,304
This list might be extended to much greater
length ; but even as far as it has gone it totals
£1,091,693. And not one of the items is revenue
proper; each one represents the proceeds of State
assets which have been got rid of. If it is
right to show these moneys as revenue, it would
be perfectly right for some other Government to
sell the railways and tramways and call the pro-
ceeds revenue. A Treasurer might easily get a
“surplus” of £40,000,000, or so, in that way. Of
course it would be a fake, because if the State had
the £40,000,000 it wouldn’t have the railways and
tramways. So a farmer could make a surplus
every year by the simple process of selling, not
his grain only and his wool, but a slice off his
land. In the end the farmer would find, not only
that he had no more land to sell, but that he had
to pay rent for land. His “ revenue ” would then
slump in two ways : the land-sale money would
be at an end, and the proceeds of the sale
of wheat and wool would have to stand a
charge for rent. One can hardly conceive of a
farmer committing this folly. Yet the State of
New South Wales does it. In 1887 it had an
annual revenue of £1,100,423 from rent of leases.
That was revenue proper ; and if the land had not
been sold the rent would probably have been 50
per cent, more to-day—the fair result of railway
extensions—and it would have gone on for ever.
But year by year an average of over £1,000,000
worth of land has been sold, and the proceeds
used as revenue; so that, by 1895, the rents from
leases had fallen to £799,146, and, last year, the
total land revenue proper was only £523,160.
• *
Even after examining only the alleged revenue,
it is certain, then, that there is no real surplus.
And there is still less of a surplus when the ex-
penditure is overhauled. The amount of borrowed
money spent last year was £1,282,995—every man,
woman, and child in the country had £1 os. Id.
added to his little load. J. Carruthers twitters
with something more than his usual energy that
it all went in “ permanent and reproductive
undertakings.” Yet police buildings, hospitals,
bridges, fire brigades’ buildings (£19,828), pulling
fallen trees out of rivers, removing the cat that
wasn’t wanted from harbors, erecting school
buildings, buying a tender for the Sobraon—these,
and a hundred other such things, were charged to
loans. A new machine for the Government Printer
—pay for it out of loans. A new stud bull, “for
the promotion of agricultural college and experi-
mental farms ’’—let Cohen pay the bill. Laying
out a new cemetery at La Perouse—charge it up
to capital. “Re-erection of tombstones”—such
an obviously permanent and reproductive under-
taking is charged to loans. How much of all this
would be charged against revenue by any honest,
sober business undertaking, with nothing to gain
by humbugging itself, it is impossible to say. But
it would certainly be something very considerable;
and every pound thus charged would take the
State still further away from that surplus which
it is claimed to have.
•'• * •
Ostensibly with the object of promoting honesty,
Treasurer Carruthers now introduces a Public
Works Fund. About one-third of the land-sales
money is to go into this fund—the Carruthers
Government proposes to be honest in strict
moderation, and to be satisfied with an 8-carat
halo. Last year’s “surplus” was £896,124; this
year’s is estimated at £598,536. Out of these
combined he proposes to pay £906,131 to the
Public Works Fund, £200,000 to the Closer
Settlement Fund, and £150,000 to the municipal
shires. But The Bulletin refuses to enthuse
even over the prospects of a second “surplus,”
because, on J. Carruthers’ estimates, it is going
to be just the same hollow fraud of a surplus
as its predecessor. Or perhaps hollower.
The whole show was given away when
the Telegraph printed these headings:—“The
Estimates: Increase of £259,301: Heavy Reduc-
tions in Public Works.” “In the Department of
Public Works,” it is explained, “the following
reductions are contemplated :—Roads, from
£390,875 to £310,000; bridges, from £48,875 to
£15,000,” &c. This is the art of manufacturing
surpluses. Last year the Works Department
made part of a road out of revenue. This year it
will make the rest of the road, but the cost will be
charged to the Works Fund. It won’t make a
bit of difference which way it is paid ; only if
it isn’t charged to revenue, there will be so much
more to make a surplus with, and so “ surplus ”
and not revenue will be used to build the road.
It will be, thus far, a purely comic-opera surplus ;
but, unfortunately, it will have this effect: it will
make borrowing easier; for, the vast crowd, both
at home and in Europe, who won’t take the trouble
to understand the fake, will stare open-mouthed
at an annua] surplus. And those at home will be
more willing to go in for another loan-drunk on
the strength of their “prosperity,” and those
abroad will be more ready to accommodate them.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Lands.
The Bill to amend the Lands Department system
in N.S.W., introduced to Parliament last week,
is the Carruthers Cabinet in microcosm. It
pretends to be a very devil of a reform Bill, but
reforms nothing, dares nothing, effects nothing,
except to give timidity and incompetence another
shield. the purpose of the measure is
to put the control of the Lands Department in
the hands of an independent Commission. Really,
it creates a dependent Commission, and leaves the
control just where it was before, in the hands of
the Minister for Lands and his colleagues.
The only difference that the new Lands
Commission will make will be to give future
Lands Ministers a Jorkins. They cau find
apologies for their misdeeds and neglects, and
excuse their incompetence by a reference to “ the
Commission,” and, supposing a Minister to wish
to be corrupt, he will have a valuable institution in
“ the Commission ” to shelter his misdeeds ; for
that body will be supposed to have some power of
check, whilst having none at all actually. If it is
the wish of N.S. Wales Parliament that “the
Lands ” should be put in Commission, on theground
that it is hopeless to expect to find in Parliament
a competent and honest administrator, well and
good. Parliament should know best on that
point. But let the Commission be a real one,
constituted on the lines of the Railway
Commission, and going even further in
the delegation of authority from the politi-
cian to the permanent administrator. A
“fake” Commission, constituted so as to be the
creature of the Minister, is worse than useless.
At the best it makes useless billets ; at the worst
it throws up a rampart to shield dishonesty and
incompetence. But Tuk Bulletin pretends to
no sympathy with this tendency to delegate all the
powers vested in Parliament to non-political
Commissioners. It is absurd to argue that it is
impossible to find honest and capable men among
politicians. If there are none such, let us change
the politicians. The “policy” of every great
State department should be controlled by a
representative of the people, assisted, as re-
gards technical work, by permanent heads of
departments with the proper professional train-
ing. And just now N.S. Wales has no reason
to love the “ independent Commission ” system.
There is in existence a supposedly independent
Public Service Board, which perpetuates most of
the evils of pQlitical patronage, adds to them
some evils of social patronage, and is withal not
amenable to direct Parliamentary criticism. There
is in process of dissolution, by special Act of Par-
liament, a Railway Commission, doomed to
destruction because its head refused to be the
puppet of political pressure and sacrifice the
public interest. These two examples give but
little encouragement to the hope that an indepen-
dent Lands Commission would mend the evils of
the Lands Department, and no hope at all that a
dependent and bogus Commission will be of any
value. The true remedy for the evils which now
mark ministerial control of great State enterprises
is to be found in the abolition of party government.
The elected Minister—chosen not because there is
an Orangeman wanted to counterbalance some
other “color” in the Cabinet, but because Parlia-
ment deliberately picks him out, without reference
to his possible colleagues, as the best man for the
Lands or the Works or the Schools—would pro-
bably be always reasonably competent; and hold-
ing office, not by the uncertain tenure of party
exigencies, but so long as his administration
proved to be good, he would be encouraged to
cultivate his competence. Good work would
mean continuance in office; slackness would in-
volve dismissal. If Parliament goes much further
in the “independent Commission” line it will
face a dilemma, and argue either that (1) State
enterprise is a failure, or (2) Parliamentary govern-
ment is a failure. Then either State Socialism or
Democracy will have to go overboard.
»
The Abortive Butter Commission.
Amidst all the talk about the enormity of lawyer
Croker’s charges for conducting the Yic. Butter
Commission little or nothing is heard about the
futility of the whole performance. An expensive
Commission resulted in a report to the Govern-
ment advising sundry things, which have not been
done, and recommending the future prevention
of abuses that are now happening again as freely
as ever. At the outset of the scandal there was a
decided movement for reform on the part of the
producers. The sinful old middlemen were for
the moment thrown over—most of them. Co-
operative associations, dealing with the output of
the various factories, sought new means of placing
their butter on the London market, and they im-
mediately got higher prices for the exports than
had been returned under the old system. This
better state of butter seems to have continued for
two years, or thereabouts, but within the past
few weeks most of the good that was done has
been quietly undone, and the business of butter
dealing is back again, to a large extent,
in the unclean hands of the men who fattened
on it of yore. Observant moralists remark of
butter commerce that it has a peculiar greasy
tendency to soil the hands of its manipulators.
Apart from the questions of “dealing” and
broking, there is the generally unsuspected fact
that some co-operative associations issue to their
client factories different account-sales from those
they get from the London agent. In certain
known cases the association has deducted 1 per
cent., and then, at the close of the season, a por-
tion of the withheld percentage was given to the
factories, with a flourish of congratulations, as a
gift or bonus resulting from clever management.
A lot of unnecessary salary-receivers are seem-
ingly at the root of the trouble. They are as
barnacles on the industry, and their interests in-
cline them to the boss corrupters of the trade. If
the men who supply the stuff were to keep a
keener eye upon their associations they would get
better results. One of these co-operative concerns
has never ceased to do business through a firm
mentioned in the Commission evidence as being
in league with one of the malodorous Melbourne
agents. Two of the latest associations are content
to give the lion’s share of their business to com-
panies whose Melbourne representatives were
unfavorably criticised by the Commission.
Those local representatives are working the
old game under new names, of course,
but their identity is well known. The
associations’ officials cannot be ignorant as to
the people they are dealing with. Factories
are in the hands of the co-operative associations,
and these, seemingly, are not always to be trusted
to do the right thing in the cleanest possible way.
If it is true that they alter the account-sales they
receive from honest brokers, and substitute their
own with increased charges, they can be fairly
suspected of winking at the cronk tactics of the
artful dodgers who have regained control, more or
less, of the butter-selling business. At any rate,


the artful dodgers seem to be on top, and as
things are going now, the blessed Butter Com-
mission might as well have been an idle dream, in
which case Victoria would have escaped the awful
reality of Croker’s £4OOO bill.
+
South. Africa.
B. R. Wise, who is accepted in London as the
unofficial mouthpiece of Australia, has, in The
Times, July 28, a letter (which evokes a “leader”
from that paper) on the position in S’Africa. The
purpose of Mr. Wise’s argument is shown in his
introductory sentences: —
Like all who have visited South Africa, whatever their
political tinge, I believe the Progressives to be right in
iheir assertion that the issue involved is “British supre-
macy.” And, if this be so, Australia and Canada are
almost as deeply interested as South Africa. Some
Liberals may deny the correctness of this diagnosis ; and
they may be asked to give impartial hearing to the proof.
South Africans feel no fear that full discussion will not
prove the question now really to be—Shall South Africa
be Dutch or British ? But they and all other Imperialists
do fear that a considerable section of the Liberal party
will perceive nothing startling in such an alternative !
For some members of this section have made a fetish of
‘‘majority rule,” whether the majority be Dutch, or even
Kaffir, as some of the more logical have urged. Others,
again, think only of punishing the perpetrators of what
iir. Seddon rightly called “ the heart-breaking” blunder
of Chinese labor. Others, in frank partisanship, would
simply reverse the policy of the preceding Government.
I appeal to all sections* to put aside prejudice and con-
sider the Constitutions as they will affect the Empire.
Therefore, he argues, the Constitutions granted
to the Orange River Colony and the Transvaal
should be so arranged as to secure, whatever the
voting power of the two sections, that the British
—i.e., the Rand and Kimberley mine-owners’
party —should dominate. Since practical poli-
tics are not a matter of putting into working
the principles of abstract ethics, Wise’s arguments
need not be questioned on the ethical basis. It is
true that it is best for the interests of “the •
Empire”—and not merely the Brixton Empire,
but all the Anglo-Saxon communities under the
more or less nominal suzerainty of Britain, in-
cluding Australia—that South Africa should now,
the of destroying the Boer republics having
been consummated, remain British. Further, it
may be admitted that if, by any ingenious system
of ••rigging” the franchise, British suprefnacy in
S’Africa could be assured, and there were no more
honest way of effecting the same end, the fraud
might be, politically speaking, excusable. But
that is hardly the position. The crime of intro-
ducing Chinese labor to the mines has not only
consolidated the Boers in opposition to British
rule, but has driven a great proportion of the
British colonists in S’Africa to the side of the
Boers, for British rule has been identified with
mining capitalists’ rule. If it is arranged that
under the new Constitution the Boer shall have
one vote and the Britisher two votes, the difficulty
is still not solved; so many of those double
votes would be east for pro-Boer candidates.
The British position in S’Africa seems to be,
frankly, hopeless. If the Liberal Government,
on coming into office, had packed off all the
Chinese laborers, and given a chance to Britishers
to fill their places, possibly that revolutionary
step would have made a happy solution of the
problem practicable. But courage was lacking.
Now the position seems to he that if England
tries to hold the conquered territory by military
force, a war will shortly have to be faced in which
the Boers will be aided by a large section of the
British colonists : if representative government is
granted, the dissatisfied British will unite with
the Boers to establish an administration hostile to
the section which caused the war, which caused
the introduction of Chinese labor, and which has
been able to assume to itself a monopoly of the
pro-British sentiment. There is no comfort for
Australia in this position. The separation of
S’Africa promises no good for this country. But
the facts are stubborn. Only a miracle can save
S’Africa for “the Empire.” A little ingenious
Constitution-rigging is not sufficient.

Constitutional Amendment.
It is a fortunate thing that no “Constitution-
alist ” party has arisen in the Australian Parlia-
ment. American progress has been stifled and
the growth of a sordid plutocracy promoted by a
blind worship of “the Constitution.” To the
American “the Constitution ” has become a sort
of fetish, and his worship of it is a shade more
absurd than the British worship of royalty.
It’s the apotheosis of the created over
the creator. Australia, happily, has missed
that folly. We’ve had Foreign-trade par-
ties, anti-Sosh parties, and various other
mental aberrations in politics. But, so far, no
one has suggested that the people of Australia
should spend their time in beatific and satisfied
contemplation of their Constitution, like the
monks of Mount Athos of their stomachs.
Accordingly, the second Federal Parliament
draws to a close with proposals for the amendment
of the Federal Constitution to allow :
1. Greater elasticity in regard to the date for summon-
ing Parliament.
2. The right to the Federal Parliament to impose special
duties of Customs, which shall be exempt from the pro-
visions of the Braddon clause of the Constitution (ordain-
ing that three-quarters of all Customs duties must be
returned to the States) so as to make old-age pensions
possible.
3. The right to the Federal Parliament to assume all
debts owing by the States—not merely, as at present, the
debts contracted prior to Federation.
4. The right to assume trade monopolies, such as the
manufacture of tobacco, inter-State shipping, &c.
All these proposals make for betterment; all
should be endorsed by the electors. In three out
of four the right sought is purely permissive ; the
Federal Parliament would be merely given power
to move in certain directions if the people thought
it desirable. The other amendment removes the
inconvenience which fixes the date of the Federal
elections normally at Christmas time.

Repudiation.
In spite of a weighty protest by Mr. R. J. Black,
N.S. Wales Legislative Council has passed with-
out amendment the Carruthers Bill to dismiss
Railway Commissioner Oliver because he was
brusquely honest. The Bill isn’t exactly called
that, but such is its meaning. Oliver as a railway
administrator was not amenable to “influence.”
His face was set against “jobs”; he persisted in
trying to serve the public interest; he was not
even afraid of offending the Labor party. He
finds his reward in dismissal —an illegal dismissal
which can only safely be effected under special
cover of an Act of Parliament. That Act is a
sad testimony to the state of degradation to which
Parliament has drifted, for it is essentially an act
of repudiation. If Oliver could be legally dis-
missed, no Act of Parliament was necessary ; if
his dismissal is contrary to the law, as Car-
ruthers’ Act admits, not a dozen Acts can wipe
from the State thejstairi of the injustice. The
damage done to the public interest by this solemn
endorsement of an act of repudiation will be two-
fold. It will make the task of getting a new Rail-
way Commissioner difficult, for there will be a
natural hesitancy about undertaking service with
a repudiating Government, since the new official
may be sacked as was the old one. And it will
inspire Civil servants generally with an unwhole-
some fear of independence. They will note the
fate of a great public servant who dared to lift a
voice of protest when actions against the public
interest were proposed, and will learn the lesson
of complaisance. It will probably be a long time
before another N.S.W. public servant dares to
give the interests of the people precedence over
the interests of his immediate bosses.

Australia for the Whites.
Dr. Arthur, M.L.A., hammers away at his sub-
ject
Cold, hard facts have opened our eyes reluctantly enough
to the need for more people. Now the foolish cry is being
raised that they must belong to the Anglo-Saxon race. I
am warned that I will injure the immigration cause unless
I confine my advocacy to the introduction of people from
the United Kingdom. When Australia deliberately aban-
doned its State-aided immigration policy 25 years ago, it
sounded the death-knell of any possibility of a purely
Anglo-Saxon Australia. Had there been 10 million Aus-
tralians now, half of them in the North, we might hardly
contemplate the idea. With things as they are, it is
folly and worse.
The coming Armageddon is going to be between the
whites and the yellows. And Australia will feel the first
brunt of it. If this continent is to be kept at all for the
white races, it will be only by casting our drag-net as
widely as possible, and bringing in all white people who
are not criminal or diseased, and who can make a living
for themselves on the soil. We simply can’t afford to be
too particular. The Hungarian, the Slav, or the Southern
European may not be up to our standard in many
respects; but his children can be converted into good
Australians. Can anyone possibly say the same for the
Mongolian or the Malay ?
Let us remember, too, that the majority of those who
wish to leave Great Britain at present are people for
whom we have little or no use. I got 50 applications
last week from men in England who wished to come
to Australia. They were, with one exception, either
clerks or commercial travellers, or men in warehouses
and shops. Imagine the effect of a few thousand of these
dumped into Melbourne or Sydney—or into the Northern
Territory ! Nor is there an unlimited supply of emi-
grants from the northern countries of Europe. The
exodus from Germany and Scandinavia has been reduced
to a minimum, and what there is of it is going to strength-
en the colonies of those nations already planted in the
various States of North and South America. It would be
almost impossible tp direct it to Australia. Anyhow,
such people would not settle in the North, and that is
where the largest proportion of our future immigrants
must go to. If they will not, we must abandon, once
and for all, our dream of a White Australia.

A “ Stiff for the Crust.”
“ L.E.” expounds : •
There is a class of people who block all progressive
legislation by blind fetichism. They have no ideas —only
precedent, joss, and mummy. When any proposal comes
to mend matters, they pile up antique idols in the lane
to obstruct the traffic. These stale idols serve them for
arguments.
A man was recently hauled up for vagrancy. The police
proved that the man was the associate of tiiieves, and
had done no work for six months. The lawyer bellowed
that there was too much interference with the sacred
liberty of the subject, and that a respectable man
couldn’t be out of work a fortnight without getting a
“stiff for the crust” ( i.e ., a warrant under the
Vagrancy Act). The victim of officialdom smirked, and
referred to the beak as their “ wash-ups,” so the charge
was dismissed. A week later this unfortunate was con-
victed for stealing coppers from untenanted houses,
having collected more than 100 in one busy week. He
was a noted thief, known to be “ doing jobs ” ; but his
long record was not read out till the case was over. Then
the Dogberries wondered.
The public maintains the foolish notion that anybody
has a chance of being run in if he is out of work and
down on his luck. Not once in ten years is a decent man,
however unlucky, brought up under “ the crust.” It is
simply a public safeguard against thieves. A “ John ”
(and the “ Johns” are a much straighter and more com-
petent body than most people will believe) knows every
man and woman on his beat, their habits and associates,
and the whole sad story of their lives. Under our absurd
system of punishments, Melbourne is swarming with
thieves who have no inducement to try another business,
and, as soon as they are out of gaol, commence operations
and so pile up double-figure convictions with consistency.
A “ John ” knows that they are doing “jobs”—stealing
coppers, walking the balconies, doing assault and battery,
larceny from the person, &c. —and he “vags” them in
the interests of public safety. Till indeterminate sen-
tences come into force, every city will be infested with
“ guns ” and rooks, spielers and garrotters, and all manner
of social anarchists. It’s dangerous at night to go down
many lanes and streets even now ; only for “ the crust ”
it would be impossible.
But for fetich interference Melbourne or any other
large eity could be fumigated in a few months. Moral
philosophy is no use ; wasters should be encouraged, by
Draconian legislation, to find out that thieving and loaf-
ing is a fool’s game. Opium dens, thieves’ and coiners’
dens, sly grog shops, tote-shops, two-up schools, places
for receiving stolen property, &c.—only for the lunatic
notion that an Englishman’s house is a castle, they could
all be wiped out right away. Anything that rots the
fibre of a nation should be grubbed out. Aus-
tralia can’t afford this interesting decadence. It needs a
strong Spartan infusion to counteract the sluggishness of
the average sensual man. But this average citizen would
prefer to see the youth corrupted, patriotism stifled, and
a feeble population dragged up rather than allow an
inspector to look through his sacred edifice (which would
sometimes be a good thing, even in the interests of
common sanitation). Why should a good citizen shiver
if an inspector or policeman tramps through his house ?
He can’t have any State secrets to hide, or a skeleton in
the chimney, or a bone in the dust-bin. The officer
wouldn’t invade him without some cause, he wouldn’t
drop in casually to see the baby or the pictures on the
walls. Yet, so lazy and selfish is the “good citizen ” that he
would see the whole world go to pot before he would be
disturbed at his painful study of whist or the new soldier
song or the Windsor Magazine.
Last night, in company with men who know, I watched
a certain house. From 1 till 3 a.m. the door would
suddenly open and folk pass to and fro. They were
mostb' notorious thieves. That place was a two-up
school, a sly-grog shop, a place for receiving stolen
property, or the lot. Now, if that crib might have been
assaulted right away something interesting would have
been discovered. But it was a castle, and inviolate, for
the liberty of the criminal must be respected.
In the dark ages liberty was priceless, for it had to be
wrenched in small sections from the aristocracy. But, in
a democracy where all are supposed to have equal rights,
it has become a synonym for laziness, or selfishness, or
jobbery, or criminal laissez-faire. Liberty to commit
nuisances, commercial or social, should be violently
suppressed ; and we won’t have a clean, forceful, and
economically sound State until ancient idols are shattered,
till the liberty of the lazy citizen is fully disrespected,
till an Englishman’s house is not his castle—until the
long feelers of Government interference shake society
out of its long sloth and chronic superstition.

Concerning Silver.
It is time that the Australian Government came
to some agreement with the British authorities
regarding the coining of silver in and for Australia.
Silver is coined at a small cost; the value of
the metal and the cost of coining together amount
to only about one-half the face value of the coins,
so that on every £1 of silver issued the coiner
receives from the public a loan of 10s. without
interest. The loan practically amounts to a
gift, and may be regarded as profit. Accord-
ing to Treasurer Forrest, there is intro-
duced into Australia annually about £77,000
(face value) of silver coin, on which the pro-
fit made by the English Mint (which issues
all Australia’s silver coin, barring that which is
said to be illicitly made in China) is about £38,000
a year. Circulating or held in reserve in Australia
is silver with a face value estimated variously at
from £1,200,000 to £2,000,000, half of which value
has already gone as profit to the English Mints.
Various proposals have been made from time to
time, among them being :
(1) That Britain should continue to coin Australia’s
silver, charging 3 per cent, or so for the work, and re-
turning the balance of profit to the Australian Govern-
ment. This rule obtains in Canada.
(2) That Australia should retain the present silver
coinage in circulation, but in future coin its own silver,
and draw therefrom the full profit of £38,000 a year.
(3) That Australia should coin all its own future silver
(with £38,000 a year profit), and, in addition, should re-
place the existing British coinage by Australian coinage,
the existing British coinage to be gradually withdrawn
at face value, or thereabouts.
The third scheme is the best. Britain, it is said,
is willing to withdraw at the rate of £lOO,OOO a
year, at face value, the only deduction being an
allowance for worn coin. Silver coin is always
withdrawn from circulation when it has lost 11
per cent, of its weight (equal to about 5 per
cent, of its real value), so that in the replacing
of £lOO,OOO, face value, of British coinage the
Commonwealth would reap a profit of about
45 per cent. —equal to £45,000 a year. And this
added profit—or loan without interest —would
go on for 12 years at the lowest estimate, and for
20 years at the highest. During this period of
12 or 20 years the profit of £38,000 a year on new
coinage would also be made, and it would continue
for ever. There is no apparent reason why this
substantial benefit to the Commonwealth finances
should not begin at once. Treasurer Forrest says
that the delay has arisen because of the desire
to introduce decimal coinage concurrently with
the coinage of Australian silver. It seems bad
business to keep losing the profit for the sake of
that idea. Even supposing that, after beginning
the coinage of half-crowns, florins, shillings, six-
pences and threepences, Australia decided to insti-
tute decimal coinage, the on--. calling in and re-
coining its silver would n./t be more than 3
per cent, or so of the face value; in fact,
the profits on one year’s coinage would suffice to
pay the expense of melting down and re-coining
the whole issue of 16 years. Treasurer Forrest
has decided to delay the whole question until
after the next Imperial conference in 1907—it is
wonderful what a capacity.for postponing things
the Australian Government has. Apparently
that much loss of time is now inevitable, but after
the Imperial conference no further sacrifice should
be made. Incidentally, it may be remembered
that the coinage of silver will provide some
further employment; in Australia; and that in
view of the fact that Australian Mints are worked
with greater efficiency and at less cost than those
in England, the margin of profit is likely to be
higher here than it ever has been in Bull’s
country.

A Lesson in Fiscalism.
“ Austra ” (London) has learned a lesson :
I left Australia last year, a strict Laborite, fully con-
vinced that the one thing necessary for tlie welfare of the
country was the Labor programme, and quite satisfied to
sale with the discredited Reid and sink the fiscal issue.
A v ary few months in the Cold Country has changed all
t.vu.. I understand now the tale, learnt long ago, of the
house built on sand, and what happened it when the
weather changed and its foundations were called onto
do a share of supporting. I had not been in this country
one and a half days before the first instance of what
Freetrade had done was brought vividly to notice.
Landing at Plymouth, I went on next day to see
some friends near Falmouth, in the south of Cornwall.
Penryn, near Falmouth, was, a couple of years ago,
turning out thousands of tons of granite per year, which
was used for building docks, also for breakwaters and the
like, and employed directly from 1300 to .1600 men.
Now that granite quarries have been opened in
Norway, where wages are a deal lower, there are barely
400 employed at Penryn, and they are working for about
two-thirds of the rate they were previously paid. The
result all round is pitiful. Many of the best workmen
have gone to America, others have gone to Norway to
accept the lower wages, but apparently constant work,
while others who remain are half their time idle, and,
when working, if skilled dressers, have to take from 20s.
to 235. per week instead of the old 30s. Unskilled
workers have to take from 14s. per week instead of about
4s. per day. The introduction of the cheaper material
does good to no one except the contractors. Not even
does the Navy Department benefit a penny, or the owners
of other works. All prices given by contractors are
based on the price of Cornish granite. Then they
import Norwegian at a profit of 2d. or 3d. per ton.
Just to put that a little more plainly, a duty of 3d. per
ton would not raise the cost of any of the works a penny,
it would still leave the contractors a fair margin of profit,
and it would keep in the country and give work at decent
pay to hundreds of workers who are being absolutely
hunted out. The reason that the English quarries are work-
ing at all is that the stone from there “ works up ” and
polishes a deal better than any imported, and is used for
pillars in big buildings, bridges, gateways, for grave-
stones, &c. The elections were approaching at the time
of my sojourn in those parts, and both candidates were
bombarded with questions re the granite industry. The
Conservative (Chambetdainite) was a tall, haw-haw youth
who was just starting out on a “ career.” He had no idea
of what was what, but fluked the true solution. He said :
“ Put a duty on foreign granite, and they will have to use
yours.” He was howled down, and told that he wanted
to give them a little loaf instead of a big one. The
Liberal, a London importer, talked about the big pasty
(pasties, instead of loaves, were used in Cornwall to
adorn the Liberal placards), the Chinese in S’Afriea, and
promised to see the Admiralty', or whomsoever it con-
cerned, and get them to stipulate for English granite.
He was cheered. Also he was elected.
