The Threat of Asia.
There are two classes of critics standing in the
way of an effort to organise a national system of
defence for Australia. One, judging Australian
manhood by its own want of manhood, condemns
any national defence project as hopeless; the
Commonwealth’s reliance must be placed in the
British navy. If that fails, then Australia must
lie prostrate, and be enslaved without a struggle
to China or Japan or some European power. The
other class considers that there is plenty of time,
and that money can be saved now by waiting a
while. It is difficult to state which lie
is the more noxious. Probably the first, for its
implications are fatal to any sense of national
respect. And there is absolutely no valid reason
for any pessimistic despair as to the capacity of
Australia for self-defence. Given ample local
factories for the manufacture of arms and ammu-
nition and the training of all the manhood to the
use of arms ; given, also, some sort of a local navy
to at least hinder an enemy at sea, and Australia
could face any imaginable foe. It would need to
fear the enmity of no European power; it could
face unmoved even the prospect of Asiatic
malignity resolving itself into a war of
invasion. For it is absurd to act on
the presumption that an unlimited force
could be launched against Australia. No existing
Power could hope to put more than 50,000 fighting
men on the Eastern Coast, at present the heart of
Australia : perhaps 100,000 fighters could be put
into the line of fire by an enemy if the Northern
Territory were the first objective, but an army
of invasion in the N.T. wouldn’t so much matter ;
it could be dealt with at leisure. Now, if a
population of 4,500,000 can’t of its manhood pro-
vide a force capable of mobilising at a given
point 50,000 or 100,000 fighters, what sort
of a poor breed is the Australian ? The
pessimist about the. possibilities of Australian
military organisation, judging by his views must
be pale-live'-**?! and currish. The wait-a-bit man,
on the other hand, may be brave enough, but he
is lazy and ignorant, and is a disaster well worthy
of the company of the calamity before mentioned.
The organisation of a citizen army cannot be
effected in a day, or a month, or a year. It’s a
matter of slow growth. The first essential step,
of course, is the establishment of factories for war
material. That could and should be done at once,
and would in itself give some measure of security,
for even an untrained man can fight some if he
has a gun. The next step Would cover a long
series of years. Allowing for the necessary teach-
ing of officers, keeping in view the impossibility
of training a vast mass of men until sufficient
instructors have been secured, it would take 10
years, beginning now, to organise a reasonably-
sized citizen army; 25 years to organise an army
at its full possible strength. There really is not a
day to wait.

The Monkey’s Paw Reaches Out.
A man who lias spent years wandering up and
down from the Philippines to Japan, writes from
Hongkong of the recent attempt of the Jap to buy
the IJ.S. out of the Philippines:— >
The commercial and general indebtedness of Japan is
now too awful to contemplate. The national debt touches
41250,000,000, and carries 5 per cent. Yet, if Japan had
succeeded in acquiring the Philippines, its stock (at the
present rate of interest) should have been worth 25 per
cent, premium. But it was a repetition of the story of
the Jlnssian peace conditions —the cunning of the yellow
race went down before the white man’s intellect. If
Japan had worked diplomatically, and had been free from
that odor of suspicion which now surrounds its almost
every transaction, and if its diplomats could have con-
vinced Roosevelt that treaty obligations would be
observed, and the rights of the Filipino respected, then the
soul-longing desire to get the Philippines might have
eventuated. But the Yankee smelt slavery ; he had to
listen to the cry that went up even from the inland tribes
when the true object of the Japanese was known ; and
the scheme failed.
This is what it meant to Japan. Within ten years ten
millions of her people would have been permanently
settled in the Philippine Islands. The mere question of
the forty-odd million sterling purchase money was
nothing. Capitalists would have rushed any heavy loan
for such a purpose. There is not any agricultural wealth
in Japan. The Philippines would not only have raised
sufficient rice and native food for local requirements, but
also would have fed Japan. The vast unexploited
fisheries would have been developed and brought in
immense wealth. The great forests would have been
utilised, and every port turned into a ship-building yard.
The annual return of £5,000,000 for hemp would have
been doubled ; and in lieu of being exported in the raw,
factories would have been erected. Instead of the pre-
sent £500,000 export of raw sugar per annum, within the
ten years there would have been a five-million total of
the refined product. The possibilities of the tobacco
and cigar trade would have been illimitable. Total-
ised, a cultivatable area equivalent to four times
the size of Japan proper would have been acquired,
with a soil and a climate eminently suitable for
Japanese immigration and development. The im-
mensity of the proposition, with the full knowledge
that the Japanese statesmen possess as to what tile
acquisition of the Philippines means to them, emphasises
the dread that a strike out will he made at the first
opportune moment. It is worth almost any struggle.
A valuable country, undeveloped mineral resources,
raw cotton and other material for its manufacturers,
food for its people adjacent to the homeland, certain—
yes, absolutely certain-wealth. The trade of the East
would have been Japan’s own, even the great Manchester
market of India must have yielded, and think of it!
thousands of official billets for the crowd now worrying
the Government ; the sous-officer raised from fid. to 2s. a
day, and without the cold fear of going back to office or
mill ! Political, naval and military domination east of
Suez, and with the cash, its own cash, to bacK its forces !,
Ten enormous industries as Government monopolies to
fill the Treasury !
The danger is over for the moment. But Australia
may know now for certain which way Japan’s eyes turn.
It may know for certain that Japan is not content with
its own country, even with Korea thrqwn in. It turns
its face south—which way power lies. Having got that
knowledge, Australia should know what to do. If it
doesn’t, God help it!
The N.S. Wales alderman has just as little idea
of getting his town hall out of pawn as J. Cah-
ruthers has of getting his State out; and almost
the greatest sinners of all are the suburban mayors
and aldermen, who shouldn’t exist at'all, and who
shouldn’t be possessed of any town halls to pawn.
These suburbs owe £704,819 besides overdrafts,
and, ruling out Marrickville and Ryde, all that
the whole 38 wasters put up last year in the way
of sinking fund was £397. Ryde and Marrickville
put up £l4OO between them. This precious crowd,
nevertheless, paid £34,041 in interest. Here and
there a suburban council paid off an odd hundred
or two, mostly because it was squeezed into it, or
because a bailiff arrived and threatened to stay, as
he is staying at Camperdown ; but, as a rule, when
the councils repaid £l, they borrowed £2 I Os.-, so
the situation got worse instead of better. The
Sydney suburban mayor and alderman’s idea of
honest and decent finance can best be reckoned
by the fact that, though -39 of the suburban
municipalities are in pawn (the 40th is in pawn,
but only on an overdraft), no more than four of
the whole shiftless, drifting crowd have sinking
funds for the systematic repayment of their debts.
The group of Melbourne butter agents which
lined up before the Minister for Lands, lately, to
complain of dairy-expert Crowe, formed an
object-lesson in extra-special, cool-chilled cheek.
Crowe, as part of his duty, issued recently a
forcibly-worded warning to the dairymen regard-
ing the dangers attached to the growing practice
of sending their cream to Melbourne. As the
result of about 17 years’ experience, Crowe has
come to the common-sense conclusion that the
proper place to make butter is in the country dis-
tricts, where the cream can he obtained fresh,
and treated properly, since cream is one of the
most easily contaminated of all food substances.
In order to break down the weaker factories, the
Melbourne agents pay a high competitive price for
cream. Then, when the factory closes its dooijs, the
price of cream falls, and the Melbourne agent reaps
his harvest. As a matter of fact, during the past
five years, several factories in Gippsland and the
north-east of Victoria have gone to the wall in this
manner. But the hard-faced agents complained
to the Minister that Crowe’s advice was out-
rageous ; that it was an unwarrantable inter-
ference with their business; and that, any-
how, it had nothing to do with Crowe. Minister
Swinburne snubbed the brass-fronted deputation,
and Dr. Cherry, head of the Department of
Agriculture, smiled sweetly on the butter-folk,
and said that he also had every confidence in
Crowe. So, after a further snort, the deputation
waddled down the Treasury steps, and went off
gloomily to its Flinders-street “ butter-factories,”
where, in a month or two, when the hot weather
sets in, the making of first-grade butter will be a
matter of extra-special difficulty.

Henry 11., by the common law of England, a dead
man’s goods were divided into three equalparts,
of which • one went to his heirs or lineal descend-
ants, another to his wife, and the third was at his
own disposal. If he died without a wife, he might

A SCIENTIFIC “PICK-ME-UP.” —No
better tonic and restorative for the victims of
social festivities exists than a morning draught of
Hunvadi Janos. Sure, swift, searching, yet safe,
and in every respect salutary. The best and
safest natural aperient,*
dispose of one-half, and the other half went to his
children ; if he had no children, he might dispose
of one-half, and the other went to his wife. This
continued to be the oommon law until the reign
of Charles I. After that period the law was
gradually altered until the present day, when, in
England, a man has the absolute disposal of the
whole of his property. The law of Scotland at
the present day is somewhat similar to the old
English common law. The widow of a Scotch-
man is entitled to one-third or one-half of his per-
sonality, according as there are, or are not, children
of the husband living, also to a life interest of one-
third in the deceased’s real estate.
*
Fiji is smiling a sickly smile at the fish yarn that
the more discolored labor a country gets in to do
its work, the more work there will be left for
whites to do. It listened to that yarn many
years ago, got in coolies by the thousand, and it
has been waiting for the white population to
arrive ever since. But instead of coming in, the
whites have steadily moved out; and except the
sugar business, out of which coolies get the work
and the foreign co. gets the profits, every industry
on the islands is sicker than it was 25 years
ago. Fibre, cotton, coffee, maize, tobacco,
limejuiee, peanuts, candlenuts, arrowroot—the
export of all these chief products of the
eighties has fallen away. Leaving sugar
out, the exports have shrivelled up from
£205,608 in 1880 to £162,768 in 1905. And in a.
recent issue the Fiji Times wailed mournfully*
that, not only was it getting no white population,
in spite of all its attractions, but “ the stamina of
the Europeans as a whole has been on the wane
since the islands came under British rule 30 or
more years ago.” In short, the degeneration that
always happens when the white hangs round and
has a horde of discolored slaves to do the work is
in violent progress.

From Melbourne:—
One of the most popular forms of amusement in Vic-
toria these days is the launching of charges of perjury.
The thing is done in the airiest and most irresponsible
manner, and honest citizens are hauled into court,
branded with an ineffaceable stigma, and left to pay big
defence costs, at the instigation of any worthless fellow
who happens to feel himself aggrieved. Victoria’s perjury
law can be set in motion by anyone at all who is willing
to lay an information. It has got to such a point that, a
while ago, an angry female prosecuted a girl for calling
out to her in the street, and, having lost the case—appa-
rently it was another girl who called out—had a second
shot at the child by having her arrested on a charge
of perjury for swearing that she didn’t call out.
Perjury charges are trumped up to such an extent that
every week at least one case, whose only foundation is in
the malice of the prosecution, comes before the courts.
The result of allowing a foolish weapon of this sort to lie
around for every one to use is that reputable witnesses
avoid Victorian courts by every known excuse. In im-
portant cases, where crooked men are to be dealt with,
the respectable persons, who know all about the matter,
are generally smitten with extraordinary loss of memory.
They know that a trial for perjury will follow if they give
evidence against an unscrupulous defendant, that being
the regular means now for terrifying witnesses. Some-
times, indeed, witnesses are threatened in advance with
arrest for perjury if they give evidence, and justice
miscarries badly through this intimidation. A remedy
lies close at hand. In a murder case, or any other case
of major crime, the police undertake the prosecution.
They get up the brief, and it is submitted to the Crown-
Prosecutor and the Attorney-General before action is
taken. These officers decide whether the evidence war-
rants a prosecution, and the liability of innocent men to
be accused of heinous crimes is reduced to a minimum.
Such a restriction of perjury charges is urgently
needed.
■
Recently eleven Tweed River (N.S.W.) Indian
sugar growers protested by petition to the House
of Representatives against being refused the
bonus granted on cane produced by white
growers
It is not in accordance with the traditions and spirit
of the British Government, which recognises that all
subjects are entitled to equal opportunities ; and that
its laws and protection are not made for the advance-
ment of certain classes to the injury or disadvantage of
others.
Well, the Indian, in his alleged own country, is
denied political rights by the Government,
“ which recognises,” &c. ; all the high posts, civil
and military, in India are filled, not by Indians,
but by men of the race whose Government “recog-
nises,” &c. —in short, the Indian, in his alleged
own country, is treated as an inferior and con-
quered person by the Government, “ which
recognises,” &c. When Britain treats the
Indian British subject in India as the equal of
the white British subject in that country, it will
be time enough for the Indian British subject in
Australia to lead out the dreary old platitude
about the “ tradition and spirit,” &c., as a reason
why he should be treated as the equal of the
white British subject in this country.
.
“J. W. Li Langerman, representing the Robinson
group of mines on the Rand, advocates the repa-
triation of the Chinese coolies,” a cable says. So
soon ! It has been a great disillusionment. J.
Bull knew nothing about the Chinaman, very
rarely having met him, and the Berlin stock-
brokers knew, if possible, less. But they had a
vague idea that he was a cheap and reliable slave ;
and so they imported him to prop up the over-
boomed market and the dividends. And he has
done neither. Instead of the market brightening
under the influence of the “cheap and reliable”
labor which they plunged a nation into a war to
secure, it has sagged, sagged, sagged. A few
months ago Albu, one of the Randlords, put the
total slump in 110 Rand stocks at £111,500,000
—that was from the beginning of last year. Since
then, and taking in all the cos., the total is com-
puted at no less than £400,000,000. Which is a
figure large enough to make Britain think. Before
this disastrous experiment, it of course knew all
about the Chow, just as it knows all about every-
thing. Now, with its quite natural ignorance of
the subject proved up to the hilt, it may be dis-
posed to let Australia do its own thinking on the
question of Asiatic immigration. That is a
point that Australia must push in—the peril
of British interference in regard to the im-
migration of a people about whom Britain clearly
knows nothing, and about whom Australia, from
its very situation and every-day experience, must
know a great deal.

AniENiBUM Hall Best and Baker’s Pictures.
Sydney has long wished in vain for a
season of grand opera. Since Melba's visit there
has been no chance of hearing a dramatic soprano.
Consequently, the keenest interest is being taken
in the forthcoming operatic season of Madame
Arral, who has dropped most unexpectedly on
this continent with a haughty European reputa-
tion and a repertoire including all the most
famous operas. Friday (to-morrow) is to be the
date of the first recital in the Town Hall. A
stage has been specially erected so as to
allow of scenic and costume adornment. A
grand opera orchestra of 40 instrumentalists,
headed by Rivers Allpress, has been en-
gaged, and scenes from several Operas will
be sung by Madame Arral. It won’t be grand
opera exactly, but the “liver wing” from half a
dozen grand operas.
* *-
In a Bland Holt melodrama there is always
a woman who is a good hater. She hates —usually
the virtuous flaccid heroine—with all her soul and
brisket. The heroine’s love for the hero can’t
compare in strength with the hate of the hater at
her hatefullest. As a matter of accuracy, there
isn’t one woman in ten millions who can hate with
sufficient strength or continuity to pay out the
other cat when Opportunity offers. Often Oppor-
tunity creeps into the palm of her hand, so to speak,
but shedoesn’t fold herclaws around it. She just
thinks, while she’s doing her hair some morning :
“O, is that you ? Get out! I don’t
want you now. While I’ve waited
for you She lent tne the pattern
of a blouse—besides, He wasn’t
all I thought him to be. And,
anyhow, strong feeling of
any kind is very bad for
the complexion and ex-
pression.” With these
few notes on the harp
of life this inky way-
farer comes out of the
Theatre Royal, after seeing
“A Woman’s Hate,” convinced that
Bland Holt has got in his revival the one woman
in the ten millions—the Hater-Woman. But even
she, in the fourth act, relents and repents, and,
as imagination is more or less elastic, one may
imagine the lady abasing her great length (Harrie
Ireland is the hater) and her red hair in the dust
the while she asks Providence to put back the
hand of Time and give her yesterday.
* * * *
Best scene in “ A Woman’s Hate ” is the
last act —the trial at the Old Bailey. Heroine
(Frances Ross) is on trial for her life, for she is
accused of shooting and killing one of two ras-
cally solicitors. Both men would be better under
ground, manuring the turnips; no one denies
that; but, just to uphold the Decalogue, the
heroine, looking like a broken lily, and with the
limelight right on her white face and black hair,
is stood up in the unpicturesque old dock opposite
a dear old Judge and four associates, and
in profile to twelve jurymen who look good
judges on the points of a bull pup. At the elbow
of the Judge and of his four partners lies a fine
posy of flowers, a phase that bothered the
gallery of the Royal to the verge of brain fag.
“ They never have them at the Water,” com-
mented one lover of detail. The dramatic
situation of this act lies in the uprising of the
barrister hero (Walter Baker) as counsel, to
prove the innocence of his own wife. Judge
and Bar declare the procedure “ most
unusual,” but as there’s no law against
it Walter Baker goes ahead and with the aid of a
railway guard and Bland Holt he establishes his
case. Very like a lawyer, the real murderer
(the partner of the murdered solicitor) left full
proofs of his guilt in his chambers while he came
down to the Old Bailey to bear false witness
against the heroine. All the innocent parties
go off with their arms around one another,
all the wicked ones go off with the “darbies”
around their wrists or are left with drooping
heads and bad consciences to work out
their salvation. A word of loud-spoken praise is
due to Miss Florrie Kelly, who spoke well in the
witness-box and wept realistically over her Mam-
ma, who was so very nearly being brought in
guilty by the naughty court.
SYDNEY ART SOCIETY SHOW.—SOME
IMPRESSIONS.
“ It’s rude to scratch, I know, but still”
By J. S. Swotkins.
The Kelly Gang; or. Our Baby is a Naughty Boy.
By Normal Barter.
A Study in Propriety; or, Art Without Legs.
By Ned Leased.
Organist Lemare’s gentle wrestling matches
with the mammoth organ at Sydney Town Hall
will close this week, leaving the organist still
boss. Sometimes after the ride of Valkyries he
shows signs of wanting a sponging, but generally
he doesn’t turn a hair. His audiences have been
so satisfactory that he will, for half the number
of recitals, take away as much money as he made
by the eighteen recitals of his last visit, which
proves that Sydney is growing more cultured, or
has more money.
* * *- *
“ The Shaughraun,” ever-green Irish
drama, just now finds capable interpretation from
the Charles Holloway Co., at a Sydney Criterion
revival. Humor and pathos are alike well pour-
trayed, and the mounting is generally good. In
the name part, John P. O’Neill suffers under some
physical disabilities : his figure approaches that
of G. H. Reid (approaches, not reaches); and
when the noble Oirish gintleman has to pic-
ture this faithful but fat crathur “bounding
over the crags like a goat,” the audi-
ence smiles, and smiles again when the
extra-plump Shaughraun does arrive with some-
thing of a goatish bound. Apart from carrying
too much weight, O’Neill makes a good Shaugh-
raun, and stands comparison with the many
famous comedians who have interpreted the part
in Sydney. As Clare Ffolliott, Miss Beatrice
Holloway plays with much sweetness and some
power of passion. But vagabond boy imper-
sonations tend to give her voice a squeaky
note. The young lady has many charming
qualifications for “heroine” work; probably
it would promise her a better career to
drop the mournful child with the hacking cough
business, round out her voice, and challenge
criticism as a “lead” in drama. Miss Mabel
Russell and Miss Ethel Appleton play their girl
parts brightly, and John Hesford and Robert
Inman are a satisfactory pair of heroes —one Irish
and the other English. “The Shaughraun” will
only be played during this week.
-*■ *- #- *
Miss Annie Mayor undertakes a vast task
in her “ Drama in Camera ” entertainment, pre-
sented to the Sydney public, on Saturday night, at
the Palace. The lady selects scenes from various
plays, connects them with a thin thread of a
lecture, and, single-handed, amuses her audiences.
She has some good qualifications for the
enterprise—a singularly sweet and flexible
voice, an appearance which is attractive,
though it doesn’t get the best of chances
in the way of “make-up,” and a fine
dramatic talent. But she adds to her difficulties
by a poor, suburban-drawing-room sort of stage
setting. The garish furniture hinders where an
absolute simplicity would‘help effects which must
depend for success upon quiet and sympathetic
attention. Her best results were got in scenes
from Shakspeare. Miss Mayor’s Sydney season
cannot stretch beyond this week.
** * *
J. C. Williamson’s plans for the Reper-
toire Co. have been altered. The idea was, in
the first place, to keep it to Gilbert and Sullivan ;
but, as this co. frivols in places where Lauri and
the rest of the Comic Opera Co. are never seen,
J.C.W. has found no reason why it should not
add “Veronique,” and other good things of that
class, to its little list. So “ Veronique ”isto be
produced for the first time by this co. at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night.
Meantime, “ Utopia, Ltd.,” continues to deserve
the things that were said about it last week;
and the voice of James Hughes, who plays the little
part of Corcoran, deserves a word or two of praise
that haven’t been said here before. This day
week (Thursday, 20th) Howard Vernon is to take
a benefit; and as the chances are that Australia
wouldn’t have had this revival of Gill>ert and
Sullivan if Vernon had not been on hand to
suggest it, the benefit should bulge with goodwill
and coin.
** * *
There have been comings and goings at
the Tivoli, with the net result that there isn’t as
much variety in the programme as there has been
lately. Nellie Kilburn, back after two years, is
the good “ serio-comic ’’ that she used to be ; and
two new-comers, Bedford and Chester, both
character comedians, make very similar remarks
regarding an English liard-up. Australia’s
familiar Tom Dawson being also in the bill, there
are three of them ; and Thomas can hold up his
end of the triangle as well as the rest.
** * *
There was a record house at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.), last Saturday, for the matinee perform-
ance of “La Poupee,” by children only, of which
it may be said that if the same show had been
presented to an ordinary any-night audience,
caring nothing for the charitable object nor at all
concerned about the success of Miss Brenan’s
pupils, the applause would have been not less
hearty. The youngsters and the stage manage-
ment earned much praise. Their work amounted
to a triumph of teachable cleverness and pains-
taking tuition. To the writer such a show seems
a powerful incentive to an improved birth-rate,
inasmuch as it reveals Australian kiddies in the
forefront of the world’s rising generation. Given
a fair start they develop themselves wonderfully.
At a time when the popular cry is for national sup-
port of local industries this performance, in aid of
a foundling hospital, emphasises the fact that
every native baby, being duly arrived, deserves
all possible encouragement, no matter how it may
have come about. Many people urge against
these juvenile opera companies the objection that
they plant a stage-fever germ in the infant mind.
Contemplating the kiddies in their artistic pre-
cocity, all coached-up and dolled-up, one feels
doubtful as to whether the kingdom of Heaven is
like unto a little mummer. Nevertheless, “‘La
Poupee ’ in miniature ” had a lot more of sweet
innocence than, say, an A.N.A. competition in
singing, or elocution.
The performance of a seven -year-old mite
(Nellie McCarthy) in the name-part was even
more pretty than amazing. She gave full-grown
expression to her words, her dumb pantomime
was humorous, and her unmelodious attempts to
sing couldn’t have been more tasteful. The lad
in the tenor part acted with modest assurance,
and sang like a skilled chorister, and among
a dozen other boy and girl principals there was
not one “duffer.” Two striking instances of
natural qualification for the stage were a saucy
seven-year-old girl who played (with spirit) the
doll-maker’s apprentice, and a Miss Lilian
Pritchard, said to be only 14, who spoke a few
lines in a noble voice—a voice of the rare kind
that gives dignity to a commonplace remark and
makes “How d’ye do” sound like a bishop’s
benediction. Miss Brenan being the leading
spirit behind the scenes, a number of extra
dancing items were introduced, all of them grace-
ful, or amusing. The “star” damsel was the
blonde and beauteous Miss Addie Hine, who had
some years of early training from Mrs. Greenland
is no longer quite a child. Miss Hine’s little
sister Ivy also tripped around charmingly, and
one smali boy among the array of girl dancers was
the promising heir-apparent of Hugh Ward.
-X- * *
“La Mascotfce” is to be revived at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday. The latest previous pro-
duction (at the Princess’s) dates back to 12 years
ago, so the attractions of Audran’s opera will come
new to one half of the city, and nearly as good as
new to the other. All the same, the run of this re-
vival is limited to 12 nights, and whilst the musi-
cal street urchin is learning to whistle “ Glou-
Glou,” like father before him, the story of Farmer
Rocco and his invaluable Bettina will be with-
drawn from circulation. In the meantime, a
numerous body of citizens, mostly football bar-
rackers, have become anxious to know why they
usually apply the term “Mascotte” to a three-
penny bit with a hole in it.
** * *
At the Royal, a healthy demand for bush-
drama accounts for what the Andersonian advt.
calls a “ Thunderbolt Furore.” Considered aparc
from its bush and bushiness, its Cobb’s coach and
quaint, ruminative native bear, “Thunderbolt”
is an exceptionally silly subject for a furore, hut
its scenery and live stock, and distant family
connection with “Robbery Under Arms,”
give the 4-act shocker a claim to patron-
age. The hero is a reprehensible idiot, but
he partly excuses himself by getting killed like
a dawg, leaving his widow out in the wet.
During his lifetime he took a lot of credit for not
having the Blood Guilt on his Soul, and although
he finally tried his best to stain his immortal spirit
with the gore of a policeman, he died innocent of
the crime. “ Thunderbolt ” will diffuse a strong
smell of gunpowder for one more week, and then
John F. Sheridan and Co. are to introduce “The
Earl and the Girl ” to Melbourne notice.
** # *
“ The Fatal Wedding ” will march to the
music of the Tin Can Band for yet another week
at the Princess’s. Then the Charles Holloway
Co. will look in, under the direction of Geach, for
the purpose of restoring “ Two Little Vagabonds”
to the stage where they found a pretty warm wel-
come in the century that is no more.
* * * -*■
The Opera House bill was somewhat
strengthened on Saturday by the arrival of Harry
Tate’s company of eccentric comedy sketchers.
The full force of their alleged eccentricity was
not irresistibly droll at the first attempt, but per-
haps the co. will improve on acquaintance. Little
Cliff has changed his old songs for new ones of
inferior quality, whereas Brinn’s “turn” is
strengthened by the introduction of another gun,
which appears to rest upon a platform on his
weary head, whilst another fellow’ sits beside and
discharges loud noises from it. The rest of the
programme consists largely of Bluett and Cassie
Walmer, the light-brown lady who sings three
songs in a taking manner and calls them imper-
sonations.

A fifth and final Dolores recital was pro-
mised for yesterday (Wednesday) afternoon at
the Town Hall, and if nothing happened to break
the promise, Melbourne has said au revoir to the
Dolores voice and smile, in the blessed hope of a
return visit. Three nights last week the versatile
songstress beamed upon a vast congregation, her
programme in each case being more effective than
the first one. An impending musical event
is a “grand opera matinee” at Princess’s.
Marshall Hall’s Conservatorium, with himself as
conductor, will supply the performance; it will
be a send-off to Mr. Hal Cohen, who is to take
sundry parts therein. Cohen is a tall young man
with a possible future. Thus far he has turned
his sweet, foggy warble in a tenor direction, but
he might do better as a baritone. And if he were
to go into steady training for an axemen’s tourna-
ment, or a brief interview with Bill Squires, his
chest notes would command more attention from
his fellow man.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Sweet Nell ” Stewart, at latest report, had engaged
to “star” a New York season in “The Lion and the
Mouse ”at £2OO a week. After a genuine “big success”
at ’Frisco, her co. didn’t frizzle the Missouri during the
rest of the U.S. tour.
At Cincinnati (U.S.A.), recently, the ever-verdant
Kyrle Bellew celebrated his 700th consecutive appearance
in “Raffles,”and was presented by an admiring co. with
a silver loving-cup.
Beauteous Gladys Ure, the Melbourne girl in ’Murka, is
now going one better than her recent concert, where all
the songs were from her own pen. As the result of her
success, she has signed a 12 months’ engagement with
Schuberts Bros, to play the leading part in “ Veronique.”
Moreover, Miss Gladys is to get a mighty big salary for a
novice, and Schuberts stipulated for the first refusal of
her songs. The “ Veronique ” tour starts at Philadelphia,
the co. going on to Boston and Chicago ere returning to
Nerv York. Gladys Ure, by the way, promises to be one
of the fashionable idols of the States. Some time ago a
New York paper invited a conflict of male opinions as to
who was the loveliest creature on the stage, and the
opinion of one Harrison Fisher was published in impres-
sive style. He said : “ After going through a long list of
stage beauties, I haVe come to the conclusion that Miss
Gladys Ure, now in Mr. H. W. Savage’s ‘ Woodland,’ at
the New York Theatre, is the one. Miss Ure has my ideal
face. It’s beautifully moulded and full of character, and
topped with a mass of rich auburn-colored hair. In
height she is about sft. 10in., and she has a superb
figure.”
There'is a plump singer in Melbourne, with a voice like
mashed potatoes, that the patient public has never quite
known what to do with. It fancies that his absence from
the Block would improve the scenery and confer a favor
on th e pate de foie gras caf6 he strongly favors, besides
making Europe happy. For this worthy object a benefit
concert would be arranged, and fat Teutons with re-
bellious hair, round specs., and wavy legs would hie them
round with ’cello or trombone for a collection, and tell
gushing females what a splendid thing it would be for
“demusikart” in Melbourne if they sent the prodigy
elsewhere. But the prodigy always lingered on, and these
benefit concerts came to be a kind of annual function.
When Melbourne grew weary and stiff, the noble duty of
sending the musical genius far away was explained to the
heathen of Ballarat and Bendigo. Still the beauteous
youth didn’t shift. The case became desperate. Now
they are going to give him an absolutely last benefit and
book his passage with the proceeds. So, presumably, the
great singer will not be seen any more leaning languidly'
over the cream cake counter, or falling gracefully all
round the Block, like a cow swaying in the autumn gale.
“Ageratum”: Re Irene Ainsley (B. 30,8/’O6). Don’t
think the English press gave all the credit of the
damsel’s tuition to Marchesi. Have been reading an
account of Irene in an English periodical (Girls’
Realm), which remarks that she studied for
eight months under Marchesi, but gives all the
credit of her teaching to Madame Minna Fischer,
of London, who is also Australian born. Mar-
chesi is only' a kind of necessary hall-mark, anyhow.
What does it matter if she is 80 or 100 and can’t teach as
she did in her younger days ? The fact that a young
singer has warbled under her will bring crowds in con-
servative London, and that is the main thing. Am told
that an interesting feature of Irene Ainsley’s concert was
that it was entirely manned (or girled) by four youngsters
in whom Melba takes a violent and helpful interest, the
Parkiua, little Sassoli, who harped it in the diva’s train in
these parts, Irene, and Maud M’Carthy'.
In a recent issue of Westminster Gazette an actor,
moved by the common belief concerning the vastness of
the salaries of those who seek behind the footlights the
means to live, throws some light upon the real position
of affairs, and offers another much-needed warning to
stage-struck youth. The actor declares that not long ago
he was wanted co play “ heavy lead ” in a very successful
melodrama, still on tour, and that he was offered the
magnificent sum of £2 10s. per week. With that salary'
in view he had to find a frock-coat suit, a lounge suit, an
evening dress suit, and a hunting suit ; a silk hat, dress
shirts, collars, ties, &c. ; grease-paints and a wig. He
had, in addition, to pay his fare to Glasgow for rehearsal,
and the tour was to last for thirteen weeks. He declined
the offer, and the part was given to another, who took
£2 a week for the work. The average provincial salary
is not more than 505., says the actor, and he puts the
price paid for the services of a leading lady, who, in nine
cases out of ten, has to provide her own dresses, at any-
thing between 30s. and £2 ss. Further, it is asserted
that “ responsible people ” —actors and actresses cast for
small parts —are to be found everywhere at 255. per week.
London critic Max Beerbohm is kind enough to offer
public sympathy to the ballet-girl, on the ground that
she is condemned to an undue development of leg and
consequent Joss of symmetry'. But when the ballerina
looks round she sees vocalists with enormous bust

Australia is to be visited by a big brass band from
Europe—not tbe Sousa organisation, which was to have
arrived three years ago, but the “ Besses o’ th’ Barn ”
band, under the direction of Tait Brothers. The
“Besses” sailed for America in July, and will get to
Maoriland early in January. After playing a fortnight’s
engagement at the Exhibition for a fee of £2500, they
proceed to Australia, and make the welkin ring in the
largest buildings of the principal cities. The band has
beaten all other English bands in competition, and won
many thousands of pounds in prize money.
The latest about George Musgrove is that he left the
“Sweet Nell” Co. “resting” in America whilst he
journeyed to London and Berlin in quest of artists for a
Grand" Opera Co. (a Melbourne scheme), “for which
further subscriptions are needed to complete the guaran-
tee promised to Mr. Musgrove.” The season of this
proposed co. is not to open until Easter next year, and,
meanwhile, G.M. appears to be securing artists and musi-
cians in order to hasten the completion of that guarantee.
Melba will be singing in London again next month, for
she is engagedfor the autumn season.of semi-grand opera,
starting at Covent Garden on October 4. The difference
between full-grand and semi-grand opera is mainly a
difference of time and tariff, it seems. The company, as
a whole, is less expensive in the autumn, but the same
operas are performed with the same mounting as before,
whilst the highest price for a stall is I2s. 6d. In due
course, no doubt, Covent Garden will be an all-the-year-
round opera house, with one short season of stiff prices.
Britain’s musical public is said to have doubled its number
within the past decade, which may, or may not, be a good
national sign of the times. No country contains a larger
proportion of people who are too consistently' hungry to
have any music in their souls, or of people who treat the
opera as a mere excuse for a society gathering.
Some parsons seem to see, or to think they see, a great
deal. The clerical critic of the Argus, in criticising a
recent singer : “ Add to all this an indefinable personal
charm of virtue, of which she at once established a kind of
intimacy with everyone of her audience —a sort of feeling
that she is singing just for him.” He goes on to say that
this accounts in part for her unique popularity. He also
says that her accompanist proves himself equal to the re-
quirements of his position, and concludes with the an-
nouncement that, owing to heavy booking, an extension
of the season has been decided on.
“ O’F.”: The recently-published photograph of Blanche
Arral’s fancy-dress-ball presentment of “Carmen ” stirred
a travelled Australian, who has sampled many stage ver-
sions of the tobacco-factory heroine, to a eulogy of Emma
Calv C’s performance in the part. Opera-goers had been
used to seeing a trim-geared “ Carmen,” finished off with
high heels and mantilla (like Mme. Arral’s), w'hen CalvC
burst upon it as the real careless, jolly factory-wench who
has just knocked off work, minus the elaborate mantilla,
and wearing any old blouse and a “ working skirt,” just
short enough to show her every-day down-at-heels shoes.
And throughout CalvC (after close study of the type in
its native factory) dared to act to the life the pleasure-
loving “ tom,” who is determined to get just what
she wants. This, of course, was voted inartistic by the
conventional kind of prima donna, who looks with the
cold eye of suspicion on all acting in opera, and walks on
in pretty well the same wav whether she is supposed to
be a queen or beggar-maid. For many years Patti
refused to appear as Carmen on the ground that the part
was immoral, and when she at last made up her mind to
pourtray stage “ immorality,” the public seemed to think
that she didn’t know how.
“ March King ” Sousa recently poured forth a legiti-
mate grievance into the ears of the Yankee Parlia-
mentary committee charged with drafting the new Copy-
right Bill. While reproducers like Melba and Caruso re-
ceived dazzling sums for singing into the phonograph,
and even cornet players got five dollars for a solo, gifted
composers (like Sousa) had their compositions tinned
wholesale, and got no fee, and had no redress. A sym-
pathetic committee promised to make the gifted com-
poser’s permission necessary in future.
An early music-hall sketch of W. S. Gilbert’s shows
the remarkable persistence of certain vaudeville types.
For instance :—“ A terrible woman, with big bones, a
raw, brazen voice, and her hair parted at the side, came
on to the stage, and screamed and roared, and slapped
her hands, and danced, and then sang again, and then
danced, and sang, and banged herself once more, which
was her energetic way of advising you, under all circum-
stances of life, to ‘speak up like a ma-a-nl’” Similar
ladies have been giving similar advice in the same curious
manner right through the half-century since that was
written, and the precedent seems likely to be followed as
long as there is a variety show in existence.
France has finally decided to do without dramatic
censorship. The institution has been moribund for many
years, and the Senate, by unanimous vote, recently
swept it into the dust-bin. Britain, however, still
retains its antiquated system of censorship, and puts
it into force whenever a play fails to win the approval of
some tottering official with musty notions of dramatic
propriety.
“ Camille ” is following in the downward path of “ East
Lynne,” which was also in the remote past a classic that
took a good company to tackle. Nearly every touring
company now takes “Camille” with it as a weepful
attraction, and as it has not been so overdone as “Lynne,”
it is temporarily in the ascendant asa draw. But since it
lacks the bathetic child who dies and goes to heaven
before the tearful eyes of the audience, it will never have
quite the pull that the old story had. The average audi-
ence loves to see a child going to heaven to mournful
music; there always is mournful music when anyone
goes to heaven, which says very little for the popular con-
fidence in that place.
“Querist”: Why do the wheels of a vehicle in a
biograph go backward, while the vehicle goes forward?
“ Lil.” : Damsels of the Dorothy Vernon type were old
acquaintances of mine before I saw Dorothy on the
boards. The rampageous maiden who is determined to
provide herself with a husband forms an indispensable
portion of the “ comic relief ” in the works of many
Elizabethan dramatists, and sometimes it is decidedly
bold relief. Besides being responsible for Miss Vernon,
the man-hunting girl of Tudor times seems to be the
stage ancestress of the red-nosed man-woman of modern
pantomime.
“ Bis ” : “ Boisseau ” (B. 23/8/’O6) wants to know if any-
body remembers hypnotist Professor L. Bushell. I saw
him give his conventional mesmerising entertainment (a
lot of chumps stared at leaden discs in their hands till
some of them were dotty and others pretended to be so),
at Castlemaine, in ’59. He was an affable looking man of
about 32, resembling Alfred Deakin a little. Bushell
began with a weak 25 minutes’ lecture on chemistry,
with a few cheap experiments. The second part he
called “ electro-biology,” Kennedy, in the 80’s, was much
cleverer at the game.
“The Prince Chap,” a rather cheap emotional Ameri-
canism, that has just reached London, seems to achieve
something of a record, by having the heroine played by
three different actresses each night. In the first act she is
aged five years ; an infant phenomenon about eight deals
with that. Next act, where she is really eight, a clever
child, pf probably 12, takes the part. Finally, she is 18,
and makes love to her own astonished but delighted
guardian ; and a full-grown actress does the love-
making.
Little Cliff, it seems, has little of the ordinary self-
complacency of youth. In Melbourne, the other day, he
was up at Dr. Bussell’s place having his teeth attended
to, and, after the work was completed, he stood moodily
contemplating himself in a mirror. “Do you ever get
tired of your face ? ” said Cliff to the dentist. “I do.
When I’m made-up for the stage, I think I’m so pretty ;
but when I look at myself like this, I think it’s a trying
face to have to live with, and I wish I could change it.”
What a pity that some of the so-called beauty actors
don’t acquire Little Cliff’s sense of the difference between
a face colored and a face plain. Perhaps they would if
the girls would let them. But the girls mostly persist in
adoring the battered old actor they meet off the stage for
the sake of his deceitful appearance between 8 p.m. and
II p.m. And when their speaking eyes and eloquent
smiles call him Lovely—well, how could an actor possibly
doubt the truth of the assertion ?
“Buna”: Will some courageous Irish or Scotch person
of some importance rise up and form the nucleus of
a society for the suppression of the old song? After the
shock at-the awfulness and heresy of the suggestion has
been got over, everybody would be glad to join, although
it is still the fashion to say, sentimentally, “ Ah, there
are no songs like the old ones ! ” We have all said it,
but only because there is nothing else possible to say
about “Annie Laurie” and “Kate O’Shane” at this
stage. They are good songs, but so are less hackneyed
and antiquated compositions. Why not give some of the
new ones a chance of becoming moss-grown also? One
feels sorry for the Scotch prima-donna breaking her heart
over the “ Blue Bells of Scotland,” and the Queen of Irish
song pumping up enthusiasm over that wearisome
person, “ Kathleen Mavourneen,” and acting sentiments
that in both cases they must be deadly bored with.
“ Bobert Hap” : Let us have an unconventional melo-
drama. It should be possible. Of the last 39 imported
bellows I have partly seen, the villain in 31 has been
an army captain. And the doings of those 31 have
lowered my respect, for the “ otticah and gentleman”
tradition. lam tired of the peer’s honest old trainer.
He’s as unreal as the wronged wife of the hero, who
prefers to starve in the garret rather than accept a pig’s
trotter from the kindly “ comic relief.” In real life, the
honest old trainer wouldn’t think of saying, “ I’m d d
if I do ” to the ’aughtv captain’s blandishments re the
nobbling of the “Darby” favorite. He would respect-
fully decline, but only because he looked upon the pro-
posal as an attempt on the part of his betters to dis-
arrange the dull monotony of his existence. And I want
the fallen Buth to marry the curate. She has become a
nurse, and gone to the war too often. And the black-
mailer with “ the document ” might be displaced in favor
of a land agent’s dummy and a faked lease. Finally, I
don’t want master Jack to run away to sea and return
after two years in an admiral’s uniform. Let him just
murder the captain as soon as possible and then start a
tote.
Greater Sydney.*
Sydney barrister J. D. Fitzgerald has for his
hobby the organisation of a Greater Sydney.
Having had the advantage of a study of the work
of old-world municipalities, he saw that in one
respect usually progressive Australia lagged
behind the rest of the world, and that was in up-to-
date city government, and returned to his native
country to preach municipal reform. And, so
far, the results have been, if not very great, at
least encouraging. To Fitzgerald’s platform and
press agitation the reform of Sydney’s municipal
government is largely due ; and the effects of a
persistent campaign show a little also in other
directions. But the inspiring work for a “ civic
statesman” —Fitzgerald has earned that title—is
the organisation of a Greater Sydney, putting
the government of a city, unrivalled in the world
for natural beauty, on rational lines,‘ so that the
blessings given it by Nature will be aided by man.
“Greater Sydney,” published this week, embodies
in a handy book the case for that great reform.
The style is terse and bright; the facts hammered
in convincingly. Telling how Sydney is at pre-
sent governed Fitzgerald thus sums up the situa-
tion :
It has 41 councils, nearly 500 aldermen, and 41 staffs to
do the work which 77 aldermen and one staff does in the
model city of Glasgow. It has built 41 costly town halls;
it has 41 separate debts, amounting to £2,428,000 ; it has
41 different health problems, met and solved in different
ways—in some boroughs effectively, in others negligently.
Then follows a sketch of how State, private,
municipal, Trust and Board control of vari-
ous utilities are all jumbled up together in
Sydney; and the history of the two Greater
Sydney movements, which came to nothing.
There are chapters on “ How to Launch Greater
Sydnev,” “ The Financial Position,” “The Cost
of Greater Sydney and the Services thereof,”
on “ City Improvement and Better Housing,” the
provision of parks and recreation grounds, and the
value of municipal ownership. It is, so far as The
Bulletin knows, the first Australian work of any
pretensions dealing with municipal politics. And
it is a good beginning.
* “ Greater Sydney,” by J. D. Fitzgerald. Published by
N.S.w! Bookstall Co. ; price Is.
“ The Law Book Co. of Australasia ” oub-
lishes “The Mirror of Justice,” being a reprint
of a series of humorous articles contributed by
Sydney barrister Curlewis (whose wife, by the
way, is the well-known authoress Ethel Turner)
to Sydney Telegraph. Mr. Curlewis mixes up
with his explanations of law points a good deal of
comedy, which is often spontaneous and seldom
obviously labored. The book can be recommended
as giving a good deal of information on the law in
an amusing way.
The municipality of Collingwood (Mel-
bourne) has had a big funeral experience lately.
First councillor Dyer, who had just been elected
mayor, died. His funeral was on the 2nd inst.
The half-mast flag had barely been furled
when ex-councillor Shaw (manager of the
Denton Hat Mill and a big figure in the munici-
pality) died. Then, within two days, councillor
Wm. Cody followed.

As to the Foreign-trade argument that
Deakin’s preferential trade proposals offer no
genuipe preference to England, these figures, from
a British Board of Trade return, are instructive.
They show the estimated ad valorem equivalent of
the import duties levied on British manufactures :
Russia, 131 per cent. ; U.S.A., 73 ; Austria, 35 ; France,
34 ; Germany, 25 ; Canada, 16 ; Belgium, 13 ; Maoriland,
9 ; Australian Commonwealth, 6.
Were the Australian tariff equivalent to that of
France, or even of Canada, we could, perhaps,
afford to lower it in Britain’s favor, but it is not
so ; therefore it is necessary to raise it all round
and then lower it against Britain, which is just
what Deakin is doing, in a few lines. The Free-
trader in this is, as usual, not so much pro-
English as anti-Australian.
No doubt the farcical decision of the
London magistrate that only pot-still spirit can
legally be called whisky will be upset on appeal.
But, anyway, whiskies are known by their brand.
Wise men, the still and silent sort, will still con-
tinue to ask for D.C.L. without wondering what
still it was distilled in. Drinkers prove by personal
experience that D.C.L. possesses all the qualities
which make a whisky wholesome, palatable, and
invigorative.*
The Maitland (N.S.W.) coal-miners having
declined to work the odious “dog watch’’—the
shift that starts at midnight—the owners
have shut up the mines, and will not allow them
to work any other shifts; and so close up to
3000 men are idle. The public can’t understand
why the trouble couldn’t have been settled. Pos-
sibly some of the owners didn’t want it settled ; a
labor trouble is the simplest way out of unprofit-
able coal contracts. On one thing, anyway, the
public can fix its eye : the “dog watch ” is worked
on no other coalfield in Australia.

warded on to the widow.

Newcastle (N.S.W.) agitates for a local
Harbor Trust similar to the Sydney institution.
Newcastle is almost as busy a port as Sydney, but
its methods are antiquated, and the local people
say that as long as it is governed from its compe-
titor, Sydney, improvement is impossible. So
Newcastle proposes to have its own local body
with power to levy charges on the local shipping,
and from the revenue raised to make the coal city
an up-to-date port.
Jack Murray, ex-Minister for Lands in
Bent’s Ministry, spoke in Vic. Assembly last
week something like the old Jack Murray :
The old pioneers did their work nobly, and they did it
easily for the most part. They came here and dis-
possessed the blackfellow, and if there was any moral
ground for doing so, it was because the blackfellow was
not putting the land to its proper use. Those pioneers
objected very strongly to have now the rule applied to
them which they or their fathers had applied to the
blackfellow. But I ask my squatter friends if they can
honestly say that they are doing the best with the land?
“ S.” : I have discovered a specialty in
jim-jams in the vicinity of the Waranga Basin (Vic-
toria). The specialist was a young pick and shovel
hand, and his particular devils took an original
form. When he had taken a great deal too much
for a week or so, he began to see things—not
painted griffins, nor plaid dogs, nor purple fiends,
but quaint and curious flowers. One afternoon he
was discovered crawling about in a paddock on
his knees, plucking nothing with one hand and
carefully placing it in the other. When ques-
tioned, the sufferer explained that he was gather-
ing a bunch for Sarah. The idea of a bunch of
rare-colored fantods as a present for one’s best
girl strikes me as unique, and a love poem should
be written round it.
Premier Carruthers was quite put out, the
other day, when a deputation of Sydney wire-
workers asked him to have some rabbit-proof
netting made locally. Snapped Joseph :
The farmers themselves—not the general taxpayer—
are paying for the imported netting. You are asking
that the farmers should he charged so much extra in
order to give you employment.
Well, even if that were so, the wire-workers, as
general taxpayers, have been bled pretty freely
hitherto to help along the farmers with cheap
railway rates, free seed wheat, &c., &c., and turn
about is fair play. But the chances are it isn’t so.
If the history of the deferred payments of land
instalments, &c., is any criterion, the general tax-
payers, including the Sydney wire-workers, will
have to pay for some of the imported rabbit-proof
netting yet.
H. G. Wells, for the benefit of S.M.
Herald and the rest of the syndicate that is
publishing his “Future in America,” gives an
extremely ugly specimen of that peculiarly Ameri-
can product, the millionaire
He clutched a long cigar with a great, clumsy hand.
He turned on you a queer, coarse, disconcerting bottle
nose with a little, hard-blue, wary, hostile eye, that
watched out from the roots, of it. He said nothing. He
attempted no civility, he looked pride and insults : you

“It was Roman,” my friend said. “ There has been
nothing like it since the days of that republic. No living

These people walked up to him and talked to him ; they
tried to flatter him and get him to listen to projects.
Abjectly. And, you know, he grunted. He didn’t talk
back. It was beneath him. He just grunted at them ! ”
Mysterious whispers are once more going
round the press to the effect that “measures may
shortly be taken to end the abnormal situation
created by the incurable insanity of the unhappy,
but robust. King Otho of Bavaria.” He is said
to imagine that he is a fowl, and to spend a good
deal of his time in a nest made of straw and fire-
wood ; but between the rare king who pretends to
be a hen and the very frequent one who pretends
to be a general and an admiral, the difference
isn’t very wide.

PENEOLD'S
WINES
Families of the ennobbled dead in Aus-
tralia may now retain the emblems bestowed by
royal favor. That accounts for the recent draw-
ing-room exhibition of the Star of St. Michael
and St. George to a company, some of whom
thought it was for sale. Under the old law an
Australian taking a title had to find a guarantor
that the emblem would be returned on his death,
but the difficulty of dragging the relic away from
ns widow induced royalty to relax the rule. It
had to relax it, in fact, unless it was going to take
the bauble by force, after the manner of Albert
Aorman, when he drags the heroine all over the
stage in a B. Holt play to make her give up the
Document.

1 logging is going off the bill at Darling-
burst and other N.S. W. prisons. Ten years ago

authorities show a quite perturbed state of mind
when the triangles are asked for. Part of the
failing-off is attributed to the exclusion of the
press from floggings. The lurid par whetted
appetite, and magistrates and judges kept up the
supply. When K. E. O’Connor, now High Court
■ udg j\i then j N 'k‘ W * Minister for Justice, first

he prophesied this result.
According to statements made by members
of N.S.W. Assembly, the Railway Commissioners
managed to get up another quarrel over the new
tram sections required when the Central Railway
fetation was opened. Public convenience could
only be properly studied by granting overlapping
sections, and Chairman Oliver proposed this, but,
apparently fur the sake of picking a fresh quarrel
with their chief, the two junior Commissioners
combined in opposition, and insisted on an arbi-
trary arrangement which necessitated crowds of
passengers paying a penny for a few yards’ jour-
ney to Regent-street, or walking the distance.
W hen this was announced loud protests were
made by the public, the press, and Parliament;
yet it was three weeks before the junior Commis-
sioners gave ip to Oliver’s scheme of sections. Of
course, these inharmonious relations were Oliver's
fault.

“P. : Let any man who, objects to giving
gaoled criminals useful industrial work make a
visit to Pentridge, or some other big prison. One
set of men at Pentridge is employed, inside wire
barricades, at breaking stones. They sit in front
of their work, and tap monotonously with a 51b.
spawling hammer. Their work is useless, and
they know it. Pentridge in a month can quarry
and break more stone than it can use in a year,
and it has no outlet for the surplus. The men
know that their labor is futile, and they tap away
mechanically and sullenly. Elsewhere in the
prison other men are brush-making, printing,
book-binding, or working at the machines in a
sadly primitive woollen-mill. These fellows are
alert, active, intelligent, industrious. Man by a
long process of civilisation has become a working
animal. Put him to useful and productive labor,
and you reform him by means of his own energy.
Condemn him to idleness, or a forced occupa-
tion which he knows to be purposeless, and you
deepen his criminality. One look at those two
groups of men should end for ever the foolish cry
against woeful prison labor.

of the piece. I was chari-
tably supposing that, owing
to her peculiar habit of mind,
she had distorted some mere
act of courtesy upon the part
of that ingenuous young poet,
when, suddenly, he himself
strolled into observation
from the other side of the
boat. When he came across
to me, and made some com-
monplace remark .about the
weather, I felt it was a
psychological moment, and
made a well-meaning attempt
to bring the lady gracefully
into the conversation, as appar-
ently he hadn’t noticed her pre-
sence. To my surprise, he gazed
blankly at her, while she went an
angry crimson, glared at me as if I had
acted with malice aforethought, and de-
camped to the other end of the boat. They
h:id never met before, and the young' man
didn’t know her from the proverbial bar of soap !

There is a hubbub at Adelaide over the
use of incense at an Anglican (of course fashion-
able) church. The Bishop disapproves of incense
in church processions and during actual worship ;
he thinks it can only be legitimately employed to
“ sweeten the church before the service.” As to
the desirableness of incense, much depends on
what happens to be the dominant odor of Society
assembled at worship. In old Sydney, for
instance, it used to be rum-and-onions.
* * * «
Despite a N.S.W. Board of Health regu-
lation forbidding it, a few cheap, insanitary
butchers are accustomed to wrap meat in old
newspapers which have made the round of two or
three, possibly germ-infested, households. T’other
day, however, two suburban offenders bumped a
10s. fine and costs, and as 10s. will buy a lot of
clean, white paper,' the filthy practice will
probably stop.

“ Homo ” : A lot of manslaughter has
been perpetrated in Bulletin barber-shop yarns
from time to time ; but, judging by tbe number of
hair-shifters who become insane whilst actively
following their calling, it’s a wonder to me that
there’s such an absence of real tragedy in the
business. I know, as a positive fact, that there
are at present no less than eight of the calling
confined in one Victorian metropolitan asylum,
while a couple of “temporarily relieved” cases
from the same institution (I feel a cold shudder
travelling along my spine) have resumed opera-
tions with the razor.
* * * #
Adelaide City Council has taken in hand
the regulation of hair-dressing and shaving
saloons. By-laws provide that all tools of trade,
such as brushes, combs, clippers, etc., must be
efficiently cleansed and disinfected at least once a
day. Each customer is to be supplied with a
clean towel, and the barber must cleanse his
hands by washing after each shave. The use of
powder puff, sponge, styptic, magnesium or other
emollient in block form is also prohibited, under
a penalty of £lO for each offence.

“Gip”: “ G.M.” (30/8/’O6) isn’t quite
fair to Bung, in his par. on the bar-girl. Hotel-
keepers running the more decent of the hotels
never allow their girls to drink with customers.
In fact, in a first-class bar a nice man would not
invite a girl to drink with him. Anyone so doing
would probably offend the girl, and mark himself
as an outsider at the same time. Some Sydney
hotel-keepers would turn rainbow tints' with
horror at the sight of one of their girls drinking
openly in the bar. But the great big temptation
remains. The girl is sometimes alone, and she is
often very tired.

“B.”: Be barmaids in Australia. Spiers
and Pond, once of Australia, now of London, do
not allow barmaids to drink in the bars either
with customers or alone. That should be an easy
reform to bring about. , If a man buys a pound of
tea or tin of jam he doesn’t ask the counter-lady
to have one, and, if he did, she would probably
refuse. Anyway, Spiers and Pond successfully
enforce their rule, to the admitted advantage of
the barmaids and without appreciably repelling a
paying class of visitors.

“F.C.”: I have just acquired several
bushels of gratuitous information, a few more-or-
less veiled insults, and an insight into the way
“news” originates. The other day, a brief
paragraph in the Age informed the public that
a Richmond lady had given birth, at one sitting
so to speak, to four children. During the next
few days I learnt from sundry sources (1) that the
four babies were all girls; (2) that the father was
but 21 years of age and the mother 22; (3) that
the mayor, being desirous of assisting these
eminently dutiful citizens, had made inquiries,
but could find no trace of them ; (4) that the quad
was a solemn fact, and that the parents were
charging an inquiring public half-a crown ahead
to view the batch; (5) that a bounty had been
applied for, and Bent was going to “do some-
thing.” Then I investigated for myself, and
ultimately found that a local resident was the
cause of all the excitement. A friend of his, a few
doors away, had lately acquired triplets—two of
whom had since died—and he seemed to have had
a magnifying eye. There was no quad at all.
“ Vulcan ” : Yankee Macfadden’s cult of
the “ altogether ” (B. IG/8/’O6) isn’t the first of its
kind. Laing’s “Notes of a Traveller” tells
of another crowd, popularly known as
the “ Muckers,” which had a brief, but
lurid, career in Germany during the
*V early part of last century. Unlike
Macfaddenism, “Aluckerism ” had
religion, not physical hygiene, as
its basis ; its main tenet was that
man (including woman) should
worship his (including her) Crea-
torin the state in which he’d
(including she’d) been pro-
duced. The sect was secret;
disciples arrived at chapel in
orthodox rig, and then un-
dressed and mixed promiscu-
ously for worship in the “alto-
gether.” Some of the forms
of worship the chronicler hints
f at, with a blush. The sect, which
included some of the Father-
land’s “naicest,” was only in
its infancy 7, but was making much
secret headway, when the boss
“Mucker” began to have visions.
The first inspiration communicated
to the sect was that a Mes-
siah was to bo born amongst
them. The next inspired
information was that lie (the
boss) was to be the new-
comer’s earthly father. He
later confidentially divulged
to a lady disciple (the beau-
tiful wife of a coun' the
heavenly-sent news tin., she
had been chosen as the
mother. The lady, also in
an inspired way, told her
husband. A mighty row be-
gan, and an astonished out-
side public started to learn
things. A few days later,
two nice, plump lady dis-
ciples died of “ sexual ex-
citement” after some hair-
raising initiation ceremonies.
Then the police called, and
the interesting sect became
non-existent.
Woollooinooloo has a
name for squalor that is, in
these days, undeserved ; for
the Sydney City Council has
cleaned it outwardly; and
Paddington, near by, can
now show the visitor more
untidiness. But . Woolloo-
rnooloo would require almost
complete re-building to be
other than squalid inwardly 7.
The number of three-roomed
places, fitted with every in-
convenience, in Sydney’s an-
cient suburbis appalling; and
their landlords get appalling
rents for them. It is a locality
in which a woman can often
lean out of her top window
and quarrel with her neighbor
at her top window, and drag her hair violently,
and bump her head against the window frame.
There are three-roomed houses in Woolloomooloo
(“front-room ” and kitchen below, and one attic
above, to which the stairs rise from the kitchen)
that run a small store in the front apartment j
and there are three-roomed houses that announce
’‘rooms to let’’—mainly on account of the high
rents charged for houses that shouldn’t be let at all.
* * * •
Victor Lansell, old George Lansell’s 23-
year-old son, who is wallowing in the pelf left
behind by his late pa, has solved the military
question as far as it applies to Bendigo. Victor
has been seized with a bad attack of military
enthusiasm, and has attained to thedignity of sub-
altern in the local infantry regiment. That regi-
ment, in the past, has had difficulty in keeping
up to its strength ; but Lansell has removed that
difficulty, and made it look as if Bendigo, in the
near future, will want to start two or three more
regiments. This is his system : He owns about
20 mines, which employ about 1500 men ; he has
a controlling interest in about 20 more, which
employ another 1500 men; that makes 3000.
Victor has issued orders that no man is to be
employed on any of these mines unless he is a
member of an infantry regiment. There are a
few exceptions; but they are all members of
Victor’s pet football clubs. Presumably he is
going to allow the necessary time off to attend
camps and drills ; and pay for it.

Why is there not one word against
gambling in the Bible ? Because it assumes that
people are better for losing their money. On the
other hand the Church assumption is that Gain is
Godliness.

Recent disclosures in England as to the
making of jam-cakes, biscuits and sweets by some
of the biggest firms rival the Chicago meat
horrors. They should be published in Australia
for the pleasant afternoon-tea reading of those
folk who can’t endure “colonial” biscuits or
sweets.
JSbS.W. public service has been circu-
larised with the offer of a billet in Japan at £4OO
a year as secretary to Suttor, commercial agent in
Asia. The applicant must be able to conduct the
affairs of the agency in Suttor’s temporary
absences; must be an expert typist and shorthand
writer, with a debonair appearance and an awe-
inspiring manner; alsb have a perfect knowledge of
the natural resources of this great country, and a
thorough acquaintance with the nocent tactics of
the wily N.S.W. exporter. (Integrity, honesty,
steadiness and ability are also demanded, but that
demand counts for nothing, for are we not all
sober, honest and industrious?) N.S W. public
service expects to get high ability cheap. The
expectation is rarely fulfilled.

The old trouble, as between the decent
employer and the sweater, confronted Vic. Chief
Secretary Gillott lately, when one or two reputable
firms and the employees in the card-box-malnng
industry asked for a Wages Board. One con-
scienceless firm was said to employ girls at so low
a rate of piece-work pay that one victim had
managed to earn only Is. 9d. for a week’s work,
while another had toiled four days for 6d.—less
than a farthing an hour ! Question for the
parsons—is there a hotel in Melbourne, or any-
where else, as conducive to immorality a« an
employer who pays a girl sixpence for four days’
work ?

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We meet with sprightly step, and eye
Abeam, the mind elated ;
We chat, the lightning hours go by,
Yet much remains unstated,
No matter how he yearns, or I,
We’re never satiated.
We part, each satisfied, and yet
Convinced we have been cheated ;
It seems we hod this minute met,
Ourselves at tea secreted,
And now to part—alas, to let
Talk thus go uncompleted !
Cut, ah ! the rosy hours do race
In such delicious chatter,
The spirit flashes from each face,
And nothing seems to matter.
The laughter has no lack of grace ;
Ours is a harmless clatter.
And always with a gracious glow
Our friendly minds commingle,
’Tis even sweet to murmur low
Some scrap of song or jingle ;
To make no love—for, ah ! we know
’Tis love that keeps us single.
VIC.

Is made of the purest and best of materials. It
acts upon the mucous membrane of the throat and
bronchial tubes, and very soon after the first dose
you feel its good effects. There is a soothing of
the inflamed and irritated mucous membranes,
and this soothing effect is felt at all places where
there is any irritation. You know the mucous
membrane extends from the throat down into the
stomach. Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy extends
its goodness all the way to the stomach, which
accounts for its being so good in deep-seated
coughs. The healthy mucous membrane is very
susceptible to outside influence. That is why you
catch cold so easily. That is why a dose of
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy has such good
effect if taken at the first indication of a cough or
cold. After your cold has developed, the mem-
brane of the throat is congested, and it is harder
to get it under control, but Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy does it. Keep a bottle in the house for
safety’s sake.*

309-311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
A novel way of making a living has been
adopted by the son of a very well-known Mel-
bourne painter. Just now he is travelling Up
and down Sydney Harbor by the Manly boats
making pencil portrait-sketches for the public at
a shilling a head. He says it pays, and he means
to voyage right round the world on the same
game. In London, by the way, there is quite a
number of clever artists who do quick portraits—
one and two-hour sittings—for moderate fees.
The success of a portrait, anyhow, is not always
in proportion to the time spent over it. Probably
the best portrait that John Longstaff ever painted
in his life—that of Henry Lawson, now hanging
in N.S.W. National Gallery —was done in under
three hours, and Millais could not have painted a
more graphic picture.
* * * *
The Salvarmy becomes more polished and
decorative as it gets older. It no longer plays
“Tommy, Make Room for YoUr Uncle,” as a
hymn-tune. Its bands, whether on march or in
barracks, play mostly good music ; and the uni-
forms of its officers grow artistic. In time the
Army will go in for altars and reredoses, and
copes and stoles. All religious movements seem
to develop from austerity or vulgarity to highly-
decorated ritual.' It is only in their first stages
that they invite persecution. The modern re-
spectable Christian would not be sent to the
lions of Diocletian. He would be a favored
figure at State functions, and the Rome Tribune
would record the dress his wife wore when pre-
sented to Mrs. Diocletian.
Now that there is a Marconi fixture
between Victoria and Tasmania, they might as
well include poor old King Island. Crusoe’s
speck was not much mere out of touch with civi-
lisation than this place. One small boat, the
Yambacoona, runs from Launceston to the Island
once a fortnight (with luck). It is the only direct
means of communication. It carries all pas-
sengers, news, mails that come or go, and the
weather is often too bad for it to run.
* * * *
In the Hr. Stockwell v. Queensland Go-
vernment action—Which resulted in the doctor
getting £2219 damages for wrongful dismissal—it
was admitted that some of the doctor’s reports on
the condition of Dunwich Asylum were altered
by the Government before submission to Parlia-
ment. Crown counsel alleged that this was
necessary, so as “not to rouse public indigna-
tion ; ” but Chief Justice Cooper sat heavily on
the Crown by describing such proceeding as “a
disgrace to Queensland.” The public is now ask-
ing if the same practice prevails in all depart-
ments, and, if so, what is the precise value of an
official document touched up to suit the views of
a Minister ? No official with a healthy backbone
would tolerate such tampering with his reports,
and Dr. Stockwell erred in not exposing the fraud
at the time.

Latest drink notion—which hasn’t pene-
trated to Australia yet—is a drop of liquid air in
your whisky and soda. It make the drink dance
with a million diamonds, and, of course, freezes it
perfectly.
* • * *
The plaint of an ancient: —
At the Parramatta (N.S.W.) old men’s asylum, the
maintenance cost per head is now reduced to ss. per
week, yet the sum demanded from paying inmates
has been raised from 7s. to 10s. a Week. In the case of
the old age pensioner, this is not only hard and
dishonest, but it is a plain breach of the Act.
Clause 31 of the Pension Act states that where a
pensioner is maintained in a charitable institution, the
reasonable cost of such maintenance shall be payable
out of the pension, but any surplus shall be paid
to the pensioner. The dodge is worked by illegally
making the pensioner surrender his certificate on
entering an asylum, whereupon he loses his pension
rights. The instalments of the pension can only legally
be forfeited if the asylums are classed as gaols, which
they have been brought to closely resemble under the
Carruthers-Hogue administration.
A story about an Asiatic philosopher. He
was asked to divide a bag of nuts among his
friends. He gave 50 to one, 20 to another, one to
another, and so on. Astonishment arose, and he
explained : “I am doing it as God would.”

“ Burchie ” : Disgraceful is the condition
of many of the Victorian country burying-
grounds. The worst case I’ve struck is at Caris-
brook, and Newbridge makes a good second.
The cemetery at ’Brook looks like an aban-
doned gold diggings. No fence, and the rabbits
in full possession. At one Vic. burying-place I
found a shin-bone, and at another a bottom jaw,
both exhumed by rabbits. In this way I hope to
build up a complete skeleton. Makes me feel
queer to think the bunnies will be scratching a
burrow through me when I’m “at rest.” Ad-
vance cremation !
N.S.W. Jewish hierarchy has promulgated
rules dealing with proselytes. The Jewish
religion has always rather looked askance at con-
verts ; and naturally. A man may “ change his
heart,” but Nature doesn’t allow him to change
his nose, and the prospect of a race of snub-nosed
Hebrews is disquieting. Few’versions to Jewry,
are, on the face of them, bona-fide. They are
usually to be accounted for by the desire of some
Christian lassie to marry a Wealthy Solomon, or
the infatuation of a Gentile for a Rebecca who
is firm not to marry “ out of the faith.” The Jew
who marries a Christian finds himself (or herself)
boycotted socially by the sterner members of the
race.

Out of six witnesses who clearly identified
a calf outside Melbourne law courts, the other
day, only one could accurately describe the
ahitnal when he got into the box. One—a woman,
of course—said she knew the beast by its face.
“It had blue eyes and an affectionate look,” but
she could not give its color or say whether it had
horns or not. On such evidence a man was con-
victed —wrongfully, as was afterwards proved.

“Yarrab”: The curse of uneasy Wor-
rality is with us. Was in a shop in Suburbia
one afternoon, when in ..bounced a miserable
specimen of the Bible-banging brigade, who de-
claimed frantically, to a frail woman within, against
the photo of a daintily-shaped and betighted
vaudeville Venus being placed in the shop window
for public observance. Said picture was perfectly
harmless, and was used as an advt. for a peripa-
tetic variety co. Only the leprous mind of the
bigoted cleric could attach the slightest nastiness

pulpit on the following Sunday. This amounted
to blackmail, and if I had had the faintest hope of
being able to finance the fine, I would have
applied the boot discourteous.
There is a good deal of ‘ ‘ shooting with the
mouth” about Australian military matters.
Latest instance is a speech by Colonel Tom Price
in which he makes the interesting allegation,
backed up to some extent by correspondence
(some of the correspondence he doesn't disclose),
that General Hutton got his post as Australian
Commander by wire-pulling and “smoodging,”
and endeavored to hold it by the same means.
Certainly the deference which was paid to such
absurd politico-military personages as Neild under
the Hutton regime bears out Price’s case to some
extent. A useful military reform would be to
put on the retired list all officers who win
Parliamentary honors. The interference of
amateur military experts in Parliament is one of
the chief vexations of Defence administration.
