Howard Ashton writes : Your art critic
(B. 6/9/’O6) bewails the lack of legs in Fred Leist’s
picture. He will find, if he looks carefully around
the Art Society’s show, that this leglessness is
common to most of the pictures. Since the Art
Society got its Royal Charter, it has passed a by-
law abolishing legs as indelicate.
The question of lodge-doctoring cropped
up again last week at a meeting of the Williams-
town (Vic.) branch of the A.N. A. The Austra-
lian Women’s Association invited the assistance
of the A.N. A. 111 bringing pressure to bear upon
a Medical Defence League which refuses to treat
the single women of the A.W.A. on ordinary
A.N. A. lodge terms. This seems to suggest that
serious-minded spinsters of the associated order
need a terrible lot of medical attendance, or else
would be awfully fond of sending for the dear
doctor to come along and souud them if he were
obtainable under the unremunerative lodge con-
tract. The artless spinsters are very indignant at
the idea of the various dear doctors “ conspiring
to repel this attempt to make them very cheap
doctors. And isn’t the spinsters’ organisation
“conspiring” to get the maximum of male medi-
cal attention at a minimum cost?

The.. Victorian Legisla-
ture has provided for the
stricter enforcement of school
attendance and the registra-
tion of private teachers and
their establishments. It is
about time some supervision
was exercised oyer the cur-
ricula of the iatter, especially
as regards the “ dame schools.”
A typical one of these is
conducted by an elderly
maiden lady of irreproach-
able character. There is
nothing to cavil at in the
moral precepts which she in-
culcates, but she confesses that
she “does not care about
those ‘School Papers’”; and
the result is that, while the
State school children a few
hundred yards down the street
are enriching their minds with
information relating to their
native land, her pupils aie
wading drearily through de-
tails connected with the causes
affecting the climate of Eng-
land, as set out in that ante-
diluvian series of publications,
the “Royal Readers.” Nor
is this all. For arithmetic she
uses one of the “ Irish Na-
tional School Books,” and
burdens the minds of her
poor little scholars with ar-
chaic tables of weights and
measures, which they will
probably never come across
again iti the course of their
existence. It is bad enough
for Austt alian children to be
denied the advantages of the
decimal system, but why on
earth they should have to
learn the number of yards in
an Irish perch passes compre-
hension.
Parson Tregear, at a re-,
cent Orange church service at
Collingwood (Vic.), announced
that after cycling through Ire-
land, he came to the con-
clusion that the cause of Ire-
land’s discontent was the
“breaking down of the Sab-
bath.” What strange theories
have been propounded to
account for Ireland’s discon-
tent with landlord robbery
and English misgovernrnent !
Julian Hawthorne declared
that the mildness of the Irish
climate was “the ruination
of the country.” . Others have-
blamed intermarriage, the ab-
sence of race suicide, celibacy,
potatoes, and horse-racing.
An ingenious M.P. named
Delahunty informed the House
of Commons that “the arrow
which pierced the heart of
Ireland was feathered with a
one-pound note, ’’ his panacea
being the abolition of one-
pound notes, though they
seem to do no harm in Scot-
land. Another theory, once
popular, was that the ruina-
tion was caused by the intro-
duction into Ireland of artificial manures and
superphosphates. But Parson Tregear’s theory
takes the fool prize.
Writes Miss Alfreda Arnold, in “ Church
Work in Japan,” just published Leprosy is
lamentably common in Japan—an estimate puts
the sufferers at 200,000 ; and no class is free from
the taint.” How will the Australian girls who
hob-nobbed with the Japs like that creepy item ?

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N.S. Wales’ financial history last year (to
June 30, 1906) was something; like this: —
Alleged revenue .. .. £12,291,367
Deduct land sales .. .. 976,772
More-or-less true revenue £11,314,595
Alleged expenditure.. .. £11,395,243
Add loan money spent .. 1,282,995
Total expenditure .. £12,678,238
Expenditure £12,678,238
Revenue 11,314,595
Deficit £1,363,643
Less Sinking Funds, etc. .. £350,000
Actual deficit .. .. £1,013,643
The Treasurer claims a surplus of £896,124. He arrives
at that by including the land sales proceeds as revenue,
and charging the' loan expenditure to another account.
The foregoing, however, shows the amount by which
N.S. W. did not pay its way, in this most prosperous year.
On the strength of this spurious surplus, and
on the strength of another spurious surplus for the
current year, Treasurer Carruthers provides for these
shrinkages and remissions of taxation and revenue :
Reduction of railway rates (mostly further
bonus to the “great natural resources”).. £150,000
Abolition of school fees 80,000
Penny postage .. 60,000
Remission of land-tax 40,000
Total £320,000
If there were a genuine surplus nothing could be said
against the proposed further reduction of railway rates.
It is true also that, after paying interest, the department
in the past year showed a surplus of £384,980. But con-
sider this
Deficit. Surplus.
1901 .. (accumulated) £1,234,153 ..
1902 .. 136,862 ..
1903 .. 521,961 ..
1904 .. 408,437 ..
1905 .. 35,079 ..
1906 .. £384,980
Net deficiency .. £1,951,512 ..
With a railway deficiency of that size—apart from what has
been charged up to capital—on the strength of a single
year’s abnormal figures, rates are to be reduced in the
interests of the class which already enjoys by far the
•lowest rates ! It maybe “Reform ’’finance ; but if a wild
rabbit or banana hawker financed that way, the Bank-
ruptcy Court would call him a fool, and suspend his certi-
ficate for two years.
* • * *
N.S.W. Treasury figures are still on the boom.
In two months the revenue has reached £1,950.023, or
.£297,246 more than it“ was in July and August, 1905.
JBiii, for all that, J. Carruthers has spent £231,217 of bor-
rowed money—at the rate of very nearly 1| millions a
.year. How much more the revenue would have to boom
before a “Reform” Treasurer would reckon the State
should pay its own way hasn’t yet transpired. In July
.and August, 1901, the revenue was £1,451,831 ; that is
.£498,192 less than Carruthers has to spend. And yet he
.must borrow £231,217 to make ends meet. Victoria’s
August revenue was £746,371, or £175,705 more than for
ithe same month of last year, and probably a record for the
State. Total for two months, £1,388,575 ; or £212,922
more than for July-August, 1905. Victoria, which has
had abundant rains all through, is safe for another extra-
good season. Queensland’s revenue for two months has
been £611,288 compared with £548,122 last year—£63,l66
better. Expenditure (excluding interest) for two months
was £363,328, or £13,350 more than in 1905. Tasmania
shows for the two months £135,941 of revenue, a slight
improvement on last year’s £134,358. Westralian figures
.are not available ; there is a great silence about Westra-
lian Treasury results in these days. And S. Australia
contents itself with quarterly figures.
* » * *
Issue of a circular this week by the Commercial
Bank of Australia partly explains the recent remarkable
rise in the bank’s ordinary shares. The Assets Trust has
£2,919,788 of deposits, carrying 3 percent, interest, and
payable in 1916. Present circular invites these deposit-
holders to offer their deposits, and announces “ The
lowest tenders to the extent of £300,000 will be accepted.”
It will be an easy enough matter to pay off the first
£300,000 ; at June 30 the bank had 54 per cent, of the
new debt to the public in liquid assets. It had £1,867,000
in cash and at short call. The use of some of this
unproductive money to get rid of money which is
costing 3 per cent, seems good business and if the
deposits can be got at a discount (present price is 155.),
it will be so much profit with which to write down
the old boom assets. But the vast deficit won’t be got
rid of by paying back these depositors ; the repayment
will simply alter the form of the debt. Also, the more
the bank improves, and the nearer it gets to 1916, the
less the profit it will be able to make out of repurchases.
•Granted that this scheme is good, in that it will save
some interest, and that the bank’s business, including
realisation of old securities, has lately been satisfactory,
the boom in ordinary shares looks nearly as absurd as it
•did last week (B. 6/9/’O6).
* * * *
The Sydney market for gilt-edged scrip
stiffened up considerably last week in consequence of
the fact that the lucky recipients of the big cheque
(£775,000), paid over the other day as the balance of the
purchase-money of Great Cobar mine, are not the sort of
people to play two-up with their money. They are
putting a lot of it where it can be found when wanted ;
one of the crowd bought £30,000 of Colonial Sugar shares.
» ■» * *
Sydney Hydraulic Power Co. has to share
profits over 10 per cent, with Sydney City Council. Five
years ago, the co. reckoned that it might as well make its
own engineeringprofits, and soformed the Engineering Co.
Last year, this paid 15 per cent, and put by more than
enough to pay another 5 per cent. Meantime, the parent
co. doesn’t make more than enough to pay the 10 per
Cent. —it doesn’t show enough to share anything with
anybody. Sydney City Council evidently thinks it smells
a rat, and has decided to take action to determine whether
it isn’t as much entitled to share in the excess profits of
the pup as in those of the parent co.

The co. which has the largest lay-by, and thus shows that

deal more eager to build reserves than this co, appears to
be. The co., in many respects, deserves well: it has
steadily written down its goodwill item to nothing ; and
practically all its assets are immediately available. In
fact, its one outstanding sin, from which it must itself
be the worst sufferer, is Wiat it compels the infant to pay
dividends before it has reached the walking stage.

The year’s trading (apart from property sales) shows up
as well as it does owing to £ll5O being realised in excess
of the amount to which certain Government debentures
had been written down. The house property sold had a
book value of £20,304 ; this realised £21,224. ' But vacant
land of the book value of £10,934 realised only £4BOO, There
remains in hand £69,921 (book value) of house property
and £49,632 of vacant land. But, in addition, there are
mortgages totalling £186,772, and some Of these may be
expected to fall into possession. Hbw much of that
represents house property ahd how much vacant land it
is impossible to tell; apparently, however, judging by
the steady reduction in the item “vacant land,” it
includes little of the latter. None at all, anyway, was
foreclosed upon last year; and that is a good sign.
As a result of the satisfactory sale of Government
debentures, 6 per cent, is to be paid to depositors
and debenture-holders for this current year. This
rate, however, is not likely to be maintained; for
the advances, properties, etc., constituting practically
all the assets, returned in the past year, on the average
amount held, only £3 11s. 9d. per cent, gross ; the pro-
perties in possession alone returned only £3 6s. per cent,
gross. The deposits and debentures outstanding amount
to £231,323 ; last year’s earnings would on this sum pay
£5 Is. per cent, gross; and repairs and rates, not to
mention costs of management, have to come out of that.
The paid capital of the Modern Building Society
is £198,897. To say how much of that will be left can be no
more than a wild guess at present—it depends upon how
the slow realisation of assets pans out; and that depends
upon the unknown conditions of the future. Here, how-
ever, are the results of 10 years’ realisations

From the £232,603 of liabilities deduct, say, £24,000,

has been done, as shown, at a cost of £353,184 of assets,
which means that in 10 years the assets have averaged
about 16s. lOd. in the £. If the remaining assets (includ-
ing premises) realise that much, they will bring in about
£325,000. This would wipe out the debt to the public,
and leave £92,397 for the shareholders.

mostly owned in Melbourne, and supplies water, &c., to

June, 1905.. 16,622 10 ~ ~ 10,000 5622 42,765
Dec., 1905.. 16,197 10 „ „ 10,000 6197 48,962
June, 1906.. 21,579 10 „ ~ 10,000 11,579 60,541
This co.’s lease has now 12 years to run ; at the end of
that time it has to hand over the undertaking to the
State, without receiving any payment. Steadily, there-
fore, it has been building up a “ contingency reserve
fund,” which now amounts to £53,500 ; also, there is
undivided profit of £28,856 and the share premiums
£3379 ; total of the three, £85,735. But on the other side
there is a paper asset, “water rights, leases, etc.,
£25,194,” and, in order to arrive at true reserves, this
latter amount must be deducted. And what is left are
not true reserves either ; because obviously a 12 years
leasehold of an undertaking isn’t worth as much as
the 28 years lease which the co. had originally.
The co. has now decided to pay back ss. of its
capital; and it has over £65,000 in deposits and
public stock to do it with. This will leave 15s.
to be paid off in 12 years. Approximately, after
paying back ss. per share, and wiping out debts owing to
public, the co. will have £44,000 of liquid assets that will
not have to be handed over. (About £lO,OOO of this is
doubtful.) The reduced capital will be £150,000 ; deduct
£44,000 = £106,000, which, at 4 per cent, for 12 years, will
call for a sinking fund of £7OOO a year. As the works will
not be affected, the profits will be very little reduced by
the reduction of capital; irrespective of interest, the pro-
fits have been £36,103 for the past year. Deducting
£7OOO sinking fund, and another £3500 for renewals, this
would show a return of 17 per cent, on this reduced capi-
tal. But there are risks : another drought may happen ;
the works may collapse (having only a 28 years lease, the
co. didn’t design them to last for ever); a metal slump
may knock most of the bottom out of Broken Hill. Yet,
even with these counted in, the Water Supply looks a
glittering thing compared with almost any other Barrier
“ investment ” at present prices.
The co. has offered to sell the concern to the
Broken Hill municipality for an annuity of £23,600 for 12
years, payment to be guaranteed by the State. This
would be equivalent to a present payment of about
£220,000, assuming the earnings of money at 4 per cent.
The co. presumably wouldn’t hand over its liquid assets,
say £35,000 after returning the ss. per share, which would
make a total of £255,000 for the property—exclusive, that
is, of the ss. to be paid back next month. This means
255. 6d. per 15s. share. In view of the things that may
happen between now and 1919, the shareholders are hot
likely to quarrel with the directors for asking too little.
What the municipality may say about it is another matter.

In the past 12 months £577 has been provided out of
profits to cover depreciation. The sad state into which
this society fell was owing to unfortunate dealings in
wheat; and it looks as though it will take some years to
recover. The capital provided (£48,368) is barely enough
for the business, and £20,078 of that is cumulative prefer-
ence ; so the deficit on capital account is represented by
overdraft, which during the year has grown from £11,120
to £17,662. Interest (including rent) has absorbed £lO7O
in the year. The extra inohey borrowed is represented
chiefly by stock. The turnover has been £496,998 ; in
view of that, the amount of sundry debtors (advances to
consignors, &e.), £32,795, is perhaps not excessive. Yet,
though credit appears to be fairly restricted, the society
has had to write off £1304 for bad debts duiirig the year.
Possibly the society, being a co-operative concern, is not
out after profits ; shareholders may get full return for
their money by having their business put through at
rock-bottom prices. The co-operator is certainly no
better off for getting £5 in dividend and bonus if he has
had to supply the £5 in commission. Whether that is
the situation here cannot, of course, be said from the
accounts. One thing can, however, be said : preference
capital is out of place in a co-operative society. It means
a perpetual mortgage on profits ; and the essence of co-
operation is that profits shall go to the co-operators.

The reserves are nominal to some extent, there being a

years stood lumped with others—“ Goodwill, office furni-

way, to have him here, where we can argue with him,
than to keep him in London or Antwerp, where he is
almost beyond arguing with.

* * * *
Sydney Exchange, which has lately promoted
numerous exhibitions of N.S. Wales’ “great natural
resources,” is now advertising the mineral wealth of
the State, partly by exhibits of ores, concentrates, &c.,
partly by placards and graphic illustrations. It isn’t the
Exchange’s fault, but, as it happens, probably no time
could be less opportune for the exhibition. The trouble
just now is, not to get the public to “ invest ” in mining,
but to W'arn them off it at present prices. Generally
speaking, mining shares reflect the metal-market quota-
tions ; and the metal markets are at w'hat looks like the
top of a boom. There is bound to be a reaction, with a
slump in share-values, and legitimate mining will suffer
from it for many years. And the higher the share-
values are forced, the more the real industry will suffer.
Mining would be most benefited now by a reminder
of past disasters that have happened through inciting the
public to rush in upon a boom basis ; instead of placards
recounting the successes, they need a warning of the
failures. In silver-lead alone, where, for instance, are
now the Webb’s, Webb’s Extended, White Rock, Bonanza,
Lady Brassey, Pinnacles, North Central, Rockwell, Day
Dream, Kanimbla, Lady Bevys, Lubra, Lewis Ponds,
Tom’s Lewis Ponds, Mt. Stewart, Bolivia, Overflow,
Treasure, Black Prince, Terrible Dick, Pioneer ? The
list might run to half-a-colunm. Every one of these was
boomed as practically a sure thing, and most of them
looked satisfactory enough on the basis of high metal
prices, With lead at £l2, copper at £45, and tin at £BO,
it is a fine thing to recall the successes ; with lead at
£lB 2s. 6d., copper at £B6, and tin at anything over £lBO,
the more people look at the failures the less sorry they
are likely to be. They may miss a few things : but they
will probably be all the better off for not swallowing even
good bait.
* * * *
Budgery has been driving Sydney off its head
this last week ; and £l5 shares—there are 100 in the
syndicate—jumped on Monday to £475 sales. The iron-
stone gossan capping on this property has often been
pointed to in this column as worth further probing ; but
it was hardly expected that the early results would be so
sensational. The property, which is 60 miles east at
Cobar, and three miles frcmi Hermidale railway station, on
the Cobar line, was worked some 20 years ago by Soren-
son and party for gold ; but as there wasn’t enough of the
metal, and no indications of copper, the usual abandon-
ment took place at 100 ft. Last year Sorenson, still
hankering after the old show, again stepped in and raised
£l5OO to work the property. Since then a new shaft has
been sunk to 216 ft., seams of carbonate of copper passing
out to the east at 186 ft. in the shaft. At 216 ft. deep a
south drive revealed nothing for the first 40ft., but at
that point an eastern crosscut (now extended to 34ft.)
passed into an intermittent ore-body, assaying from 8
per cent, to 49 per cent, from picked stuff ; and the face
now shows black oxides and glance ore of high values.
Lode is dipping east, and has a strike of 14 degrees west
of north ; and, on appearances, it will probably take 20ft,
more to reach the hanging wall. Up to date and down to
present level, Budgery is more than promising; and
there is no apparent reason why it shouldn’t live into rich
sulphides. But of its size and character much too little
is yet known to base a definite opinion upon.
4*4*
Budgery South, of 60 acres, immediately joinihg
Budgery, and Within 300 ft. of latter’s main shaft, passed
into the hands of a co. of 600 shares, of £lO ; 200 went to
vendors, and 400 to public ; terms, £1 on application and
£1 on allotment; balance, 10s. per share pef month.
Soutar and Hoare had barely floated the thing in Sydney
before £2 shares were rushed to £6. The men who owned
it (mainly, it is understood, Brookman and Matchett, twd
western men) know the country thoroughly, and have
followed every movement in Budgery ; moreover, they
are men who know a good deal about shares as well aS
sheep. And the Pitt-street speculator, whb was, probably
nevfer within 300 miles of the show, and knew nothing abofft
it, immediately valued it at three times the amount asked
by the promoters. Once in a thousand times that Sort
of folly may turn out all right; and this may be the once.
At present, anyhow, shares are at about £5 premium.
No doubt Budgery South is the best thing the public will
strike outside Budgery itself; for, though so far it is only
a position claim, it can hardly miss the lode to the south—
that is, if the lode exists to the south ; and, looking at
the body in the parent show, it is hardly likely that it
will peter out before it gets to the boundary.

Tin, £lB6. Which accounts for the 450 tin
ventures of one sort or another that are said to have
Sprung up in the New England district (N.S.W.). Also
tin ShoWs arfe being floated at EUfiowle and Waukeroo,
on the othet Side of the sattte State, Some fingers will
be burned ; for thete is no trickier metal—tricky in its
obciirrfenCe ; tricky in its market value. Ten years ago
the average price was £59 9S. lid. per ton. Eight years
befote that the avefdge Was £ll7 10s. Just 20 years ago
the market was rising just as steadily and persistently as
it has been dding lately; and there was the same
tin-mining boom. Between 1879 afid 1882 the N.S. W;
output jumped from £372,349 to £833,461. Two years
later it was less than half of that, and bj'
1898 it had got down to £60,600, most of which Was WOfi
by Chinese and fossickers making the most beggarly
living. Practically not a single new discovery of tin has
been made lately ; turn up the records of the 20-year-old
boom, and you find just the same names that you find to-
day. Certainly there are better appliances now ; but
they can’t be used universally ; and, generally speaking,
tin-saving has moved on very little. Everything hangs
on the market, just, as it has always hung hitherto ; and
the past history of the industry in N.S. V\ ales gives proof
enough of what reliance can be placed in the market.
* * * *
Junction North, Broken Hill, will be a greatly-
improved mine, on surface, at any rate, by the time the
shaft is repaired and hauling is resumed. Much money
has been spent on the mill, and if temporary manager
Weir’s ideas are worth anything (and the improved work
done by his own North mill show that they are), the pro-
duction of concentrates should be high both in quantity
and value. The inside of the mill has been practically
ripped out, repaired, altered, and replaced. The im-
provement is obvious. There is, of course, the risk of a
discrepancy between theory and practice, and the chance
of the new manager wanting things his own way. The
ticklish work of repairing the collapsed portion of the
shaft at the 570 ft. is being entered upon, and as many of
the terrors of the task have already disappeared, the
management is approaching the job with more confi-
dence.

With great ore-bodies developed at 900 ft., the
new mill at the Broken Hill South may be placed in the
contractor's hands at any time now ; the railway to the
site has already been started.
* * » *
Block 10, Broken Hill, is getting out a little
more ore, as men can now get back into some of the
upper-level stopes.
n # * *
“Bromide”: The close mullocking of the old
ead stopes has assisted, no doubt, to keep Block 13,
Broken Hill Prop, open, but the real thing, in my opinion,
is the square-set timbering of all the stopes above the
250 ft., coupled with the big support of a pillar on the
400 ft. The trouble in Block 13 is not so much back
weight, as side weight, and mullock alone has proved
incapable of resisting heavy side pressure. Luckily all
the shallow level stopes were in running ground and
square sets had to be used, and now they check any
movement. Since they have got out of the running
ground, and are working the big refractory sulphide lode
on the open stope and bulk system, any chance they
might have had to check a movement is gone, and all they
can do is to call the men out and wait until the ground
has settled. A stope is 14ft. high (average), and when it
is sufficiently advanced to permit of mullock being tipped
without interfering with the miners, they rear the chute
and ladder-road sets Bft., and mullock to the top of them,
but no higher. This leaves 6ft. from the mullock
to the back, or roof, which must be held
somehow ; so they stick “ bulks ” of timber on the newly-
tipped mullock. If a creep is on, these bulks are squeezed
down into this loose mullock, very often clean out of
sight, and the consequence is the ground can’t be stopped
until it hits something solid below. As proof, take two
recent creeps. In both cases the mine was moving bodily
from the bottom of the old lead stopes (or 250 ft.) down to
the 500 ft. level, but not above, where the square sets
were. From the 300 ft. down they couldn’t get near many
of the old mullock passes, and those they could get to
were too broken to be of much use, while above the 250 ft.
they were all in comparatively good order, and could
have been used straight away—pretty conclusive proof
that the movement had been from the 250 ft. down.
*.* * *
The Sulphide Corporation’s Central mine
emergency mill started off very well a few weeks ago,
but the bins soon ran short of ore, and of late only half
the plant has been at work. The sinking of the shaft to
the 1000 ft. is making good progress, and when that work
is completed plenty of ore should be available. The
heavy wood framework for the granulation plant which
is to treat Block 10 slimes is now throwing its shadow
across the old Extended shaft.
* » * *
The New White Leads, south of Broken Hill,
started diamond-drilling with a four-drill compressor and
a great flourish of trumpets. In 10 days they hadn’t
bored 30ft., but the drill men bad discovered many
things. One was that the air-compressor would only
drive the drill when the mine’s rock drills were idle. The
diamond drill is now, therefore, worked only night shift,
when it can have to itself all the air that is going.
* * * *
Some very fine carbonates have'been broken in
a rise over an old drive in the main shaft of the North
British Blocks. This appears to be a lode which was just
missed by the old Bonanza Blocks Company in driving
south. It has also every appearance of being the con-
tinuation of the carbonate shoot which in the old days
was so rich that even the most hardened liars could
scarcely exaggerate it.

reserved shares (to Lionel Robinson and crowd) for £3

* * * *
Who said that the people who have been
buying big blocks of Broken Hill shares are going to hold
them for 10 years for dividends? Or one year? The
amateur jobber has a habit of saying, “ These people
have inside information.” Whereupon he opens his
mouth and swallows the shares that the big “block”
purchasers have bought for the purpose of feeding to
him.
* » * *
In spite of what the diamond drills said, C.S.A.,
Cobar (N.S.W.), continues to develop high-grade ore
bodies, as it did in the upper zone. Since the general
market upheaval, when the public frantically rushed to
get in at £B, and afterwards tore its clothes in scrambling
to get out at 405., interest has subsided. But that hasn’t
affected the mine, from which 887 tons of ore have been
broken and treated at Cockle Creek for a net return of
£4166, after deducting all charges off the mine. Ex-
ploratory work still proceeds vigorously. In the old
shaft, from a point 33ft. in the east crosscut (where most
of the marketed ore has been mined), a north drive is
now in 200 ft., and the face is in good ore. Then, 90ft.
further east in the crosscut, the face of the ore body
shows an average of 52 per cent, lead and 2dwt. gold.
The new main shaft has been sunk to 216 ft. ; it has
passed through gossan, carrying gold ; but until connec-
tions have been made with the old shaft, and more ex-
ploratory work done, no estimate can be given of
values in the western lode ; and there is yet about 206 ft.
of sinking and 110 ft. of crosscutting to be done before
connections can be made at present working level.
Mine’s policy (in view of being continually jambed in
getting the dirt away from the old shaft) will probably
include connections to facilitate hauling. Still, there
is quite enough high-grade ore easily available to keep
the pot boiling ; and, in the circumstances, there should
not be any room for mistakes to creep into the policy as
to the mine’s general equipment. Ac present, water
jackets look inevitable ; but the main thing is to hustle
and put into the shareholders’ pockets the profits that
are now going to the railway and to Cockle Creek.
* * * *
“The directors of the Commonwealth Oil Cor-
poration have decided to abandon the tunnel which was
being driven through the Capertee Mountains to Wolgan
Valley (N.5.W.).” Pop goes about £25,000 ; and a great
ouantity of plant, &c., will also have to be shifted. Tiie
Bulletin begins to see why this co. needed £400,000 of
capital; but this first experience doesn’t make it any
plainer how interest is going to be earned on the money.
Possibly the co. will grow mushrooms in its abandoned
tunnels.
* * * *
If the Tasmanian tin boom was not a matter of
every day regret, it would be necessary to give a word
of caution over the market which is being made for
Tingha tin shows in Melbourne. Sydney folk know
Tingha, both lode and stream ; so they should not require
advice. The present Melbourne movement is the pro-
duct of a Melbourne-Castlemaine combination of hydraulic
sluicers. One advantage the Tasmanian shows had was
a heavy rainfall for a good part of the year ; but they suffer
from the want of water during the summer. What will
be the state of the sluicers up Tingha way in an average
dry season? There is no Coliban water supply to draw on
like there is at Castlemaine. Hence a market made
while the water supply is good must be looked at very
critically. Pearson’s group of mines have gone to London
to the Coleman crowd that shook up Lyell Blocks, and,
if bought, the “ shop ” influence may help those who buy
shares for a market. What is not forthcoming so far
from the majority of promoters is data showing the
extent of the tin-bearing wash, its length, breadth and
depth, or any statement about the water supply.
* * # #
Wamboyne alluvial gold-find, near Wyalong
(N.S.W.), has not turned out the success anticipated.
Prospector Harry Nelson, from the first, was not satisfied
that the prospects obtained (from Jgr. to a grain to the
dish) warranted the hoisting of the red flag. The biggest
clatter was made by applicants for permits, and the
Warden was kept going for some time in marking out
areas, most of which are now practically idle. Which
once more goes to show that a provision should be
inserted in the new Mining Bill compelling applicants to
either apply for a lease or pay a substantial deposit before
the Crown is put to the expense of marking out areas on
private property.

At Mt. David (N.S.W.), “excellent stone” is
being stoped below the 300 ft. level, north-west drive, and
south-east the stone is “ 3ft. thick, and of high-grade
quality'.” Why be so definite about the size of the reef?
Wouldn’t “ enormous” do—just to keep the “ excellent”
and the “high-grade” company? It was announced a
week previously that samples had been sent to Sydney.
Did the assayer just report them “excellent” and
“high-grade”? Same mine reports, later, 400 tons for
1670 z., with sdwt. in the tailings.
The W.A. gold yield for August is 156,9100 z.,
which compares with 174,681 oz. for August, 1905, and
168,4320 z. for August, 1904. The total for the first eight
months of the year is 1,211,6330 z., against 1,306,0560 z. for
the same period of 1905, and 1,313,7660 z. for 1904. The
shortage, as compared with 1905, is therefore 94,4230 z.,
and, with 1904, is 102,1330 z., a very' serious falling off. So
far as one can see the decline is likely to continue, and
the final deficit is likely to be very nearly 150,0000 z.
W. J. Loring, head of Bewick, Moreing and
Co. in Australia, has, after six months spent in Mel-
bourne, done a tour of the W.A. mines, and says he is
quite satisfied with their position and prospects. The
grade of ore treated, and to be treated, is lower, but so
many economies have been introduced that profits can
be maintained for a long time to come, though, in most
cases, at a lower level. According to Loring', who is
admittedly' the pioneer of cheap mining in W.A., the
Oroya-Brown Hill is looking better than ever in its
history, in spite of the tons of gold it has produced. In
July last year the value of the ore was 117 s. 4d. per ton,
and the year’s profit £39,200; in July of this year the
year’s profit was £39,180, or practically the same,
although the grade of ore had fallen from 117 s. 4d. to
98s. 9d., or by 18s. 7d. per ton. The average grade of ore in
the chief mines dealt in on the market has fallen, during
the past twelve months, from 12dwt. to lOdwt. per ton,
and, so far as one can see, the grade will be still lower:
but the quantity of reserves is being largely increased by
the inclusion of ore which was discarded as worthless in
the days of Johnnie managers and antediluvian treat-
ment plants.
* * • *
Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, is opening out satisfac-
torily at 1373 ft. For a long time this level looked like a
duffer, and a lot of drivmgwas done in barren country.
Diamond drilling then showed that the real ore channel
was 60ft. east, and since then 350 ft. of driving has been
done in pay ore. The face of the south drive is worth
455., but the drive shows only 16s. ore, the country being
disturbed. Comparing July, 1905, with July, 1906, the
grade of ore has only dropped from 525. sd. to 50s. 4d.,
and the monthly profit from £23,500 to £21,000. Mining
costs (including everything, even div.-tax) come out at
about 265. per ton, which shows what can be done with
economical management.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie Amalgamated is to be reconstructed
in 600,000 shares of 2s. The co. includes in its belongings
a litter of wildcats. Holders of scrip in old co. are asked
to put up 2s. per share, and get share for share now held,
as well as a bonus of one share for every two shares
applied for. This means 4s. for three shares, or Is. 4d.
per share. This is anything but a tempting proposition ;
people with tenners to put into a gamble could get things
more quick and lively, such as Whitford’s Reward, which
has some possibilities. Money sunk in Amalgamated
will not be realisable—judging by the fact that for years
past the old scrip has not been negotiable.
■X i r * *
Boulder Main Reef, Kalgoorlie, is likely to
resume crushing in October. Since Manager Wauchope
took charge the mine has been re-sampled, and work
systematised. The treatment plant is being overhauled
and brought up to date, and is expected to treat 2000
tons monthly at a much reduced cost. Formerly the
plant treated 1500 tons monthly and the expenses were
more than the mine could stand. Ore reserves, on July 1,
were estimated at 20,000 tons, worth 345., or enough to
keep the mill going for ten months. Wauchope showed
his capacity for discovering gold when working the
alleged played-out Lady Shenton on tribute, as he rooted
out a lot of pay ore, which had been missed. Under his
management the Main Reef should give a fair account of
itself.
• * * *
Idaho, Kalgoorlie, situated east of Boulder
Deep Levels, has treated 37 tons for 380 oz. Total from
the mine, 22720 z. from 470 tons. Property is held by four
working miners, who pick out the stone from cross
leaders, which intersect the main lode, and are very
rich. At 130 ft. the main lode is 15ft. wide, and worth
6dwt. to Bdwt. ; and though it is of no value to pros-
pectors, some day it may be taken in hand by a co., and
worked on the big scale it deserves.
* * * *
Oroya-Black Range (W.A.) is doing very little
development work at present, owing, it is said, to the
reef pinching to 4in. only at the deepest point cut. At
110 ft. about 1000 ft. has been driven on ore ranging from
2£ft. to 7ft. in width, and worth about 80s. A good deal
of prospecting has also been done at 175 ft. Down to this
depth the underlay of the reef is about two co one, but
it seems to have become still flatter with depth. At
175 ft. the reef is 100 ft. west of the shaft, but came into
the shaft at 205 ft., so it had flattened to about three to one.
When the co. was floated, shareholders were told that
the co. would have the reef to a vertical depth of
2000 ft. ; but this flattening of the dip will materially alter
this. The deep levels are being floated in Sydney as the
Sandstone Development Go., in 250,000 ss. shares. It is
an open question whether the deeper development of the
Oroya-Black Range has been suspended because of the
pinching, or because of the flattening, of the reef. If the
pinch is permanent, the deep levels are worthless. If
merely temporary, and the flattening is permanent, then
the Oroya will lose the reef in any case, and the Sydney
co. will get it.
* * * *
Great Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), has been
looking rather better of late. The winze sunk at 580 ft.
north in the north drive at No. 12 level, showed the lode
to be improving in depth. At 200 ft. the ore was 72in.
wide and worth 565. per ton. This depth corresponds
with the thirteenth level. In driving south to connect
with the main drive from the shaft, the lode is 60in. wide,
worth 325. 6d. per ton. The north drive from the bottom
of the winze is in ore 64in. wide, worth 38s. per ton.
These values are not gaudy; but, then, the first 200 ft.
driven north from the shaft did not exceed Bs. per ton,
which caused the recent deep depression in the scrip.
Mine’s costs are now only about 225. per ton, so pretty
low grade ore pays.
* ■* * *
Underwood, who succeeded Isdell as member
for Pilbarra goldfields in Westralian Assembly, was, until
his election, a working miner, and had the good luck to
get an interest in the Castlemaine—a remarkably rich,
but patchy, show near Nullagine. Three tons gave 9000 z.,
but the ore is limited. The biggest nuggets found ■ in
Westralia have come from this Pilbarra district. One
piece, found by Archie Clyde, weighed 4750 z., and, on the
strength of his rise, he went away for a spell. In his
absence another old battler unearthed 4000 z., and when
Clyde returned he started work in the same locality.
Four or five days afterwards he dropped on 1300 z., and
subsequently got several smaller pieces. The name of
Pantomime Gully was given to an alluvial patch in which
a 500 oz. nugget was rooted up —the prospectors who
found it had such a high time that the camp was turned
upside down for a month. Good finds of gold and copper
have now been reported at Roebourne, on the coastal
fringe of the Pilbarra fields. The copper deposits were
worked years ago, but only exceptionally rich ore was
taken. Systematic prospecting has been going on re-
cently, and several well-defined lodes have been located.
A big conglomerate body, 20ft. wide, carrying in places
over 2oz. gold, has also been found in the Roebourne dis-
trict by R. B. Pettigrew, who was sent up from Kalgoorlie
to see what the place was like.
* * » *
Cumberland, Norseman (W. A.), appears to be
in a precarious condition, judging by the latest progress
report; the reef in the bottom level is of no value what-
ever. In no other part is anything better than 7dwt. ore
exposed, and values range down to Idwt. As no pay ore
is being developed, the future does not appear hopeful.
On January 1 ore reserves were given as 11,300 tons,
worth 85s/per ton, or say about 18 months’ supply.
During July 694 tons, worth 745, per ton, were treated at
a cost of 645., leaving a profit of 10s. per ton only. It is
interesting to note that the 232 ft. of development in
worthless ore cost 725. 2d. per foot, while upkeep of plant
cost £356, or 10s. 2d. per ton treated, as against 10s. lOd.
for milling and cyaniding. This does not appear to be a
very satisfactory state of affairs, and requires looking
into.

Another Westralian wild cat got loose the other
dayatWagin, on the Perth-Albany line. Specimens of
phenomenal richness were shown, but the Government
Geologist unhesitatingly declared that there was no
stone like them within a hundred miles of Wagin. There
was a big reef, but traces of gold were few. Notwith-
standing this report somebody had the gall to publish in
a Kalgoorlie paper that four parcels of stone from Wagin
were assayed by the School of Mines, and that the
results were Surface, full width of reef, 9dwt. per
ton ; a few feet under, 15dwt. ; 10ft., the deepest, loz.
15dwt. 7gr. ; lode matter, 3dwt. The director of the
school promptly contradicted the statement and added
“ The only Wagin samples assayed and reported upon by
the Kalgoorlie School of Mines were on August 16, as
follows :—No. 1, lOgr. per ton ; No. 2,15 gr. per ton ; No.
3,12 gr. per ton ; No. 4, lOgr. per ton.” And so the cat
died.
• • * i
The A.M. A. of Victoria has been investigating
the conditions of the contractor underground in the
mines of Bendigo and Ballarat. And it was time. Ben-
digo is one of the most flourishing mining fields in the
Commonwealth, but some of its directors remind one of
Quilp. Else why should they begrudge the tributor (or
contractor) half pay for dead work ? Why should there be
any objection to giving a copy of the contract and making
it binding for 12 months ? Why should there be objection
to an appeal to an impartial tribunal in case of dispute ?
Grabbing gold in the concentrates, also copper-plate gold,
is not unusual, though such gold comes directly
from stone broken under tribute agreement. In
Ballarat the system is still worse, the poorness of the
field helping the sweater. Here is a case : Men were paid
only ss. a foot for driving a main level, which was worth
£2 ss. per foot; 4s. lid. per ton was deducted for cartage,
and then out of the residuum half was taken as royalty.
In some cases tributors have to take an inferior explosive,
and that at a price above the retail price. Why? Ask
the directors. There is a howl when a miner pockets
a specimen ; and nobody is saying there shouldn’t be.
But"what about this? “Men are working at starvation
rates, and through force of circumstances, are little short
of slaves,” miners’ committee says. In the Transvaal
the Randlords have at least to give niggers and yellow
Johns good food, clean quarters, and regular pay. In
Victoria the contracting system is so worked that
cases have occurred where miners have had only 4s.
to divide among eight men for a week’s work.
The most liberal owner buttons up if 30s. per week per
man is earned. Is that enough to pay rent and keep a
family on ? Better that the mines should be shut up than
that destitution of the sort should continue. Minister
McLeod’s hatchet only lopped off a branch when he
tackled the lease owner who lets his claim on tribute to a
co. He wants to smash up the tributing cos. which now
play the part of the sweater. And there is a long list of
them in Victoria.
• * * •
Forest Creek Gold Reefs, of Castlemaine (Vic.),
has partially closed, after a long struggle against adverse
fortune. Year 1905 saw such a change in its returns that
it looked as if co. might give the lie to recent quartz
mining results at Castlemaine. Claim since then has
joined the great majority of duffers that represent the deep
mining industry of that district. In 1904 the co.’s gold
was worth £14,138, and last year it about reached £20,000.
This little run of good fortune was applied to reducing
the debenture debt. Mine is on the Quartz Hill line,
which has cost local folk a few odd shillings at different
times. The formations are big, but the gold is low.
Much of the quartz is as white as snow, and would be sent
by most to the mullock tip. It, however, carries odd
splashes of gold, and so everything has to be crushed.
Co. has treated between 40,000 and 50,000 tons, averaging
about 4Adwt. This shows how close to the margin the
manager has had to go. Claim is now to be handed over
to the tributor ; and good luck to him ! Francis Ormond,
another of the district’s mines, has put through about
40,000 tons for under 90000 z., but it has paid a div. or
two, while the Forest Reefs has never reached that
dignity.
* * * *
Lyell Blocks (Tas.) directors recently got their
fees doubled. It was a sort of final peck. Out of the
300,000 shares in the co., probably only one-twentieth are
now owned in Australia. The Board, therefore, is only
making the most of its opportunity by getting as much
as it can before some Bull agitator moves for the transfer
of the whole concern to London. Two causes helped to
lift Lyell Blocks shares out of the mire. One was the
advance in the price of metals, the other the advent of a
smart miner named Ferguson, who was able to realise the
mistakes of his predecessors, and take full advantage of the
dead work done by them. Even these factors would never
have given the stock a value like it has reached had not
a London “ bucket-shop ” floated out the shares on a Bull
market. How little the mine can do with its low-grade
clays is proved by the dividend list, only 3d. having come
after the years of waiting since W. R. Wilson took the
claim in hand; and what Australians think of the
market is illustrated by their wholesale quittance
of scrip. Floods almost brought disaster to the
mine during the past winter, and now there js
something worse—a shortage in ore. On December 31
lastco. had 90,000 tons in sight; to-day that total has
dwindled down to 40,000 tons. With production going
on at the rate of 6000 tons a month, there is nothing like
the value of the co.’s shares in sight. The co. has got
into this plight through the failure of exploratory work
to open up a fair-sized body of copper bearing clays off
the Consols Tunnel.

* * * *
Gympie’s Two-mile venture has been floated,
mostly in Sydney; so that unprospected end of the field
will get a trial at last. The new co.’s funds are small;
but if the same energy that has been put into the raising
of the money is put into the work of sinking, a little will
go a long way. And, in view of the possibility of opening
up practically a new field, the Government seems dis-
posed to help. Gympie certainly is in almost desperate
need of a lift. S. Great Eastern No. 2, which ran up to
£4 10s. in its specimen days, year before last, stands
unsteadily at about Its., while the co., like a good many
others at Gympie, waits for its neighbors to do the neces-
sary prospecting. *
Smith’s Creek (N.Q.) tin shareholders have
faith in Captain Warren, who is sticking to the tough
job of pulling the concern out of the mud, and so put up
more money to buy machinery. The ore from the lower
levels, down to 300 ft., is sulphide, and not oxide of tin,
as the report sometimes said it was ; and that is the
reason for the Merton furnace to roasfthe ore, and addi-
tional machinery to concentrate it. Another lode was
discovered about 600 ft. from the original mine, and a
trial crushing has been put through the mill. Smith’s
Creek lodes are poor, and it is a struggle to make them
pay ; but it is not every tin lode that carries such a big
body of ore down to 300 ft. This co. should be able to
secure an option over some of the big low-grade proposi-
tions in the neighborhood. Things are slack in the dis-
trict from a labor point of view ; the dry season partly
accounts for that.
» * * *
The battery at Reid’s Creek (N.Q.) is being put
up by the Irvinebank Co., whose chief is John Moffatt.
The crowd that can float tin mines and then put up a
plant to crush the ore, is doing good business. There is
no danger of the mill being short of ore; J. Moffatt and
the Irvinebank crowd own the Adelaide mine, south of
Dalziell’s, which could keep it going. The Adelaide
has a wide and continuous run of low-grade ore over "
300 ft. long. Another mine to the north of Dalziell’s (the
Village Blacksmith) has a shaft down 100 ft. on a strong
and payable tin lode. The field has a permanent look ;
the market is the unknown quantity.
* * * *
Charters Towers market, which has been life-
less for 18 months, shows signs of moving. Brilliant
Central and East Mexican have benefited most so far.
There have been no mine developments of any conse-
quence ; in fact. East Mexicans moved along solely on
the decision of the directors to put in a crosscut at 600 ft.
to meet Mills’ United reef. The mine-gambling fever has,
however, bitten Australia again ; and the Towers is
making' its claim to a share of the plunder. •
* * * * ■
The New Era, near Watsonville (N.Q.), is the
lowest-grade payable tin mine in Australia. In five
months, crushing stuff averaging only '64 per cent, black
tin, or about '45 per cent, white metal, a profit of £llOO
has been made out of 4SOO tons. The venture began on a
capital of £1250, but the profit has all been put back into
plant. The mine is mostly owned by the men who are at
work on it. The little syndicate is now tackling the old
Baker mine, worked with very good results 20 years ago.
* * * *
“Quidam”: The world is suffering from a
scarcity of aluminium, in spite of the fact that the metal,
in its raw state, is in every clay bed. The Pittsburg
Reduction Co., which controls the manufacture in
America, has placed all it can turn out for the season,
and is behind in deliveries. As aluminium is taking the
place of other metals for many industrial purposes, people
behind the proposed Barron Falls (Q.) electric smelters
might do worse than think over the local possibilities of
aluminium smelting.
* * * *
Stevens’ “Copper Handbook” gives a list of
big mines ; six of the largest—the Boston and Montana,
Anaconda, Rio Tinto, Calumet and Hecla, Green’s
(Mexico), and the Copper Queen —unitedly produce
200,000 tons of copper per annum. Anaconda concen-
trates enormous quantities of low-grade ore to produce
40,000 tons of copper per annum. Average yearly
increase of the world’s copper is 42,000 tons, which is
about as much as the Boston-Montan a can produce.
Copper production, at present rate of increase, would
about double itself in 15 years ; but some of the big mines
which are forcing production will be played out long
before then. The most interesting thing is the decline in
the value of ore handled by nearly all big copper mines ;
it shows there is a strain on ore reserves to produce so
much copper. Save under exceptional circumstances,
the decrease in the value of the ore means a higher cost
of producing copper.. .

That regrettable result of imported industry,
the convict ship Success, is still pervading the fringe of
the Cold Country and justifying its name in its old age.
At the annual meeting of shareholders in Melbourne,
lately, the directors reported that the blessed vessel was
camping around Bristol, Cardiff and the villages adjacent,
and that the inhabitants were still parting up freely to
gape open-mouthed at the horrors of the “System.”
Last year the sum of £439 had been spent in docking and
outside repairs—nothing was said about inside repairs, or
whether any new thumbscrews or triangles had been
added. A dividend of Is. per share, absorbing £5OO, was
declared.
* * * *
Representatives of the Marconi Wireless Tele-
graph Co., of Canada, are just now pervading the Com-
monwealth, placing a number of shares—capital,
£1,000,000, shares to be fully paid upon application.
The co. has got a practical monopoly of Britain’s future
wireless business without any consideration ; it has also
exclusive traffic arrangements with the Canadian Govern-
ment, the Western Union, and C.-P. systems. It owns all
patents issued or hereafter to be issued in Canada to
Marconi. Its methods are much those of the Eastern
Extension Co., so the spectacle may presently be wit-
nessed of a large, voracious young shark swallowing an
equally voracious, but debilitated, old shark. The Mar-
coni Co. says it can put up and fit a station complete for
£3OO, and after that the only expense is the maintenance
of the building and machines and the operators’ wages.
It promises to send messages across the Atlantic next
November for the general public at 6d. a word—about a
quarter of the present cost. But it is only three years
since its system was worked up to practicability ; the
next ten years may see it superseded by something even
more simple and sudden.
The Aerial Tramways now being erected at the
British and Central Mines at Broken Hill on the Bleichert
system, will enable mine managers of Australia to learn
the saving- in transport charges of ore from mine to mill,
or mill to railway with concentrates. Many mines pay
from Is. 6d. upwards for horse and dray, whereas they
could do the same work at about one penny per ton by
means of a proper Automatic Ropeway. W. and J.
Lempriere, of 506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne, are the
Agents for A. Bleichert & Co., of 76 Cheapside, London.*
* * * *
The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has only one working part—the hammer—-which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. In some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2£in. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

Aboriginalities.
‘ Hie Prooshan ” : The generality of bush
dwellings are built on blocks. These piles run
from three feet in front, back to nothing, or flush
with the ground. The space between the floor
and earth remains a gaping void till father gets
those “slabs down from the mill,” and that’s a
deuce of a time. The rowdiest camp a man can
reasonably expect to find is in such a house on a
wet wintry night when lien, the black farrow pig,
Sandy, another of the same persuasion, and old
Gip, the black sow, crowd in under the house for
a dry camp. As soon as Sandy and the sow are
cosy, old lien walks over the sow and “scrooges”
down in the middle, for centralization means
warmth. There is an awful grunting and roaring
and angry protest for about 50 seconds. Then
conies silence, till one of the outsiders, feeling it
chilly, does the climb and scrooge into the middle,
and there arises on the stilly night a wild “O-
ware-aare-aare,” and much sub-floor chaos. Then
Ben says “ lie can’t sleep,” and starts rubbing
against one of the piles; and, later, the fleas
charge up through the cracks in the floor with
fixed bayonets, and give you a lively time. It is
on such nights that a man swears off eating pig,
pork, ham, or any of the progeny of the hog.
* * « *
“ Nero ” : A great deal has been written
of late in the metropolitan papers eulogising the
magnanimity and large-heartedness of “West
o’Darling” people, but, for down-right, low-down,
contemptible meanness, commend me to some of
the station-holders out there. Boundary-riders, or
at least “pannikin overseers” (who are a size
larger and a shilling cheaper than the genuine
article), are now supplied with strychnine, to
destroy dingoes in their spare time and as a
recreation on Sundays. The dogs so destroyed
must be skinned by the boundary-rider, and the
skin delivered to the head-station without delay.
The boss of the station hands the skin in to the
Stock Board for the district, and receives 10s. for
it. The boundary-rider gets nothing for his work,
but he sometimes gets the sack when he is sus-
pected of being too tired to skin a dingo, whereby
the boss has dark suspicions that he lias lost 10s.

flaring red coat on, too?"
“Veritas ” : For three or four years I was
an eye-and-ear witness of much of the prison life
on Norfolk Island during the regime of John
Price. It is quite an erroneous idea that only
the worst types of criminals were sent there. My
father (one of the two doctors in charge at the
time) had in his employ a man called John. John
had no legs, both having been amputated just
above the knees (had broken them. I have heard
him say, by jumping from an omnibus, or some-
thing of the sort, in an attempt to escape justice).
Justice overtook him, promptly cut off both legs,
and sent what was left of him to the Island. He
was an amazingly chirpy specimen of misfortune,
with a queer stock-in-trade of talents, was a first-
class “laundress,” could make dresses, stitch
shirt-fronts, had a fondness for verse, and last, but
not least, could ride at full gallop. It was ludi-
crously pathetic to see him hobbling about the
house on the two poor stumps that served him
as carriers. He was a highly-esteemed little
“citizen,” being even somewhat of a favorite
with the other John. I never heard of him
having been in irons, nor of making any
further attempt to escape justice by bolting. He
died, I think, in Hobart.
AN ULTIMATUM.
IMPASSIONED MOTORIST :
“ Muriel, if you refuse
to love me I swear to do
something rash and desperate.”
Some time ago there was a “ Socialist
Ball ”at Catherine Hill Bay (N.S. W .)• A couple
of nights after some one asked Bullocky Bill, who
was M.C., “ Why Socialist ? ” “ Well,” said Bill,
“ it’s this way. These fellows down at the
Bay, as have graft, find the brass for the
hall, tucker, and music ; and us fellows, as are
out of work, go down on their invite, and have
the——fun free, gratis, and for nothin’. That’s
a Socialist ball.”

The Squeaker met Johnny Dunnomutch,
th’uther day. He said : “Jus’ come back from
Sydney, fiorsekick me, but I had a good time.
The girls uster all say ‘ Good night ’ ter me when
I was goin’ back to me pub up King-street ev a
night. Went to the mooseem a good bit. Wot
took me fancy there was the weighing machines.
Yer had to pass thro’ them goin’ in an’ cornin’ out.
There was notices up ‘Way in’ and ‘Way out.’
I arst the bloke once wot I weighed, but be only
larfed. They can tell that way whether you’ve
pinched any ev the coorios. Cos you’d weigh a
bit extry when yer cornin’ out. Them blokes is
clever.”

Num A few months ago I was travel-
ling through Wyoming (U.S.A.) on the back
track to Australia. At Cheyenne a cow-boy
entered. He was the real thing—wearing spurs
six inches long, and carrying more armament
than the whole Commonwealth forces. While I
was wondering whether I ought to call him
“ pard,” he caught sight of The Bulletin I had
dropped on the seat. “ Say,” he remarked, in a
dialect that seemed strangely familiar, “If yer
done with that Bulletin, I’d be glad if ye’d let’s
have a look at it.” Astonished, at his request, I
asked “ Have you ever seen the paper before ? ”
Deadwood Dick laughed. “Took it fur years
when I was home,” he said. “I’m from Benalla,
on the north-eastern line, in Victoria. Ha’ yer
ever bin there ? ” Then we talked. I firmly
1 dieve that when they discover the North Pole
some Australian will glance round the corner of
an iceberg, and ask who won the Squires-O’Brien
light.

“ Quidam ” : In view of the way in which
the fur supply of the world is being cut out, and
the ever advancing price of furs, also considering
the plentiful supply of eucalyptus leaf diet lying
around everywhere, and the fact that the ’p Citium
is an animal that gets along very well in captivity,
there might be worse things in the future than
running a ’possum-farm. Some of the elevated
portions of south-eastern Australia, where nothing
grows so luxuriously as gum-leaves, would be
elegant places to develop fine furs at a minimum
cost.

“ J.L.C.” : We went with the newchum
to the pub ; the question of shouting was dis-
cussed, and someone suggested drawing from the
hat to settle the shout. The barman numbered
seven tickets, which were folded, placed in the
hat, and gravely shaken. We all drew in turn,
grinned complacently, and tore up our tickets.
The newchum drew—blissfully unconscious that
the tickets had all been numbered 7 by the un-
scrupulous barman—and shouted. The newchum
grew suspicious as the township became benignly
hilarious at his expense, and finally insisted on
numbering the tickets himself. He numbered
them from 1 to 7, and the Fates were facetious —
he drew 7 again.
“ Kar,” from India :—-
Have just seen a full-length photo, of the late Lady
Curzon iri her famous peacock-feather gown. This
recalls the native (and, to some extent, European) super-
stition that peacock-feathers are very unlucky. Lady
Curzon, at all events, had one affliction after another
after, she wore it. Father’s death, aunt’s death, hus-
band’s illness, her own, husband’s resignation of the Vice-
royalty, the vicious attacks upon him by the scurrilous
native press, and, finally, her own untimely end. All
this will greatly strengthen the silly superstition.

“ F.H.R.” : The bushman who follows up
dancing needs to be versatile. In no two corners
of this continent do they “darnce” the “shot-
teese,” “ varsoove,” or even the “fust set” the
same way. A veteran generally stands
off for a dance or two, to note the
predominating styles before “buck-
ing in. ” In some places you grasp
the maiden round the waist with
the right arm, while the left
hand, clutching firmly her
“lump er meat” (hand) is
drawn back to your hip. In
other places you are allowed
to throw both arms round her
neck, but you mustn’t do that
to an engaged girl or you’ll
find her an enraged girl. Also,
her “man” may be on hand,
looking for gore. Was at a
country dance once where the
chief attraction was the raffling
of a fat pig. The pig was in a
sty, amongst some thick scrub,
50 yards from the house, and
the number of couples that
didn’t visit that pig was small.
He must have come in for a
good deal of criticism, too, for
some of them lost a lot of
dancing while on the inspec-
tion. I was up in a “ fust set,”
and, noticing that the other
gentlemen didn’t bother to take
their arms from round their part-
ners’ waists between figures, I
tried it myself successfully. Was
getting on fine, and was just about
to suggest a visit of inspection to that
pig, when a burly, unshaven, scowling
face peered through the door of a bed-
room and drew my attention. “Who’s
that?” I asked. “That’s the boss,” she
whispered, “my husband.” You’ve seen the
arm of a railway signal drop sharp and sudden—
well, that’s how mine dropped. Something in his
face reminded me of private sec. Wallington.
Better not, it said, and I didn’t get a look at that
Pig-

“Jimmie P.” : Between Binda (in-back
from Crook well, which is behind Goulburn) and
Bigga there are, just now, millions of wattles in
full bloom. I never saw such a magnificent dis-
play of wattle-gold. It would pay lovers of
Australian bush beauty to drive from Crookwell
to Bigga. Many Australians do not even faintly
realise what an exquisitely beautiful flowering
tree the black wattle is. If it were an imported
article, they’d rave about it and pay a big price for
saplings.
“ Benicia ” : Re a close season for wild-
bees (“D.H.R.,” 9/8/’O6), such certainly isn’t
needed in Maoriland. Wild honey over here is-
very much a gamble. The insect has a habit of
extracting, from poisonous native flowers, a honey
which, though harmless to the bee, is a pretty
strong proposition for anything on two legs to-
tackle. It gives you a creepy, crawly, want-to-
get-off-the-earth sort of feeling, accompanied by
violent retching, and if the honey happens to be-
over-proof there’s a funeral in about three days.
Any nests in the neighborhood of poisonous
flowers are as secure from Maori depredation as if
they were tapu.

(29/8/’O6) snow fell over an area of 100 miles square.
That usually arid district is now a paradise of
green, variegated in places by carpets of wild
flowers acres in extent.

“L.E.”: I wish to nominate a certain
Vic. squatter for the mean-man championship. A
new rouser was feeding the pigs. The squatter
was looking on. One hog made off with the tin,
and in its hurry got its head stuck. As it dashed)
wildly round the scenery, with the rouser in pur-
suit, the squatter laughed till ho was all creases.
Noting the boss in this glad mood, the rouser
started to laugh, too, to make himself good. The
creases were straightened out suddenly. Fat
sacked that rouser on the spot. “ What right
have you to laugh,” he screamed, “in my time?”
* * * •
“ Quan ” : Love of bright colors is a re-
cognised characteristic of our black brothers.
Brunettes also exhibit this trait, but in none is it
so pronounced as in the Hindu, who is a truly
gorgeous creature. Recently, at Murwillumbah
(N.S.W.), I saw one who must have got the
notion into his head that he was a rainbow. His
t.urban was dark green, and was tipped with a
light green tassel. His neck was encircled by two
handkerchiefs of. blue and pink respectively. He
wore a football guernsey of alternate red and
black two-inch stripes, and beneath it a navy-blue
sweater, the tail of which showed below the tail
of the guernsey. Round his wrist he wore a
bracelet of small tassels of the kind used in fring-
ing bamboo suites. His coat was of a fawn color,
and his boots were tan. The only gloomy spot
upon this bird of Paradise was his pants, which
matched his complexion.
* * * *
“ Vigilans ” : The Northern Territory is a
great field for ornithologists. Amongst the many
beautiful birds is a miniature kookaburra. His
hilarious nibs is a fac-simile of the jackass of the
South, except that he has a finer plumage. His
habits are similar; he is the last to go to loost,
and the first to rise. He laughs in a minor key,
and is about half the size of the southern bird.
His home is on the rivers and lagoons, and he
apparently lives on fish. I camped with a noted
bird-catcher, a Frenchman who had been 20 years
in the North, except for sundry trips to Europe.
A man of 65 years ; £2OOO in the bank, with pro-
perty besides; nearly blind, and filthily dirty.
Ho makes trips solus with an old cart, two wrecks
of horses, and a mongrel of a dog. between
Palmerston and Wyndham, and from Wyndham
to Derby and Broome (W.A.).
DEPARTED.
TOM : “ Whatsh raizsh y' 'at for, Bill? ”

“Groper”: Let me defend “Wurrym”
against “Pylock.” “Wurrym” may not have
seen the well be wrote about, but I have. It is
35 miles east of Wagin, and it is on a granite rock
on the side of a hill. It flows regularly all night.
The water, which has been conserved somewhere
behind the rock, oozes through a crack. In the
daytime the heat expands the granite, and the
crack closes, gradually opening again as the
atmosphere cools off at night. That is why the
flow is greatest just before daybreak.
UNDER THE PINES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Under the pines, whose crests are tossing'
In coy disdain of the wooing breeze ;
Watching the western sunbeams glossing
With sunset glory the sombre trees.
High above are soft white clouds sailing
Over the infinite heaven’s blue,
While in the woods the winds are wailing
Old-world tunes as they wander through.
Strange soul-songs are those breezes singing—
Low, rippling laughter and long-drawn sigh—
Youthful yearnings, and fancies bringing
Out of their tombs in the time gone by.
See ! from yon bough the sere leaf falling—
Emblem this of the springtime fled—
Scenes and songs of life’s spring recalling,
Dreams half forgotten, and loves long dead.
Hope may paint in a rosy color
Pair visions for hearts with care o’ereast,
Hut fainter her pictures, aye, and duller
Than mem’ry’s hues of a cherished past.
n.s.w., 1901.

Janor ; The subject was records—
athletic and otherwise—and their chances of being
clipped. Warrigal Jack opined that a drover
he d met up Carpentaria way clutched a record
™ would take a lot of reaching out for.
Inis bull-shifter could turn out, light a fire,
boil his quart, bake a johnny cake (John called
the confection a charcoal tart), eat his breakfast,
roll up his swag, saddle his moke, fill his pipe and
mount —time for the lot 10 minutes. We reckoned
it was about a bed-rock lie.

“ Itan ” : “Caber” (13. 30/8/’O6) solves
the prickly pear problem in Bananaland by sug-
gesting that Eidston should plough it out. Well,
there are 4,000.000 acres infested, tens of thou-
sands of which are a dense mass, 20ft. high, and
intertwined with brush and scrub. The Rids ton
Government dug into a patch in the Chinchilla
district, with the aid of the unemployed, and
although many of the men engaged only made
oare tucker, the cost ran into about £l2 per acre,
lhere are few of the cursed areas which men will
take from the Government as a gift. Anyone
who wants to try an idea would probably receive
very liberal treatment from Minister for Lands
Bell, and all the official help possible.
* * • *
A Groperlander, in a thirsty mood probably,
has been gathering' facts about big Australian
grape vines. Apparently Westralia takes the
bunch, with Hester’s vine at Blackwood Park, near
Bridgewater—a huge intemperate thing with a
trunk 4ft. 4Jin. in circumference, and covering 2520
square feet of rough trellis, thus eclipsing the great
Hampton Court (Eng.) vegetable. At M‘Laren
Vale (S. A.) an old neglected vine, 40in. in girth,
covers 972 square feet; but Thomas Hardy’s great
currant vine at Bankside (S.A.) beats this. It
covers an area of 1500 square feet, and has yielded
in one season as much as 3001 b. of dried fruit. In
another good S.A. wine district, Charles Doade-
ridge, of Angaston, has a similar vine a little
larger, from which a crop of 3401 b. has been sold
for £8 10s. The Westralian Club, St. George’s-
terrace, Perth, has a vine 30in. in the trunk on
its premises, kept very much back of late by hard
pruning. What about the old gnarled climber
which wanders round the Bank of New South
Wales at Parramatta?

WIDDY O'BRIEN; “Yes, Missus O'Grady, we

“ Hornblende ”; One of the queerest uses
for Bulletin pictures is as church decorations.
About 14 years ago I was at Deptford, on the
Nicholson River, Gippsland, and used to go with
great irregularity to the Church of England in
that place. The church was a slab hut about
15ft. x 10ft., with the usual bark roof. It had
been built by a digger, and he presented it to the
township as a church when he left the district.
The inside was papered with pages of illustrated
papers and Bulletin cartoons. Usually there
was an interval in the service for a dog fight—for
the dogs attended church just as regularly as the
humans.
A BAD NEIGHBOR.

“Ralph Rodney”: School-teaching out-
back once, I boarded with a boundary-rider whose
wife served us with great variety of meals. No
matter what young Jimmy’s rifle took a fancy to,
his mother would work it into a stew, and, during
the mice plague, I often had horrible suspicions,
especially when mince-pies came along. My land-
lady was liberal with native “game,’’from kangaroo-
rat to the hedgehog, or grey magpie, but, when it
came to beef or mutton, she didn’t merely econo-
mise—she “ econominimised. ’ One day I saw a
601 b. tin of honey arrive. Next morning she
caught me eating butter and honey on my bread.
“Lawks, Mr. Rodney, do you mean to say you
eats honey and butter together?” “Yes,” 1 re-
plied, “ I’m trying to save the extra slice of bread,
Mrs. Screw.” The following day the storekeeper
got his honey back with this note: “Dear Sir,
the noo teacher don’t like honey I find, so I retern
the tin.” Next day I was on bandicoot.
“Prooshan” : For the snake-catching
championship, I am prepared to nominate one
August Eichhorn, of Main Adelong. From
his youth he has lived in a district infested
with all our venomous snakes—bar the death-
adder—and has made a close study of them.
In the snake season it’s a rare thing to meet
“Org” without a sarpint in his shirt or
pocket. He has absolutely no fear of the
brutes. Eichhorn scorns the forked stick,
always capturing with the hand, and
grooming the snake like one caressing a cat.
If the wriggler looks ugly out of the corner
of his eye, he drops him from thumb and
forefinger; then he dabs on to him again,
and drops him perhaps a second time, and
gives him a run, ever going backward from
him and turning him repeatedly with his
boot. I saw a big, red-bellied black snake one
day coiled in among some razor-grass, and
called “ Org.” He came, and dabbed his
hand in, and had him tethered with a bit of
bootlace presently to a stout bracken fern ;
but the snake got a purchase on another fern
after a while with his tail, and, on returning,
we found him nearly choked. Eichhorn re-
leased him, and, engaging him with his right
hand, he pulled off his boot and sock with his
left, and in a twinkling had the snake’s head
in the sock, and tied round securely ; then he
threw him up on to his coat on the bank, and
resumed his work till evening, when he took
the snake home. He has an effective antidote.
I saw him bitten on the tongue once by a tiger
snake, during a snake swallowing exhibition,
but he didn’t suffer any serious consequences.
“ Janor ” : The moon has a big say in the
superstitions of bushmen. For instance, they will
not leave saws or other steel tools exposed to its
rays because their temper will be destroyed.
There also prevails among them the idea that
timber felled at full moon will quickly decay.
Then there is the belief in moon blindness as the
result of sleeping in the glare of the lunar light.
The bush weather-prophet’s outfit is mainly moon.
When it comes in new he makes his forecast. If
the crescent is disposed horizontally in the sky,
with the horns pointing upwards, he reckons it
won’t rain; the rain might spill out of the
perpendicular moon, but not out of the horizontal
one. The yarn that food, especially fish, is
rendered poisonous by exposure to moonlight is
not confined to bushies, but is universal. I regard
most moon legends with an eye of unbelief.
“Bricky” (who. as far as we know, is a
sober man): There is a snake here (the High
Plains of Gippsland, Vic.) which £ have never
seen mentioned. It is about two feet long and
dark in color, and when the snow is two and
three feet deep is often to be met gliding along
the surface. It’s a lively little beggar, too. I
can’t say if it is venomous or not, but the miners
look upon it with awe. I was always under the
impression that all snakes lay dormant during
the winter months, but I’ve altered my opinion.
* * * *■
In the “ Never-never country,”
outside civilisation, in Queensland, everyone
keeps goats. When the small boys want a
holiday from school they first ride a William.
During lessons various complaints, “Teacher, I
can’t sit next to Tommy Jones; he has been
riding goats, and it isn’t nice. I feel sick.”
Result, Tommy Jones and others sent home for
the day—just what the young wretches wanted.

“ Nulla ” : There used to be (and may be
still) red deer around O’Connell Plains and out
towards Oberon, in the Bathurst district. Tom
M'Kibbin, the erstwhile break-bowler, was a
mighty deer hunter, and years ago some of the
Glebe cricketers had shoes made out of a deer
skin got by him.
* * * *
‘ ‘ Veldt ”: I note your remarks re Ha wkes-
bury College mules (26/7/’O6), and differ from you
in regarding the hybrid as wholly sulky and cross-
grained. The term may apply,to individuals, but
is not true genetically. They use mules in
the U.S. A. simply because the mule won’t allow
himself to be overdriven by the nigger. The mule
means dollars because (1) he is active and saves
time ; (2) he will do more work than a horse ; (3)
he will live on the smell of a gun-rag. In Colorado,
where beet sugar isabig thing, the mule is a feature.
There the beets are planted in rows only 18 inches
apart, and the mule, with his small 'hoofs and
nimble feet, will walk in this space all day and
not smother a single plant. In France and Ger-
many, where horses are used, the rows are at
least three feet apart. Of course, one mustn’t get
too near a mule’s hind legs; but, then, one
shouldn’t play the frivolous ox with a threshing
machine.
* * • •
“Paul Pearson”; Saw a rare sight yes-
terday, and regretted I had not a camera. It was
a baby camel carefully strapped on its mother’s
back, accompanying the team out of the town.
The driver (white) informed me that the youngster
was suffering from a sore leg. Anyhow, it seemed
perfectly happy, and gazed down at me with the
supercilious expression which is characteristic of
the animal. The “old woman ” wore a contented
and human-like expression also, and seemed
pleased to hump Baby Bunting in this fashion.
BALLAD OF THE GAME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fed up ? Fed up, and full of it ?
You’ll only make a bull of it
If you go smooging round for help and looking for a
hand ;
The only hand that’s any good
S a level left as hard as wood
To deal out stoush to Destiny as stiff as you can land.
Suppose you feel the wrong of it?
What odds ! Don’t make a song of it,
?*or put your trust in Providence; be Pagan, and pay
cash
In blow for blow around, the ring.
Don t pray, for that’s the kind of thing
To leave your soul unguarded for Misfortune’s half-arm
smash.
What though there’s not much show for it;
Give Fate a solid go for it.
be sure your chance will come again to be top dog, and
bite ;
There’s neither luck nor trick to it,
If .you run mute and stick to it.
The question isn’t “ Did you fail ? ” but only “Did you
fight ? ”
