ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
Australian (Ceylon): The real facts as to the
Six Hatters case ? The Australian Immigration Law pro-
vides that laborers must not be introduced under con-
tract to lower the Australian wage-rate. If it is intended
to introduce laborers under contract, a certificate of
exemption must be secured by the employer, who must
show that the men are not imported with the object of
casting Australians out of work. A Sydney hat manu-
facturer imported six hatters from England. He neg-
lected, either from ignorance of the law or for some
other motive, to take out an exemption certificate! • The
men were, therefore, stopped at the Customs, and
allowed to land on parole. Otherwise their liberty was
in no way interfered with. A general election was immi-
nent, and the Reid party and the Reid'press saw in the
incident a stick with which to beat the Protectionist
Government and its ally, the Labor party. A flight of
1000 lies coruscated in the firmament. It was generally
said that these British subjects were imprisoned (one
yarn stated that they were sent back in irons). Meanwhile
the employer, after taking full advantage of the gorgeous
advertisement which chance had given him, took out an
exemption certificate, and the Six Hatters got to work in
his factory. This didn't stop the lies. But at the election
w'hich followed all Six Hatters were on the committee of
the Labor candidate who opposed Eeid ; which is a
pretty fair indication that they had no grievance. Yet the
anti-Australian Stinking Fish party still circulates abroad
stories of how six British subjects were put in
irons at Sydney and deported ; it’s one of these
yarns you have encountered in your Ceylon fast-
ness. .. .T.F.M. : The Age campaign “to restore the
Riverina to Victoria” is just sheer silliness
Phid : Your questions show some slight misunderstand-
ing'- of the position. (1) The obsolete cruisers which
Britain sells are auctioned on strict scrap-heap condi-
tions : the vessels must be broken up completely ; if they
were sold as going concerns, the price would be vastly
different.. (2) A just-obsolete cruiser would be a more
valuable war weapon to Australia than a converted mer-
chantman, but it would fie of no use at all in peace time.
The “ convertible cruiser ” is of some use in war time,
and a whole lot of use in peace time.... 1.: Please
make sure of your facts. The postage from Eng-
land to Australia is one penny. Heaps of British
letters arrive here safely under a penny stamp
Coramba : First Australian gold discovery was made near
Bathurst (N.S. W.) in 1814 ; the convicts were flogged in
order to teach them not to do it again. In January, 1849,
Thomas Chapman found gold at Daisy Hill (Vic.), and
sold alfioz. nuggetin Melbourne ; Superintendent Latrobe
sent Captain Dana and 16 mounted native police to pre-
vent anybody digging any more of it. E. H. Hargreaves
found his gold at Summer Hill Creek (N.S.W.), Feb. 12,
1851 ; that led to the first rush. On May, 22, 1851, the
business was sanctioned, and provision made for licenses.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Premier Price (S.A.) is working too hard
and worrying too much, and a man built as he is
can t stand it.
Henry Parkes’s old friend, Oscar Meyer,
the man of many Exhibitions, lias found his way
to N.S. W. Bankruptcy Court.
Henry Sheffield, formerly Melbourne
public librarian, is living at Warrnambool (Vic.)
in a good state of preservation, aged 98.
Said at N.S.W. Bar that Pilcher, Wade,
and Street, will soon have the opportunity of
refusing appointments to the judicial Bench.
Tourists in M.L. now regularly pilgrim to
Dick Seddon’s old home at Big Dam, Kumara.
this is fame—or the result of good advertising.
Samuel Davenport, just dead in Adelaide,
was the last surviving member of the first South
Australian Ministry under Responsible Govern-
ment.
Judkins, the Methodist apostle of anti-
everything, lias, it seems, political aspirations,
and would climb on the dead body of the tote into
Parliament.
Maoriland Government has £6OOO on the
estimates as a grant to the widow of the late
Richard Seddon. But, from present appearances,
no such charity is needed.
Died April 6, at the Monastery, Ivings-
wood, Bristol (Eng.), Rev. Philip Bernard
Lassetter, second son of Fredk. Lassetter, of Red
Leaf, Double Bay, Sydney.
The frequent illnesses of Premier Kidston
seem to indicate a possible breakdown, and fears
are entertained that he may not be able to attend
regularly at the House during the present session.
Dead recently, in England, Charles Bagot,
who is credited with having discovered, on his
father’s sheep run, in 1842, the first copper ore in
S..Aus. The find was the origin of the Kapunda
mine.
The oldest chorister in Australasia is
Joseph Tuokwell, of St. Paul’s Church, Canter-
bury, aged 85. In his ninth year he was a
member of the choir of St. George’s Royal
Chapel, Windsor.
That talented painter, Charles Conder,
who, when in Sj'dney, was a bookseller’s assis-
tant, has struck a little gold-mine in Paris. He
has a continental reputation as a painter of fans,
and his work brings huge prices.
B. R. Wise, K.C., has disposed of his
fishing property at Port Hacking (Sydney), which
breaks another link of his chain to Australia.
Latest heard of the brilliant counsel, he was on
his way to Brazil to earn a huge law fee.
The offer made by the Stewart family to
the Carrutliers Government of a large area of
Bathurst country for purposes of closer settlement
reminds how the first of the Stewarts squatted on
the Mount. Colonel Stewart, happening to be
Acting-Governor for a few weeks between the
reigns of Brisbane and Darling, claimed the grant,
which was a Governor’s by right, and chose his
area on the Bathurst Plains. Darling refused to
confirm the grant, and a bitter quarrel resulted,
the outcome being, however, that Stewart was
allowed to retain 15,000 acres of the country. The
estate is worked now under tenant farmer
conditions.
INKY WAY CHARACTERS.—NO. 1.
SCHULER , Editor of the AGE.
“J.W. , m The Bulletin (7/6/’O6),
wrote:—“ln Melbourne and Sydney there are
many advt. writers, and only two that know their
business.” Now there is only one, since 11. J.
Bartlett died, September 8, "in his G3rd year.
Money can buy most things, but Sam Hordern
will find it difficult to duplicate Bartlett.
Many condemned Bartlett’s “ads.” as con-
trary to American principles, but he coined
“Famous Low Prices,” and liis quaint stories in
the dailies every morning will be missed by thou-
sands of women, who always looked for them
even before births, marriages and deaths. Strange
that on the day of the announcement of Bartlett’s
death from pneumonia, Bartlett inserted in the
dailies an advt. giving the range of the cough
and cold medicines sold by Anthony Hordern.
The official figures puWhe cost of the new
Sydneyrailway station—called “central” because
it isn’t—at £940,4G3. This represents the cost of
the premises when opened for traffic. So far,
only the first storey of the original design is com-
pleted, with a temporary roof thrown on promis-
cuous-like. If Owe’Sullivan’s ideas are ever
finished, goodness only knows what the total will
be— Owe’Sullivan, for one, never inquired.
The most pic-
turesque figure in
the new Maori-
land Government
is .Tohn Andrew
Millar, Minister
for Marine and
Labor and Com-
missioner of Cus-
toms. He was born
in the Punjab (In-
dia) in 1855, where
his father was a
military captain,
and he smelt pow-
der and blood
through the great
Mutiny. When
that misunder-
standing was set-
tled the family re-
turned to Scot-
land, where An-
drew was edu-
cated with a view
to going back to
India to shed
blood in the next
Mutiny. But he
declared his dis-
like for that busi-
ness early in life,
and was sent out
to an uncle in
Otago to learn the
art and science of
watching the wool
grow on the sheep’s
back. That voy-
age gave him a
taste for the sea,
and to the sea he
went, and even-
tually rose to be a
chief officer in the
service of the N.Z.
Shipping Com-
pany. Then he
joined the Black
Diamond line as
captain. In 1887
he left the service
to become secre-
tary to the Sea-
men’s Union, and
was soon up to the
ears in fight. In
1888 there was a
strike in the Nor-
thern Steamship
Company’s ser-
vice, and practi-
cally every man
came out. Millar chartered three steamers and
used them to keep the strikers employed, and ran
his line in opposition to the Northern Co. for 1(1
months. Then the Northern Co. caved in and
came to terms, and the chartered steamers were
returned to their owners. After that Millar set
to work to organise Labor right through the Isles.
The Wharf Laborers’ Union, the Carters’ Union,
and the Tailoresses’ Union were all his creations,
and by 1890 he was President of the Trades and
Labor Council and Secretary of the Maritime
Labor Council. In that capacity the weight of
the heavy fight in connection with the great strike
fell on his shoulders. Millar contested Port
Chalmers against Union Company Mills, and was
defeated, but three years later lie won the seat
from E. G. Allen. In ’96 he was elected for
Dunedin City ; in ’99 he was made chairman of
the Labor Bills Committee : in 1900 he was on the
Federation Commission; and in 1902 became
Chairman of Committees in the House of Reps.
So the “agitator” of 16 years ago is to-day a
Minister, and as loyal a Labor man a 9 ever.
The Bulletin’s old friend, Frank Maliony,
the brilliant animal painter, writes cheerfully
from London, where things now go very well with
him. He works steadily in blaek-and white, has
some good painting commissions on hand, and is
very proud of his son and heir, now a promising
youth of 18 months. Mahony, who is much
addicted to playing a lone hand, is not much
heard of in Australian circles in London.
N.S. W. Lands Minister Ashton was, till re-
cently, a land agent. He passed his examinations
for the Bar, but did not get himself called; because,
if he were called, he could not do business
direct with his clients and would have to wait
for work until the coy and reluctant attorney
approached him with a brief. Ashton’s knowledge
of that labyrinthine tangle, the land-law, was
extensive, and he therefore had many briefs in a
region where most are blind, and the one-eyed are
kings. It is said that Ashton now- contemplates
having himself called to the Bar, realising, no
doubt, that the palmy days of land agency are
up ; “land agent ” and “ road agent ” have almost
become convertible terms.
Obit, afc the age of 76, Andrew Lysaght,
father of Wollongong, and Illawarra’s oldest
inhabitant. For many years he was mayor of
the town, and represented Illawarra in Par-
liament. He was chock-full of humor and
quaint sayings. On one occasion he had
engaged two of Wollongong's chief beer and
rum experts to erect some verandah-posts, and
the inspector of police doubted their ability to
perform the task, and advised him to engage
more. “Nonsense!” said Lysaght—“you don’t
know my men. Why, they could lift these posts
with their breath !” Even in articulo mortis he
had his joke. He made signs that he had some-
thing important to communicate, and whispered,
“Don’t forget to get me a ticket in Tatt.’s
sweep.” He used to take considerable interest
in turf matters, and was for a long time judge for
the Wollongong Turf Club. In early life remarkable
for strength and skill with his fists, as M.L.A.
he cowed many bullies in the Sydney Parliament.
A constable once thought it necessary to remove
“Andy” from the Illawarra racecourse, and pro-
ceeded with due care, but he turned the tables,
handcuffed the constable and carried him to the
lockup.

Judkins’ magazine, the Review op Re-
views, should be called The Jugful of Judkins.
In the few editorial articles in July number are
53 “I’s,” to say nothing of the “my’s” and
“ me’s.” And Judkins is such a dry subject.
The Bulletin hears from an Australian
visitor to London that Captain Collins, who is in
charge of the Commonwealth offices, has been
“ having a great time socially.” Which is very
nice for Mr. Collins. But what about Australia?
Dr. Yon Neumayer, who was in charge of
the old Melbourne Observatory at Flagstaff Hill
from the early 50’s to ! 63, and has since been a
resident of Neustadt, Germany, was recently
fkcd in his native country on attaining his 80th
year.
Canon Goodman has just retired, after 52
years pastorate of Christ Church, Geelong. The
foundation stone of that edifice was laid in 1843,
by Bishop Broughton, Sydney Metropolitan.
Before that, Charles Sladen conducted the ser-
vices in his own house.
It is proposed by the salmon packers of
British Columbia to import Hindu coolies to eke
out the number of Chinese hands engaged in
putting up tinned salmon. Just as well for
Australians to know that tinned salmon is packed
by Chinese and niggers.
A young artist who scores the honor of
getting a picture into N.S.W. National Gallery
almost at first try is Mary Heydon, a daughter of
L. E. Heydon, M.L.C. The canvas is a fine
landscape in a subdued color key, and it is a long
time since any Australian has painted still water
more charmingly.
There has just died at the Perth (W.A.)
poor-house, an old fellow called “Sam,” who, as
a private in the 33rd Regiment, was sentenced by
Baron Martin to 20 years transportation for
stealing a woman’s pocket-handkerchief in Man-
chester in 1861. Sam maintained to the last that
he did not steal it ; that the lady dropped it and
he picked it up, and was in the act of returning it
when she accused him of theft. He never prayed
after receiving his sentence, as he said he could
not'conscientiously repeat “forgive us our tres-
passes as we forgive those who trespass against
us.” He never could forgive Baron Martin.
Tom Seddon, entering Maoriland Parlia-
ment at 22, receives his show as a Premier’s son.
N.S.W. Premiers’ sons did not make much of
similar shows. Three of them—Charlie Cowper,
Frank Earn ell, and Varney Parkes—won seats on
the strength of their fathers’ names, but achieved
comparatively little in Parliament, and are now
outside. Old Slippery Cowper made a desperate
effort to boost young Cowper, by making the
Clerk of the Executive Council (which was young
Cowper) a member of the Cabinet, but even that
didn’t give young Charles a foothold.
That eminent humorist, Lord Rosebery
(an adept, by the way, at horse-nomenclature),
missed one distinctly beautiful chance of a joke.
His family name is Primrose, and he married a
daughter of the great Jewish house of Rothschild.
But he didn’t have his eldest son and heir chris-
tened Moses Primrose.
The Maori does not usually shine as an
advocate of Cold Tea, but A. S. Ngata, the new
M.H.R, for the eastern Maori electorate, is a
strenuous speaker in the prohibition cause.
Ngata, who is a B. A. of the Maoriland University,
and also a lawyer, is called the leader of the
Young Maori Party—whatever that may be.
Ormond College (Vic.) Theological Hall,
in appointing a professor of theology and church
history, chose a person from Aberdeen in pre-
ference to Australian nominees, on the ground
that to reject the Aberdonian would be “flouting”
the Scotch Commissioners who had recommended
him. They didn’t mind flouting the Australians.
Alfred Deakin suffers so much from
modesty that probably it would never occur to
him that he would make an excellent High Court
Judge. The affable Alfred has spent most of his
political career playing second fiddle to men
whom he really inspired, but who had more liking
for the middle of the stage and the limelight than
he.
Apropos D. H. Denieliy and Kent-street
(23/8/06). When “Little Dan” was born, Kent-
street was a somewhat aristocratic part of Sydney.
A few of the fine houses of the time still stand.
At one time, D’Arcy Wentworth lived in Kent-
street, so did Justice Dowling, Colonel Snodgrass,
Dr. Fattorini, Frederick Garling. and Capt.
Hunter, A.D.C. to Governor Bourke.
Dan O’Connor had a triumphal journey,
the other day, from ’Frisco to New York. Sara
Bernhardt was aboard the same train, and the
Sydney acquaintance of many years ago was
revived. Sara remembered how Postmaster-
General O’Connor had lavished the hospitality of
a rich and generous Government on her, excitedly
embraced Daniel, and insisted on him accompany-
ing her for the rest of the journey. Daniel, in his
grandest and most courtly manner, eloquently
responded. Everybody along the line knew Sara
was aboard, so the stations were crowded, and
each time the train slowed down Sara and Dan
put their heads through the windows. And Dan
divided with Sara the roar of cheers. It was a
great time for Dan.
“J.T.”, on an “identify” of many gold-
fields : —
Reported that Tom Meehan, otherwise “Tom the
Rager,” has bottomed his last shaft. Few harder battlers
ever breathed. He was one of the first men on Bendigo;
then drifted gradually up to Charters Towers, overlanded
from there to Port Darwin, and finally struck Kimberley
diggings. Hall’s Creek proving a duffer, “The Rager”
shouldered Matilda, and, accompanied only by his dog,
“Paddy,” made his way down south to Lake Austin.
From there he went through Cue, Nannine, and Abbott’s
to Peak Hill, the richest patch in Australia. He acquired
a third interest in a small but wonderfully rich lease,
and, when Darlington Simpson bought all the others out,
“The Rager ” declined to sign a transfer unless the pur-
chase money was planked down. In vain did Simpson
represent that his cheque was the same as money ; and
he had to set off for London without “The Rager’s”
block. At last “ Toppy ” Longden, formerly of the Mel-
bourne Football Club, succeeded in getting “ The Rager’s”
signature, the consideration being an open cheque for
£15,000. Writer saw the cheque. Longden resold to
Simpson’s crowd for £33,000.
Tom Seddon, who represents his late
father’s constituency in M.L. Parliament, lately
visited the wilds of his electorate. At Karangarua
he refereed at a football match, and seized the
opportunity, whilst speaking of the finer points of
the game, to thank spectators and pi a vers for
electing him. The M.L.
politician who can mix
with football points a
political speech need
never fear the result of
an election.

The British Commissioner to the Christ-
church (M.L.) Exhibition is John Eldon Gorst,
who first saw Maoriland in 1860. He emigrated
with the intention of becoming a lay-helper to
Bishop Selwyn. Having acquired a knowledge of
the Maori language, he was sent to the Upper
Waikato as Civil Commissioner. He set up an
industrial school and established a newspaper,
Pihoihoi Mokk Moke. Incensed by an article,
the Maoris seized the plant and expelled Gorst.
He returned to England in 1865, and entered the
Commons as member for Cambridge. In 1885
he became Solicitor-General in the Salisbury
Government, and subsequently was Under-Secre-
tary for India.

The Teeth of Tommy Atkins. —The last report
of the director of recruiting in the British army, shows an
increasing number of rejections and especially in those
refused on account of defective teeth. Now our recruits
are principally drawn from the artisan classes whose food
is largely composed of white bread. Everyone knows
that the bones and teeth are composed of phosphates, and
that nearly all these phosphates are removed from wheat
with the bran in milling white flour. llow, therefore, can
we expect strong physique when the actual substances
out of which the body is built are absent from the food?
If “ Cerebos ” Salt instead of common salt were employed
in the kitchen, it would, to a great extent, remedy the
defect, because this salt contains an exact proportion of
these vital phosphates, and, therefore, increases the food
value of everything to which it is added. One of our
most pressing national needs is a clear knowledge of the
absolute relation between sound food and a sound body.*
In shorthand speed examinations it is unusual'
for ladies to top the list. Up to the standard required by
the shorthand clerk, 120 words per minute, the lady
students of our shorthand schools will usually excel the
male, but the majority of speed certificates at rates of 150
words per minute and upwards are gained by male short-
hand writers. At the shorthand speed examination of
the Incorporated Phonographic Society held in Sydney on
26th May last, the highest and second-highest speeds
were gained by lady candidates, Misses Alice Hagarty
and Gertrude Mole. Both of these ladies, also more than
two-thirds of the successful candidates, were trained at
Messrs. Stott and Hoare’s Business College.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ Sydney, September 11, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
Madame Arral, the operatic soprano, who opens
at our Town Hall next Friday, is the youngest of
a family of seventeen children. Caruso came
somewhere about the middle of a group of 24
youngsters—all the offspring of one mother. “As
arrows in the hands of the mighty,’’etc. You
know the rest of the Biblical verse. In 3uch large
families it is only a fair thing of Providence to
?. ro P > n a genius. Paris Conservatoire, and then
the Opera Comique, gave Mme. Arral training
and experience. It was while filling a two-years
engagement in Russia that the then Mademoi-
selle married a Russian prince. That was just
before the present Czar came into his kingdom—
and his bombs. She became a widow some years
ago, and is now possessed of an American husband,
a very blonde bird, whose voice hangs caressingly
on the name of Claire when he is addressing
*l ls w '^ e - Claire suits Madame better than
Blanche, for she has eyes of velvet-black and hair
as black and bright as a crow’s wing. The
American must be a good sort, for Madame’s cats
rush to meet him with loud miows of a purely
disinterested nature, leaving their milk to make
him welcome. The Siamese cat, a blue-eyed
feline, with blue-grey fur and black points, looks
too proud to touch a bird of paradise with his
whiskers.
In Russia, the status of the actress and the
singer is higher than in any other European
capital. Dolores acknowledged this in a recent
interview, and said that, no matter how humble a
woman’s birth, she may, by the exploitation of a
certain talent in the fine art 3 or by the lever of
beauty and manners, rise to rustle among the
coroneted and starred and eagled crowd who
snare with the Imperial house the attentions of
the dynamitard. Mostly, when the lowly-born
damsel “rises,” she has to do so by way of the
marriage ring bestowed by some male who was
swell born or had “ arrived.” And often she does
not crave for this kind of feast. Madame Arral
wears a Russian decoration and an Egyptian
decoration, but her six hundred pound frock will
be of more interest to Sydney women.
One of the most refreshingly fresh Bridge
parties given in Sydney occurred last week at the
house of a French merchant. Guests played in
fancy dress. They, on arrival, were mazed and
dazed at being received by a “lady” they had
never prevjously seen. Her face and hair were
artfully artistic, but her shoulders were beautiful,
the dimples in the proper spots, and she showed
a lot of them. That is, a large quantity of
shoulder and many dimples. All these charms,
including the faked face and hair, were those of the
host. The hostess was got up as a waiting maid.
Monsieur Pinard, the French Consul-General,
frisked in a powdered wig, and was wreathed
with white camellias, emblematic of his inno-
cence. (Innocence of English, of course.) Mrs.
Monty Severn, in a blue gown, black hat and red
roses, and a crook, represented Lady Mary
Carlyle. The smart widow White, of “Green-
oaks, ’ took the trump card, or the cake, or what-
ever you take at Bridge, by representing Cleopatra.
She dressed the star-eyed Egyptian all in long,
clinging, glittering black, and the veil that hung
from below her eyes was secured to the bridge of
the nose by some marvellous mother-o’-pearl clip.
Chubby Mrs. Jack Sheridan was a Dutch doll.
One damsel was in male evening dress ; and a
man who owns a motor drove his gasoline cart to
the house and played in his motor clothes and
fascinating goggles. It was all so accurately
Bridged, that one pair who came as Punch and
Judy also brought along their dog Toby with a
ham frill on his neck.
A London friend sends an account of Annette
Kellermann’s last attempt to swim the English
Channel
A rap at the door, “Time soon to start, ladies 1” This
was the news that greeted Annette Kellermann’s and my
ears when we had, so it seemed, jusu started off in a
sound sleep. It was then 12.45 p.m. Up we jumped.
Soon I was sewing the swimmer into her blue and white
striped bathing suit, after smearing her body with
lanoline. That lanoline is excellent stuff ; it just “ caked ”
on, and when the Australian mermaid had been some little
time in the water she appeared “ a la Milo,” for her flesh
below and above the bathing-suit shone like marble.
The action of the water “ thickened ” the lanoline, which
resisted the water. Then came the adjustment of her
motor goggles, which I secured to her face by means of
collodion (the painful sensation her eyes were undergoing
from the fumes of the collodion, was part of her Channel
swimming troubles). Next her ears were plugged with
cotton wool. Then, after eating the whites of two eggs
beaten to a froth and slightly sweetened, Annette
Kellermann was ready for the fray, or rather the spray.
The Something Bay bachelors gave a dance iast
week at Mosman Town Hall. Everything went
as it should—until supper time, when it was dis-
covered that no supper was available. The
caterer had forgotten the date. The bachelors did
the best they could by buying up all the available
cake in Mosman, and getting a local shop to make
coffee, but it had to be eaten in the roughest way
possible, as there were only a couple of bare tables
available for the setting-out.
A group of the loveliest, quaintest-looking little
children may be seen running about the neigh-
borhood of Wolseley-road, near Cranbrook. They
wear, apparently, a single woollen garment—a
jersey-and-knicker combination—no hats or shoes,
and, out of a picturesque disarray of carefully-
Cared-for hair, their frank young faces look with
more than the average fearlessness of vouth. They
are the children of the Lucys (he is “an imported
railway official). Small Lucy girl is dressed as her
brothers, and plays football with the infant football
club on Rushcutters Bay oval. This combination
numbers about twenty frilly lambs. None over
the doddering age of eight is eligible, but, so far,
the little Lucy girl is the only kickeress. The
club is coached and umpired by a veteran pro-
fessional. If it attacks him, as footballers do
sometimes go for the umpire, he cannot be hurt
severely.
A certain gifted musician, from whose hair and
beard you’d expect to see larks and field-mice
issue, generally has an adoring bevy of debutantes
tagging on to him. One of them : “0, Professor,
there’s suck a white hair loose in your beard ; do
let me pull it out! ” She pulled a piece of white
cotton threaded into the eye of an able-bodied
needle. “Ach!” remarked the ivory-pounder,
“dot is vere it vos ’idin, eh ?” And he put it into
his coat-lapel.
The Ewan Frazers have got a new cypress-green
motor, and the Mac Neils have another that
doesn’t look new. But it’s roomy enough to carry
many of the officers of the Pah’f’l. Those who
pad the hoof between the Mac Neil’s Point and
town think this is the motor omnibus, and are
always rushing it with a tuppence fare in their
unmanicured hands. And, when it won’t stop,
they go home and write to the Traffic Inspector
demanding the dismissal of the chap who drives it.
Two more Pah’f’l officers are getting ready to
ask “Will you?” to two Sydney girls who, no
doubt, are practising the whispered “ Yes.”
Twelve dances are fixed for race week, so this
over-girled State will thrill to the froufrou of frills
and frocks. The Dibbs, the Abbey Dangars, and
the Ewan Frazers, will be amongst the benefactors.
That remarkably bouncing ballroom floor that
was made for Mr. Willie Watt (and which is a
moveable feast prepared by a local builder), will
do duty for several of the dances.
Last night Mayor and Mayoress Allen Taylor
had some 90 persons to dinner at the Town Hall.
To the Aldermen and several Harbor and Bar
lights (that is naval and law men), and their
wives, who made up the party, were given the
fatted bird on the lordly dish, and great was the
reign of the pink rose over the rose-pink tables.
When Mrs Allen Taylor goes out of office, the
name of her favorite charity, the Thirlmere Home
for Consumptives, will be found written—in lines
of worry—on her forehead. Of course, if she gets
the £lOOO required, the lines won’t be written.
The committee has £2OOO, but it wants a third.
If this intimation should cause an irresistible on-
rush of donors armed with cheques, the com-
mittee won’t mind being a little injured in the
crush.

cynic to me, “ generally has to think of brandy-

A bit of scenic criticism overheard on a U.S.S.
boat from Auckland to Sydney (uttered by a
young Englishman) in a check suit and a 6-inch
collar. “ They—aw—say the Wangan ui River is the
Rhine of New Zealand. I don’t think it is—aw—
much like the Rhine. The meals served on board
the boats are nothing like. You get the most
rippin, feeds on the Rhine boats.”
Freeman-Thomas has his portrait in London
illustrated papers because he, as a Liberal,
defeated some other man at an election, or some
such foolishness. Picture of the large-eyed, wide-
browed man with the rather narrow jaw reminds
that the original is the husband of the youngest
daughter of ex-Governor of Victoria, “Sunbeam ”
Brassey. She rustled considerably at the election,
and must have helped her young man and hin-
dered his opponent immensely. For she was one of
the most vital sparks, and one of the best-looking
that ever lighted a vice-regal landscape. She
couldn’t have been more than two or three and
twenty when she came to Australia, fresh as a
wild rose, and wearing her hair Sweet Simplicity
style, parted in the centre, and knobbed on the
nape of her neck. Her frankness and absence of
frill were child-like. At a Masonic'ball she told a
group of Australiennes, “You mustn’t think
Freeman is a Mason—l wouldn’t let him, because
I didn’t want the bother of worming the secrets
out of him.” And Freeman, standing by, uttered
a remonstrative “Oh.” Also, her three-year-old
son and the step-mother’s girl, kids of about the
same age, used to fight like mad—which was all
very human and refreshing.
Death of Mrs. Hughes, wife of Federal member
W. M. Hughes, who was Minister for External
Affairs in the Watson Government, is a sad loss
to those bereaved, especially as there are young
children in the family. Deceased lady didn’t set
out to make any figure in Society or politics, and
was a believer in the creed that the “hand that
rocks the cradle rules the world.”
Miss Annie Mayor, who is running various
gems of literature in character, at the Palace, was
noted, when playing here in former days, for her
brown eyes, russet hair, and dimples. The
S’African climate has left her, so her friends say,
with all three charms unfaded.

Dr. Arthur a while ago sent a circular letter to
the Anglo-Indian papers, drawing attention to
the value of Australia as a garden of rest for
retired officers and other officials, a good sporting
ground for young officers on leave, and a fine land
for fresh enterprise for planters. All the papers
printed the letter, and by last mail a sackful of
replies reached the doctor. Writers are all
desirous of a change, either of climate, or
sport, or work. Majority make detailed in-
quiries as to facilities for educating children
here and ask after “our socialistic and revolu-
tionary trend.” Curiously enough, whilst most
of the inquirers confess a blank ignorance of Aus-
tralia, they have some knowledge of Tasmania.
That little island has long been a harbor of refuge
for retired Indian Civil Service functionaries.
An Australian artist writes from London
I went to the E.A. t’other day and had a look round.
P. Fox, of Melbourne, has a very good thing in,
badly hunt'', of course, as is the case with the other
Australians. Longstaff’s portrait deserved better placing.
Fullwood has two oils—“A Chalk Pit and Nelsons
Column.” They are very good apparently, but hard to
see. Streeton has two water-colors—St. Paul s from the
river and New South Kensington Museum (al maze of
scaffolding), both rather slight. Lambert exhibits two
oils—study of a head and a picture called Lottie and
the Lady,” which I can’t exactly make out, but both
show good work. I may be wrong, but I don t think Ive
seen as good work here by our men as I did in Australia.

From my Brisbane correspondent:—
Captain and Mrs. Bertram Brooke, who are living in
the pretty river-side residence of D. B. Eden, gave a
smart party on Friday night. Masks and dominoes were
worn by the 70 or 80 guests, who were received by an
orderly, ushered into the ballroom, and turned loose.
No sign of host or hostess, who quietly slipped into the
room along with the rest. It was nearly 11 before the
hostess was recognised.
Everyone .is golfing madly this week, and the annual
ladies’ match resulted in favor of Miss D. Henderson, a
young and very promising player, who beat a number of
old hands right out.

by Jury,” which followed, was chiefly notable for the
6inging and acting of Mrs. R. Taylor, one of the Munro
clan, and sister to Mrs. Colonel Spencer Browne.
Yours Affectionately, ARENEHI.
A small sum might be devoted annually
by some State Government —they all grant votes
for much worse purposes—for scientific botanical
work in the direction of attempting to improve
Australian fruits. A trial is being made at the
Howlong (N.S.W.) State farm to use a root of the
wild grape in the hope of producing a phylloxera-
resistant variety. The native strawberry, which
is gradually giving way to many imported noxious
pests, is considered to be well worth attention in
this connection. Also, the wild cherry, native
lime, the North Coast blue plum, tamarinds, and
others. Have we a Fatman in the State who is
anxious to perpetuate his memory by a bequest
for the purpose? It would be a change from the
stained church window and the town pump.
“ C.M.” : A resident of the Cressy district
(Vic.), a lady who excels in cookery, has mastered
the art of making braized sparrow on toast as
savory as quail. Her husband, who is a keen
“sport,” declares that sparrow is not distinguish-
able from quail the way she cooks it. This lady
has possibly solved the problem of the sparrow
nuisance. Just let “ sparrow on toast ” catch on,
and there will be an early slump in one bird
nuisance. Is there any hope for crow on toast?
The Romance of Railway Signalling.
“ C. A. J.” on railway signalling : —
An obscure cablegram recently mentioned that the
London and North Western Railway Company of England
had adopted a system of “audible signals.” The story
of progress in railway signalling is a fascinating one. But
something substantial in money and fame still awaits the
man who can devise the system of signalling that will
be absolutely independent of human fallibility.
The first railway signaller was the man who rode on
horseback, carrying a flag, in front of George Stephen-
son’s engine on the Stockton -and Darlington railway.
He was not there to protect the travelling public on the
train, but the other portion of it which might be crossing
the line. The usual speed was about nine miles an hour;
but there were places where the signalman got from in
front and held his breath while a daredevil drove the
show at the dizzy rate of 15 miles an hour. By 1839
speed had increased to 37 miles an hour, and a host
of narrow escapes from serious accidents, mostly
through trains rushing full speed into stations
before their time, and nearly colliding with shunt-
ing operations, proved the necessity of some sort
of signalling system. At first the men who were moving
trucks about protected themselves by placing red or blue
flags beside the line. But in wet and foggy weather the
sodden flags hung limply round the sticks, and on gloomy
days failed to attract the notice of the drivers. They
were replaced by colored discs, and then a great contro-
versy broke out as to the merits of the “ negative ” or
“ positive ” systems of signalling. Under the negative,
the driver rushed into the station, unless he saw a signal
to stop him. Under the positive, he stayed outside till
he got a signal to come in. Both systems had their
drawbacks. Under the negative, people were killed at
the station, under the positive they were killed outside.
The positive system won.
* * • *
In 1841 Charles Hutton Gregory erected the first sema-
phore of modern type on the top of New Cross Station,
on the Croydon line. Then an unknown genius devised
the use of the home and distant signal, which was a com-
bination of both positive and negative systems. The
driver who found the distant signal against him whistled
loudly, slowed down to four miles an hour, and crept
inside the distant signal and up to the home signal,
where he waited till the lowering of the arm gave him
the sign to enter the station, the distant signal mean-
while protecting his rear from attack. This was reckoned
the solution of the whole problem of station signalling.
In the meantime, the other side of signalling, that con-
trolling the movements of trains on given sections of rail-
way, was proceeding apace. In 1838 the Great Western
Company erected the first railway telegraph, between
Paddington and West Drayton, the famous five-needle
instrument, on which the departure of a train was tele-
graphed to the next station, and its safe arrival reported.
Not long after this wire was erected a murder was com-
mitted on the train going west. A Quaker was seen to
get out of the carriage in which the body was found and
enter the train for London, and the man who wired the
arrival and departure of the trains sent a message to
Paddington to “ arrest Quaker on No. 24 ; man murdered
No. 21,” and the Quaker was so astonished at his arrest
that he confessed.
n * * *■
In 1844 the first “ Absolute Block ” system of signalling
was introduced on the Norwich-Yarmouth line. It was
invented by Cooke and Wheatstone, and consisted of a
box which showed a red disc, marked “ line from A to B
Blocked ” when a train had been correctly signalled. But
at best it was only a reminder, and drunken and forget-
ful officers forgot to signal'and sent trains into one
another cheerfully. For several years a frightful
massacre of the public went on, for by this time trains
were running at high rates of speed, and following one
another over the same set of rails with only ten minutes
between them.
* * * *
After that there is no clear history of invention. Differ-
ent companies used different appliances, and -were
constantly modifying and superseding systems. Improve-
ments in signalling and safe work meant a saving of
millions to the railway companies, for the closer they
could run their trains the more they could run per day,
and the fewer lines of rails they could work with. And
lines cost an awful lot of money. The stations became a
network of rails, out of which the untrained
mind could make nothing but confusion. Railway
management had become a great science. Slowly, and
with many failures, the famous “interlocking” system
was evolved. This was a system of points by which
when siding No. 4 was opened for a train on No. 1 main
line to enter, all entrances to that siding from main lines
Nos. 2, 3, 4, etc., were automatically locked. Then
Winter and Preece devised an electric block by which the
man at the station which was to receive the train had to
open his points for the siding into which the train was to
go before it left the previous signalling station, and it re-
mained locked open till the train had passed into it, when
it closed automatically, and remained locked till a fresh
set of signals opened it again. The same appliance worked
the starting signal. The officer at the station of departure
could not lower his starting semaphore till the officer at
the receiving station had locked the line and siding for
that particular train, and unless the semaphore came
down the driver would not start. This was a great
advance, and under it the carrying capacity of the lines
was increased by 100 per cent.
* * m *
But all these devices were mere aids to human memory,
and a drunken driver could wreck the whole mechanism.
What the railway mind sought was the system by which,
if every man on the train fell down dead, and every
officer in the signalling cabins also fell down, drunk or
otherwise, the train would be brought to a sudden stand-
still. This was a high ideal, but progress in its direction
is being made. In 1893 tbe Western Liverpool Overhead
Railway introduced the track circuit system, by which
an electric current runs through every engine on the
line. Should the train become derailed, a galvanometer
in the cabs of the trains in front and behind rings a
warning-bell. So far no controlling brake system
has been adopted, but from Maoriland comes
news that the thing has been perfected at last.
The Buckeridge Brothers have, it is said, invented
the ideal system. The whole line is divided into “safety
sections.” Thin copper rails running on insulators rest
between the rails that carry the engine. A trolly-wheel
like that on the Sydney electric trams connects them
with the mechanism of the engine. The copper
rails are not continuous, and have breaks at
regular intervals. On one side there is a long
rail, on the other, alternately, two short ones. The
long rail makes a danger zone, and immediately two
trains come within dangerous distance of one another
they form a complete electric circuit, and from storage
batteries, charged by the turning of the engine wheels, a
current leaps out and blows the engine whistle, extin-
guishes the signal lamp in the engine, and shuts off
steam. Every man on both trains may be dead, but the
current will bring both trains to a standstill, for the
same movement that shuts off the steam lets loose the
brake power. And every train following lets loose a
fresh current to stop itself. That is the last word in
railway signalling up to date.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 11, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The Northcote reception lasted a brief hour,
with a genuflection and a sandwich. The guests,
turned loose in the ball room, the only available
space for a tail-spread, moped about till the band
struck up the anthem and the G.-G. contingent
entered into sight. Then they adopted their
show-case smile and fashion-plate dignity. The
appearance of the two Vice-regal dames was
matter for comparison. The hostess (and greater
Ex.) gleamed royally upon the multitude. As she
passed up the room, nodding with blended
ceremony and cheeriness from side to side, her
emeralds and diamonds sent out a myriad twinkles.
Behind, the tall black figure of Dame Talbot reared,
with a cold electric gleam showing from her gray
height. And she jerked her head loftily now and
then—an amused and rather cynical smile on her
face. It seemed to hurt the lady of Stonington to
walk in the rear.
Official marms and their chicks, wearing dull
garb and a pained expression, were most numer-
ous at the reception. Just now and then a flut-
terer like Miss Whiting, a dazzler like Miss
Atherton, a flower-girl beauty like Miss Lynette
Molesworth, shot on the grateful view. Other
times, one looked in vain for resplendence. Mrs.
Albert Miller was a Polar study—all white from
crown to toe. Mrs. Dyce Murphy showed off her
magnificent carriage with a suggestion of mischief
in her eye to correct its unrelenting rigor. Miss
Drummond, the ewe-lamb browsing at the Federal
V.-R. eyrie at present, was a gentle St. Agnes
study in vestal white and a centre hair-parting
that suggested pious, uplifting thoughts. Mrs.
N. Lockyer, just over from Sydney with her
Collector spouse, who prepares to play chief of
the Commonwealth Customs, was an unpreten-
tious figure in salmon pink. As Miss Wollaston,
in the spinster past, she struck a cheerier dress-
note.
After contemplating Oom Bill Lyne and Big-
john Forrest in Court dress, and getting the
quartet of bulging pink calves in serious focus, I
decided that any sensible man of bulk would
refuse a portfolio if the future were to bring such
a rig upon him. At the Northcote party, the two
hulking figures footed it in a track of laughter.
The Tooraketty element look in chagrin and
despair upon a supply of good-looking aides, who
remain, when off the duty-chain, still out of reach.
Ibe present V.-R. caddies are not taking any
rakish joy of the hour ; and therefore the claws
of the angered Chinchillas grow long.
The mildest miss housed at Government House
for many seasons is Miss Rawson, whose black
garb of woe has a rustle of grief as she walks.
Some mourning habiliments have a jarring /row-
frou, like a requiem service chanted by a vaude-
ville chorus in tights. Miss Rawson’s black rig
h is the sadness of the hateful cypress.
N.S.W. Governor Rawson and his daughter
have learned something about felt-hat making in
Melbourne. The manager of the Austral Hat
Mills lured them to the scene of manufacture,
and talked to them about Australian goods and
anti-Australian prejudices whilst showing them
round. Having been quite convinced that hats
as good as ever came into the country are daily
coming out of it, Gov. Rawson and the aide,
likewise the young lady, left their measures at
the mills for a number of hats.
All Bohemian Melbourne, and Melbourne that
thinks itself Bohemian, turned up to show its legs
at the Artists’ Ball last week, and the unofficial
first prize fell to a dainty little person whose
funereal costume revealed a pair of nether some-
things that excited raving admiration from artistic
and unartistic alike. The costumes were, on the
whole, wonderful examples of economy, financial
and material, and showed how far the taste of a
more or less artistic crowd is ahead of that of the
crowd that is only less artistic. The dresses of
the society function, at the Glaciarium a few
nights before, probably averaged £2 apiece more
than those at the Artists’ Ball, yet, in com pari-
son, looked like the wardrobe of a stranded barn-
storming company in the noonday sun. If the
Artists had any business capacity, they would
hold their annual carnival in the Melb. Town
Hall, and reap many shekels by charging the
general public 2s. 6d. for looking on. But, if they
had any business capacity, they wouldn’t be
artists.
Does millinery pay ? asks a man. Does it pay ?
He’s a bachelor, and evidently has never lost a
toque bet, or he’d know. An instance of the for-
tunes in the business : Melbourne’s crack bonnet-
builder, queening it over an extensive and lavish
clientele, was recently offered a partnership in a
smart chiffon business, and accepted it. The firm,
whose millinery branch had increased in import-
ance and multiplied in custom under her cute
charge, held out various inducements and offered
almost a Cabinet Minister’s pay if she would re-
main. But the level-headed little expert guesses
she has a fortune in the new enterprise, and has
started under a significant golden sign to build
top-masts for beauty on her own.
From a recent smart novel:—
At a dinner party, after the ladies returned to the
drawing-room, the hostess, her broad expanse tinkling
with diamonds, leaned back in a great tufted chair and
shivered slightly. A footman went in search of the lady’s
maid. “ Franeoise,” said the magnet’s lady, with languid
magnificence, “ I feel chilly ; bring me another diamond
necklace.”
The Landales are coming back to the parent
roost in the Tooraketty quarter. In the Big
Smoke the skittish sisters were lost in the fog or
somewhere adjacent. They were sighted for a
fraction of a Piccadilly moment or a Strand spasm
by one or two inky scouts, and then no more. In
this capital they will be as glittersome phosphor-
escence in a slate-grey sea.
Every tongue is wagging over the right or
wrong of Mrs. Staughton’s action in letting her
Saturday party stumble along without her—the
absentee hostess putting in a more resplendent
hour at the greater Gov’ment House. Guests
were not informed of the dame’s plan. They
arrived, asked for the hostess, and were informed
in a glib speech by Mary Ann that the Missus
was dining with the Northcotes, but would they
please walk inside, and have some musical sand-
wiches scraps of Chopin between coffee-wafers ?
In some indignant instances, they didn’t walk
inside, but left the hostless spree to its own
random course. Others went in, looked round,
told each other what they thought, and revenged
themselves at supper.
Mrs. Staughton defends herself first with the
statement that an invitation from the top Exes,
counts as a command, and secondly with the
excuse that it wasn’t an ordinary party she
abandoned, but a mere fraternizing shivoo for the
National Leaguers.
Ihe Austral Salon raises its still small voice
now and then as a protest against the rumor that
it is an extinct body. The other day Lady Talbot
was lassoed and secured for a demonstration.
There were painted and wordy addresses for the
outgoing secretary and treasurer, and these went
to the industrious deservers by way of the Vice-
regal hand.
An experienced bridegroom (he prepares for a
third mate) tells me he has discovered a jeweller
who is an artist at the selling of the engage-
ment circle. It appears that this young
man speaks very softly, and has a way of
treating the demand for a ring with such
reverential respect that the customer, half-
ashamed of his commercial views of life, is
led into an expensive deal. The salesman
drops his alert “What next, please?” manner,
and repeats, ever so softly, “An engagement
ring?’ and, after a pause, “ Yes,” or any other
old monosyllable that expresses a gentle reply to
much active argumentative thought. “An
engagement ring! ” he murmurs a second time,
and in such a seductive, insinuating manner that
the plighter glows from his hat-brim to his boots,
and tells himself, “By gad! yes; it is a big
thing in one’s life.” And so, with all this soft
speech, and gentle fuss, and reverential manner,
the purchaser buys a 22-guinea affair instead of a
five-guinea one, and goes out of the shop feeling
he has done an heroic thing. This experienced
’groom has just done the heroic thing for the third
time.
There’s no doubt that an ingratiating salesman
accomplishes big things while the surly counter-
hand loses trade. The she-customer who fusses
and gets a surly shop-hand into a Sphinxy state of
stony silence and glare, just bangs out of the shop
in a temper, and tells her hubbie who wants, say,
socks, that she wouldn’t buy them to please the
sulky cat in the shop. “ But they were the kind
I want,” pleads poor Mr. Brown. “Of course—
the very identical,” puts in Mrs. B.; “ but do you
think I’d buy them from that insolent baggage.
I’d see you wearing none at all, rather.” And
she would. Nothing could persuade her to take
any other view of the matter. On the other hand,
should a nice, smiling, sympathetic salesman have
listened in a charmed way to all her irrelevant
chatter of the children, and the cook and the
weather, Mrs. Brown would have bought up a
dozen and a-half pairs of brown socks, and cried
incontinently if Brown had refused to wear them.
Miss Elmhirst Goode, touring Europewards,
sends word in sweet humorless fashion that “it’s
so nice on board. We have no musical people
but our own lot, and you’d never know we were
musical —we all sounded so horrible when sick in
the Bight.”
Mrs. Ernest Raleigh, as a prelude to Mrs. Jack
Fisher’s fancy-rig hop, on Thursday night, ban-
quetted a dozen, at the Grand, with any amount
of floral fuss. It was mostly a pink-and-blue
scheme, and, as Keats puts it, “ wild rose-
trees pavilioned them in bloom.” It’s a great
notion to crowd a scene with flowers. The gaiety
of a dinner is almost assured by a profligate dis-
play of festive blossoms.
In the midst of a week full of song and endless
callers, Dolores snatched a day for a frolic. It
took the form of a jaunt to the show of live stock,
harvesters, and curly-tailed pig at Elemington.
With the beaming eye, the rosy cheek, the glad hope
of a child, the “star” took her treat. She had
never been to an Agricultural Show in her melo-
dious life, nor come into contact with pink pigs
or white Shorthorns, though raised herself—she
says laughingly—in a rural quarter of la Idle
France. So Dolores turned over the fleece of the
Shropshire rams, and stroked the smooth, black
noses of the Ayrshire champions, and fell into
raptures over a litter of 10 pink pigs, saying to
Mr. Tait —“Look, Mr. Manager, there is a full
house ” —and was stricken speechless with admira-
tion at the 26cwt. of colossal cream Shorthorn
bull, with mandarin ears and golden-red eyebrows
and a creamy silk floss giving delicacy to the
ruminating head. It was delightful to enjoy the
fresh fun of this experienced artiste with a
youthful capacity for a frolic, and to study
draught stock and white breeds of pig in such
joyous company.
The cruellest experience at the Elemington
Agricultural Show was found in the tent of
plenty (?), where the tea was pitched at one hap-
hazard, and sometimes landed on the lap, and,
more often overflowed from the cup, and rocked
threateningly in the saucer. It was a severe test
for feminine serenity ; yet Dolores suffered the
rough condition of things with an unruffled coun-
tenance. That’s one of the singer’s charms, her
perfect patience. Dolores must be a philosopher
—a thing rare for a public pet. When some wag
suggested that she should go and try some voices-
in the pig-pen, where several baby pigs squeaked
in different keys, the star shook with good-
natured laughter.
A mining magnate, suspected of matrimonial
intentions toward a lady of a long term of widow-
hood, did the Flemington Show with the widow,
and took such an interest in pickled preserves,
and such domestic exhibits, that he can no
longer hope to stem the tide of inflowing con-
gratulations.
A curious thing about the stranded Australiennes
in the Bull capital, says Dolores, is the fact that
they prefer to starve, as hopelessly as a rat in a
jeweller’s show-case, rather than return to the
land they came from. To several failed singers
who have appealed for assistance to Dolores, it
has been pointed out that there would be room
for some sort of work, even outside of music, in
this fat continent for the failed performer. She
has asked the applicant to think of returning and
to come to her, when decided, for assistance.
“But they never come back to me,” says Dolores.
“They will not return to a living in this great
country of theirs—they’d rather starve on in a
city that doesn’t want them.”
On Saturday the Cream found a sudden novel
charm in kiddies. It had turned en masse from
poodle interests and the usual afternoon card-
flutter to the contemplation of infant strutters on
Her Majesty s boards. During the progress of
“La Poupee,” the flabbergasted Cream broke into
ecstasies. “ What a duck ! What an absolutely
cute dear ! What a little love ! ” It made me
think of the recent Kennel Show, and the excla-
mation affectionate of all Society when it beheld
the prize pug or the Pomeranian champion. The
vice-regal of Stonington—herself a better judge of
a dogthanababy—lookedonatthe skilled juveniles
with amazement and a lorgnette, and at the in-
terval issued a command for the novice organiser
and stage manageress. When Miss Jeannie Brenan
fluttered into her pen, the grey Ex. was still more
astonished. She had expected to see an advanced
and strenuous battler. Instead, her cold eye was
greeted by quite a girlish flutterer, rather fragile,
somewhat pretty, and altogether youthful. The
Talbot Dame paid much stately praise to the
charmer, and unbent towards the close of her
compliments to a friendly hand-grip. So Miss
Brenan personally must have pleased greatly.
The rigid Ex. has to be most favourably pleased
before she takes a new-comer into her friendly
smiles.
Mrs. Norman Menzies (Miss Dorothy Yane)
looked at the tot of seven summers —Nellie
M‘Carthy—pirouetting and pattering through her
old La Poupee role with a wide wondering smile.
Nothing more naive than this kiddie’s assump-
tion of the star part could be imagined. She
sang of her over-wrought soul with an
elderly air of sorrow and of love, with one small
hand on her beating heart and t’other extended
in earnest appeal, till the males of the audience
vowed a vow to trust no more the sex. whose acting
art is in perfection at seven. The infant M‘Carthy
is a cuddlesome, unspoilt pet off the boards, and
much more concerned about her own black coon
dollie with a sawdust leg missing than the ap-
plause of a multitude like Saturday’s. The boy-
lover—Harold Keig—could give points in senti-
mental struttings to many a professional. He
actually got a thrill into one of Kenningham’s
solo gems.
Mrs. Tom Eairbairn has hied again to Europe
with her giant-hubbie and a sister—Miss Simson.
Mrs. Tom made her last appearance at a
Christian Science talk before setting out. The
skittish little dame has taken very solemnly to
white poodles and C. Science. And she skits not
at all j ust now.
Miss Edie 0 Sullivan, one of the handsomest
girls of Bohemian Melbourne, who was gazetted
missing after the ’Frisco earthquake, has turned
up, well, but temporarily stone-broke, in a refugee
camp. On leaving Melbourne, 18 months ago,
she started a beauty-parlor in ’Frisco, and, doing
well, put all her earnings into the development of
the business. Consequently, when the ’quake
came along she was left stranded. Feminine
Frisco, attired mostly in its nighty, forgot all
about appearances ; so the fair maker of Yenuses
bad for a while to eat at the soup-kitchens, not
being able to raise the price of a cable to her
relatives.

A n , ie , Ce .? f Federal Speaker Holder gave a piano recital
at Adelaide Lyric Club the other night, and out of 250
members who have free entrance and ten guests’ tickets
each during the year, an audience of perhaps 30
turned up. As the player was a non-member, who
had given the recital purely out of good nature
it wasn t a pretty incident. Fact is, the Lyric Club has
become more social than musical during the last few
years, and the average member nowadays is too bored to
sit through a classical programme unless the perpetrator
be a ‘fashionable.” The Holders are too intellectual to
be wildly sassiety.” The damsel in question, who is still
in her teens, is a promising pupil of long-haired Conserva-
tonum Professor Bryceson Trehearne, and got off a
Beethoven - Lizst - Schumann - Chopin-Weber programme
■entirely from memory.
Tis rumored, by the way, that the learned professor
removes to London at the end of the year. He is quieter
now than when he first burst on Adelaide and wrote
weird effusions on “ Brahms my demi-god,” etc., for the
local press. He’ll be missed by musical Adelaide.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Reid’s only consistency has been in writ-
ing bad poetry and advocating pauper labor. His
plea for a sweated population to make manufac-
tures possible under Freetrade conditions was
first made in 1875, was repeated in 1880, and
again in 1905.
The talk was of Adam Smith, and Attor-
ney-General Isaacs chipped in with a smart
thing: “He wrote ‘The Wealth of Nations,’ and
was a Freetrader. We have to consider in these
selfish days the wealth of a nation, our nation,
and must be therefore Protectionists.”
Lyne this time is being attacked in the
Hume by a tandem team, Mr. and Mrs. Gibb, and
the lady, from all accounts, is the better man of
the two. But the electors don’t like the idea of
a candidate being led round like the prize animal
at the show with a ring through his nose.
It doesn’t seem likely that Maoriland will
endorse the preferential trade treaty with Aus-
tralia. The late Seddon entered into the treaty
with the blare of the welcoming band in his ear
and the bubble of the amicable champagne in his
eye, and, according to Maoriland opinion, he
didn’t make a good bargain.
William Lyne is being opposed in the
Greid interest for a N.S. Wales constituency by a
Victorian who isn’t reckoned to have a dog’s
chance for any seat in the State which knows him
best. What a shriek from the Reidites if the
Deakin party or the Labor party imported a
candidate in that fashion to N.S. Wales !
Jack Murray, Vic. M.L.A., upon the
Ministry from which he marched away :
The shadow of the dissolution is on the wall. I got out
of the wet in good time. I was in that cold, sleety atmo-
sphere long enough. It froze me and my political prin-
ciples into a state of torpor, from which I am entirely
recovered.
Which is a poor sort of testimonial to party
government.

I object to you accusing our Joseph Ward of subjecting
our country’s interest to the English tourist. Joe Ward
has provided the brains for this country and commanded
its finance for the last 13 years. He has faults, but one
redeeming virtue is that he is Australasian right down to
his boot-laces.

financially, hasn’t the redeeming virtue. He has
only the pawning vice.— Ed. B.J
BUILDING THE ARK OF REFORM.
As has been since the days of Noah, they will do
nothing- to help themselves until the flood is uncon-
trollable—then they will be tile first to suffer.— Argus,
deploring the deadness of the Citizens’ League.
Freely rumored in the lobby,- last week,
that if, at the police-court hearing against the
persons charged with complicity in the Lands
Department scandals, Peter Close’s memory had
failed again, the Carruthers Government would
have gone out of office. Fortunately for the
Government, Peter’s memory (which seems to be
of the intermittent kind) was in working order,
and he remembered all sorts of things which, he
alleges, happened.
Replying to a question as to whether it
intended to enforce the law against the sale of
newspapers on Sunday, the N.S.W. Carruthers
Government—with one eye on the fact that its
one press supporter in N.S.W now is Sunday
Truth —pointed ou t that to enforce the Sabbatarian
laws as they stood, would be absurd ; would, for
instance, stop all ferry boats and trams. True
and sensible enough. But why isn’t the same
common-sense exercised in regard to the Sabbath
banana and squash ?
N S.W. Assembly doesn’t seem to see it
(that politically blear-eyed body isn’t really
capable of seeing much but a shindy or a dog-
fight these days), yet it should be obvious to any-
one that members of Parliament emphatically
should not act as land agents. They may be as
pure as snow, and the Lands Minister of lily-
white reputation, but the position of a man
putting a land matter before a Minister depend-
ent on his vote for office must be embarrassing.
Charlie Jeffries, Melbourne’s flash boot-
maker, and a member of its city council, has
just returned from a tour round Japan. He went
to Japan not believing in White Australia. He
comes back converted. “ After his experiences,
he would champion the cause of a pure Australia,
even if he had to go without sugar the remainder
of his life,” he told a welcome-back gathering.
Bruce Smith these days has to go to the
debating clubs for an audience. He told the
Y.M.C.A. gabbers the other night:—
First, the speech should evidence the qualify of the
speaker’s mind. Here the keeping of the body in a
healthy state was necessary.
Well, Bruce, physically, is as fat as a prig. Pre-
sumably that shows a fat mind.
The Deakin Government’s contingent voting
scheme has been abandoned ; and it’s no great loss
in itself. But what does matter is that the next
elections will now be carried out without any
provision for a second ballot or other safeguard
against minority representation. A Contingent
Voting Bill to have had the effect of a second ballot
would have, doubtless, gone through; the Deakin
Bill foundered through being overloaded with
“scientific voting” fads.
“Loco-Fitter,” in defence :
Re your par (23/8/'OC), why call the Queensland railway
system a “shoddy” one? Queensland has been making
its own locomotives for years, being ahead of N.S.W. in
that respect. The loco, shops at Ipswich are the most
up-to-date below the Equator, being run by electricity.
Certainly, the narrow gauge is a drawback, but if it were
4ft. or sft. 3in., would that prevent any stores
scandals occurring? And a point worth noting is that
Queensland has the best record on earth as far as acci-
dents are concerned.
Airy persiflage from the Reps. Dr.
Maloney referred to Kelly as “the youth with
the Oxford drawl and the Whitechapel crawl.”
Joe Cook' interfered on behalf of his protege.
Then Maloney : “I would ask my bald-headed
friend to be quiet.” (Baldness is Cook’s sore
point.) “You are an insulting little man to-
night,” he responded, stingingly. “ You are an
insulting pup,” Maloney replied. Almost like a
State Parliament.
Can anything bad enough be said of Sydney
Telegraph, G. Iteid, and Joe Cook, who are still
croaking dismay and disaster over the new mail
contract, simply because it was the Deakin Gov-
ernment that entered into it ? Whatever they can
say the thing is done, and, had the Telegraph
and its supporters any patriotism, they would
pray that for Australia’s sake it would turn out
all right. Should it fail, the Deakin Government
won’t suffer, as the Government’s fate will be
settled before the contract starts. Yet nothing is
plainer than that, if the Preetrade crowd had the
power, it would joyfully wreck the first
mail steamer that arrives in Australia under
the Commonwealth flag. As it happens, success
and not failure seems assured. The contractors
have already started to build two steamships
larger than the largest merchant vessel that has
hitherto entered Port Jackson, and the London
shipping press contains laudatory notices of the
good fortune of the Commonwealth. But the
Telegraph push still snorts and fumes and cusses.
The Seddon-Deakin preferential trade
agreement has been greeted
with a weird variety of curses
by the Freetrade party ; but,
up-to-date, the curse of Puls-
ford is the most vigorous and
comprehensive. Pulsford dis-
covers a “reason for shame”
in it, to begin with; and his
“reason” is the old, old
foreign-trade one—that the
manufacturer in Potsdam or
Burmah may be injured. True
to the old anti-Australian,
importing instinct, the whole
of Pulsford’s limited mental
gaze has been turned on the
manufacturers outside Austra-
lia. Not once, in the course of a
column contribution to the
Argus, does the insensate Puls-
ford mention that there is any
chance of an Australian manu-
facturer benefiting—as Seddon
and Deakin intended—by the
new arrangement; in fact, so
far as Pulsford is concerned,
there is no such thing as an
Australian factory ; or, if there
is, it is not worth considering.
The squatters, some of the farmers, and
anti-Soshers generally of the New England
(N.S.W.) Federal electorate howl dismally about
Watson’s proposed land tax, but they look at
Lonsdale’s single tax proposals with the blind
eye. Do they know that Watson is sincere, and
that his land tax is within the region of practical
politics, and that Lonsdale is insincere, and his
single tax merely flam ?
Premier Carruthers proposes to absorb
the N.S.W. Statistician’s Department in his new-
fangled “ Intelligence Department.” This will
save him the necessity of deciding whether
W. H. Hall should be appointed Govt. Statis-
tician. Ever since the sectarian rumpus
which centred round this officer, Carruthers has
been in a dilemma as to what to do, and this con-
temptible dodging of the difficulty is character-
istically Carrutherish. There is an obvious
objection to absorbing the Statistical Depart-
ment —which is supposed to have impartial and
judicial functions —with the Intelligence Depart-
ment, which is formed to “boom” the State with
as little sacrifice of truth as possible.
The ignorance of Joe Cook set his Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) constituency in a cackle of derisive
laughter the other day. Having told a meeting
of orchardists how, in pursuance of his staunch
Freetrade and anti-Soeialistic convictions, he had
pleaded hard during the Bounties Bill debate for
a bonus on fruit-growing, he got down to Deakin’s
preferential trade proposals with Maoriland. He
explained in a deep, “ alone-I-did-it ” voice that
immediately he heard that oranges had been left
out of the agreement he had bearded the savage
Lyne in his lair, and asked that oranges be in-
cluded. He had pleaded for oranges ; argued for
oranges; done his very best for oranges. And
then, just when Cook was proceeding to hang a
perfervid denunciation of the Ministry on the
limb of an orange tree, the hoarse voice of one,
weary with shooing the woolly aphis in the dewy
morn, came from the back: “But oranges enter
Maoriland free already.” Then a very foolish-
looking Cook sat down, and the meeting laughed
and laughed till every codlin moth in the district
thought an earthquake was coming.

or subscription to any society, club.
hen convention, sports organisa-
tion, or individual cadger m
his electorate at any time.
That would protect mem-
bers against the blackmail-
ing demands which now
absorb so much of their
allowances.

half as much faith in the
“vast natural resources”
and the “great primary
industries” as he pretends
to have, or he is a plunder-
ing humbug. For, while
he will lend money to the
miserable little Sydney
suburban council at from
3 to 4£ per cent., he very
often condemns the
country municipality,
which has all the great
natural resources behind
it, to pay over 6 per cent.
Newtown, Petersham, and
North Sydney have bor-
rowed at 4 per cent, or
under; Armidale, Bathurst
and Goulburn are all pay-
ing from C to 7 per cent.,
and a few places are pay-
ing even more. The vast
natural resources and the
great primary industries
are all very good pap for
Fat to feed fools on ; but
they evidently aren’t reckoned anything as se-
curity for Fat to lend money on.
The effect of the legal and political cam-
paign to smother the N.S.W. Arbitration Act is
shown in the withdrawal of unions from regis-
tration. So little of the Act is left that the
unions see no advantage in its shelter. Toryism
will one day be wakened trom its snug com-
placency at this “ desirable consummation ” by a
gigantic strike.
Bent’s latest kite, flown at the luncheon
of the Agricultural Show, is an “ agricultural
parliament,” whatever that may be, to be composed
of a lower house of practical farmers and an upper
house of “scientific men, who know all about
patent manures and those things.” What the
parliament was to do, and what the fat persons
were to obstruct, Thomas did not explain. Still,
the crowd of Collins-street farmers, who run the
show, cheered wildly. The fact that there was to
be an “ upper house ” was good enough for them.
For hours the debate on the harvester
duties proceeded in the House of Reps, last week
with decorum, until Joe Cook walked into the
Chamber. In 40 seconds what had been a quiet
assembly was an angry bear-garden. Cook is the
centre of discord in the House. He is never
good-tempered, never pleasant, never anything
but acrid, spiteful, and snarling. He imports
into every debate in which he takes part a degree
of bitterness and temper that is never present
without him. No insinuation is too mean for
him ; no motive too unworthy to impute to an
opponent.
“ S.R.” : So Tom Glassey, father and one
time leader of the Queensland Parliament, is now
a life-assurance representative (B. 28/8/’06)? Well,
he has himself to blame. Even as late as the last
Senatorial elections the Queensland Labor party
held out the olive branch to Glassey, and he
refused to accept it. Glassey’s defection has
caused many a pang to honest Democrats. There
is something pathetic in the spectacle of the old
leader peevishly estranging himself from the
friends and the idc-als of his youth. Adversity is
the backbone with some natures; prosperity
unnerves them.
The Nepean—the newly-added electorate
in N.S.W.— is likely to provide a battle among
political derelicts. Hawthorne, who “whipped”
for Reid in days long gone by, is out under cover
of the Australian Democratic Union ; “Tommy”
Taylor is an alleged Liberal and Anti-Soshite, but
refuses to give his name to the care of the “ Cen-
tral Executive,” and won’t retire for anybody;
and John Waugh—a third Anti-Soshite—has
suffered defeat down the South Coast way in the
Parliamentary fight. T. R. Smith, ex-M.L.A.,
and brother of Sydney Smith, is the Protectionist
hope in the scramble.
The Deakin Government, having passed
the Commerce Act, which can prevent the com-
petition of foreign shoddy with Australian manu-
factures ; the Anti-Trust Act, which can smother
the unfair competition of foreign Trusts, and can
stop dumping; the Bounties Act, which will
encourage new industries ; and having raised the
tariff wall as regards two sorely threatened indus-
tries, spirit distillation and the manufacture of
agricultural machinery—lets the work of Protec-
tion rest there for this Parliament. There is still
an enormous amount to be done, but, in view of
the captious opposition of the Reidites, no more is
possible. And those'same Reidites now howl
bitterly. They opposed each step of tariff reform
as it was proposed. Now they ask why the whole
tariff should not be settled this session, and thus
leave no tariff issue for the elections !
Last week about 13 aged greybeards met
in the Melbourne Town Hall and solemnly made
speeches to one another about Victoria’s “claim”
to the Riverina. In the chair was Joe Panton,
P.M., who becomes more feeble every day.
These old derelicts passed a resolution up-
holding Victoria’s “rights,” and constituting
themselves into the Victorian Boundary League,
to pile up bills of costs in the effort to snatch the
Riverina from the jaws of N.S. Wales. They
even went so far as to contribute ss. each towards
this noble end. And all of the greybeards had
“ evidence ”to bring forward. A sample of this
evidence was the hazy recollection of one ancient
that, “before he came out here, he had read in
Scotland, or somewhere, a book about Victoria.
He thought there was a map in that book, and if
that book could be found the map would settle
the matter. He didn’t know what the book was,
but remembered that it cost a shilling, and had a
chocolate-colored cover.” The league after that
should feel quite sure of success.
THE TOLL GATE.
Mr. William Lyne having declared himself in favor of
a toll on bachelors, the fund to be held in trust for the
benefit of unmarried women, spinsters throughout the
States, recognising a good idea, display some anxiety to
“ push it along.”
It would be such an inexpensive tax, too. All
that would be necessary would be to allow a
spinster to put up a toll gate wherever she pleased
and collect her own dues.
Premier Bent’s promise to have £50,000
worth of wire-netting made in Pentridge gaol now
appears to have been mostly bluff, but a flax-
treating plant has been installed in the big prison
and a nine-acre crop of the fibre grown on the
Government farm ; besides this, the Prisons De-
partment notifies farmers that it is prepared to
treat their crops or buy them outright. The
industry is said to be a profitable one, and easily
carried out inside gaol walls, and, so far, nobody
has said anything about interference with Private
Enterprise.
A recent Melb. Argus article, dealing with
“The Stirring Past,” tries to perform the
miracle of depicting the great Victorian land
struggle of the sixties, without making the
slightest reference to the Constitutional Associa-
tion, brought into existence to provide mint
sauce for legislators. It observes a solemn
silence as to the nefarious doings of the big land
thieves and banking magnates, who conspired to
pollute the law at its fountain-head, by bribing
the law-makers; and hasn’t a syllable to say
about the ill-famed Quieting of Titles Bill. It is
a wonderful article for the things it doesn’t say.
As soon as Greater Sydney is talked of,
the tin-pot suburban alderman at once rises
to warn the people of his pocket-handker-
chief borough (there are 41 boroughs in
the Greater Sydney area) that the suburban
voters will be overpowered and swamped by
the city electors. Newtown is the particular
borough in which the latest warning note was
sounded. The figures of enrolment in 1904 were :
Sydney, 37,500 electors ; suburban, 70,664. The
suburban aldermanic yarn is evidently that the
70,000 are about to be overpowered and swamped
by the 37,500. It’s the story of “poor N.S.W.”
and Federation over again.
Gallery pressmen in the Australian
Senate have been growling for a long time about
the stuffy atmosphere pervading their den, and
the other night, at 10.20, Professor Osborne, of
Melbourne University, looked in and pocketed a
few specimens of the smellful substance. Analysis
revealed 27‘7 per 10,000 of carbon dioxide—a per-
centage of gas 17'7 per 10,000 above what the
Professor reckons it healthful for any man to work
in. On this pronouncement the President rose in
haste and ordered the glass ventilators of the
building to be opened, and now the asphyxiated
ink-slingers are asking between gasps why that
simple thing wasn’t done long ago.
S.M. Herald still barracks hard for an
imported Railway Commissioner. The conditions
in Canada and the United States, so it says,
“have bred their own type of railway man.”
Just so ! A type of man who can be relied on to
implicitly obey a millionaire boss to keep down
traffic and permanent way expenses at cost to the
health, lives, and limbs of employees. We know
that type of man quite well. The Yankee mil-
lionaire has bred him to perfection. He is one of
the products of that perfected system of private
enterprise which has generated the millionaire
himself. But Australian railways don’t exist
solely to earn dividends; they are designed also
to serve the public interest and convenience.
