SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Sydney Metropolitan Rugby campaign
came to a virtual end on Saturday last, when Glebe
overthrew Newtown, on University Oval, by 16 points to
eight. Newtown having won the toss, it' was expected
that, with the wind behind, it would pile up a big score
of points during the first half, and then play a sound
defensive game in the second, and let Glebe do all the
bullocking. Theoretically the idea was superb, but
Glebe put up such a strenuous struggle that New-
town, in the first half, only managed to score a
couple of tries, one of which it converted. Glebe
crossed the line once. With the change of ends,
Glebe attacked Newton with fury, and play became
very rough. Newtown was simply overwhelmed,
and failed to score, while Glebe ran up to 13. Had New-
town triumphed, Eastern Suburbs would have been a
dangerous rival in the final—if it won its match against
Sydney. But things all worked together for the good of
Glebe, and while it stamped Newtown underfoot, Sydney
was pounding Eastern Suburbs to pieces at the Sports
Ground. Eastern Suburbs seemed stale, and, with the
exception of the brilliant Messenger, decidedly off-color.
On the other hand, Sydney was unusually smart, and full
back O. F. O. Wharton played the game of his life.
* * * *
■Although in all probability Glebe and Sydney
District will play an alleged final, which will no doubt
draw a crowd and make for huge excitement, it will not
affect the destination of the premiership, which belongs
to Glebe, with 28 competition points. Glebe gained the
honor outright in 1900, and in 1901 divided with Uni-
versity.
» « * •
In the Second Grade competition Glebe has
been very successful, having won the junior premiership
four times outright, and shared it with University once.
This year it fell to Marrickville.
* « « «
Newtown and Refern put up a great struggle at
Sydney Agricultural Ground under Australian Rules, on
Saturday, for the possession of the Rawson Oup. Over
4000 people looked on and roared themselves hoarse (the
people who deplore the tendency of 5000 people to sit and
watch 36 others play football forget the valuable lung-
exercise that the bulk of the 5000 get—it does them
almost as much good as playing in the ruck, and is less
dangerous). Winning the toss, Newtown took advantage
of the wind, which helped the shooting somewhat and
handicapped that of Redfern. It is when there is a wind
that the Australian rule of playing the game in four
quarters makes things much more even. But when
Redfern got the use of the wind in the second quarter, it
failed to make any use of it, and Newtown continued to
score rapidly, arriving at half-time with 30 points to 6.
Redfern played a hard, resolute game, but Newtown had
too much skill, and against its combination the brilliant
individual play of the Redferners came to nothing. The
Rawson Oup was won by 62 points to 20.
None of the Vic. League matches counted on
Saturday, as after the day’s proceedings the four clubs
which had occupied the rearmost positions all along—
Souths, Saints, Geelong, and Melbourne —retired from
business till May, 1907. Seeing that the last-named has
won but one match out of 17 played, its retirement
seems to have been unduly delayed. The usual Saturday
crowds either went to the Show, or patronised the Foots-
cray v. Williamstown Association souffle, the winner of
which got into the semi-finals. Up to half-time it was
an exciting skirmish, full of strenuous, if ill-regu-
lated ardor—a game of the give-and-take order ;
but shortly after the spell ’Town lost a couple of players,
one with a broken ankle, and thence on Footseray
had the better of the tussle, finally winning by 64 points
to 34. This leaves West, Richmond, North, and
Footseray to battle for the pennant —and the gate.
The most joyful afternoon’s sport, however, occurred
at Victoria Park, where the Junior Association “chal-
lengers,” Yarraville. met Northcote for Wren’s shield and
badges. The recent Squires and Murphy argument was
perhaps more scientific, but it was certainly no more
willing. The 4000 spectators, mostly of the “ bloke ” and
Lizzie persuasion, spoke familiarly and firmly to the
members of the teams and to each other. There were
two hefty “ scraps” on the ground, and fully half-a-dozen
among the “ blokes,” and colors, flags, and badges were
torn off and strewn around recklessly. The most pains-
taking and competent man on the ground was the
umpire ; but for him the argument would have de-
veloped into a riot long before half-time. As it was,
Northcote’s breaches of the rules were somewhat less
frequent than Yarraville’s, wherefore the frequent free
kicks awarded them enabled them to score 49 points to
Yarraville’s 29. These “Juniors”—many of them as old
and as weighty as League players—are as eager and lusty
as any exponents of the game, but someone ought to im-
press it on them that a first essential of football playing
is a knowledge of the rules ; that charging, slinging and
tripping are not contemplated therein ; and that the be-
ginning, middle and end of the success of an all-conquer-
ing team is unselfishness. The essence of the game
is to acquire the ball, clearly and cleverly', then to pass
it on immediately and directly' to the man in front in the
most feasible position. That makes for victory, and vic-
tory means kudos and gate. A few may at times
applaud the “ gallery ” player for a brilliant run, but it is
the unselfish, combined play that makes for science, skill,
and victory. As yet these “Juniors” don’t know the
rudiments of the game.
Competition for the Gardiner Cup, the Holy
Grail of the Sydney “Soccer” game, progresses slowly.
On Saturday last Pyrmont and Balmain wriggled and
tussled, and sweated and bullocked, but all in vain. On
either side the defence was too sound, and the game
ended in a scoreless draw, leaving things as they were.
* * * *
Dunedin is stirred to its heart core. A game of
British Association football has actually drawn a very
fair crowd, and, worse still, the play moved the spectators
to wild applause and general enthusiasm. Not since “a
kist of whustles ” was first placed in the stronghold of
true-blue Presbyterianism, has anything stirred Dunedin
like this.
« * * *
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers made a sorry
exhibition of himself over the latest cricket develop-
ments. His wire to the Marylebone Club was quite
irresponsible, the Board of Control knowing nothing of
it. Although the Marylebone Club called a special
meeting to deal with the cablegram, it was merely an act
of courtesy to what they think is Joey’s exalted position.
It could do nothing else than adhere to its former decision,
which had been communicated to the Board of Control.
The Club had received no invitation from the Board of
Control to reconsider its decision, and had it arranged to
send a second-rate team out in response to the Premier’s
request (its representative team is pledged to S’Africa),
the Board of Control would have.been perfectly justified in
declining to recognise it. And what a fool the Club
would have been to place itself in that position, just to
give Joseph a little cheap advertisement among the
supporters of a section of the players. It was a silly
intrusion on the part of Joseph.
• • • «
Now whispered that the Carruthers cable to
Marylebone Club was instigated by a prominent player,
who was desperately anxious to impress the Board of
Control with the occult string-pulling powers possessed
by himself and his colleagues. Now that the last forlorn
hope has failed, there is, it is said, bitter recrimination
among the lately suspended players,, and sundry threats
of “letting the cat out of the bag ’ are being bandied
about. In the name of Heaven, how many cats were in
that bag?
The feature of Sydney Tatt’s. meeting, at
Randwick, Saturday, was Maoriland mare Solution’s
brilliant win of the Rawson Stakes. She gave nothing a
chance at the right end of the argument, and simply
romped away from such forefronters as Maniapoto, Scot
Free, Collarit, Antonious, and Famous. Solution’s
manner of qualifying for the coin was reminiscent of the
way in which Mr. Stead’s nags, from the same section,
did the trick on the same track last year. How any one
of those behind her on Saturday is going to heat Solution
in the Metrop. is a puzzle, for she will then meet most of
them on better terms. Another notable thing was
imported Pompous’ triumph in Tramway Handicap
(6fur.). He came out near the finish and squelched
Epsom favorite, Magazine—and this after a comparatively
very poor showing over a mile at Warwick Farm the
Saturday before. Remarkable in another way was
Kveadgerie’s gathering of Novice Handicap and atten-
dant huge haul from Ikey Moses. His trainer (Frank
McGrath) rarely makes a mistake, and the money went
on in hatfuls. ' Kyeadgerie is beautifully bred by Sir
Foote (imp.) from Witchery, and he scored in a way that
suggests him as more than a possibility for coming
Epsom. Winning the Spring Handicap, Noreen did well
enough to justify her position in the Metropolitan bet-
ting ; but it looks all over, bar shouting, if Solution can
repeat Saturday’s display.
* * * *
Seems certain now that Lord Ullin’s Daughter
has got over the trouble that gave her Melbourne Cup
backers such a bad scare a week or two ago. Last week
she galloped twice or thrice in good style, and pulled up
without the least sign of lameness. Trainer J. Allsopp
says the trouble wasn’t anything like as serious as at
first sight appeared.
* * • *
At last meeting of the Q.T.C. committee a
motion to abolish bookmakers was declared lost on the
voices. The requisite number of votes being in support
of the proposal a ballot was demanded at the next club
meeting. But it is not likely that the club (which
numbers several members connected with bookie syndi-
cates) will pass so useful a measure. Mr. Hewitt gave
the committee meeting the opinions (cabled) of several of
the leading clubs of M.L., all strongly favorable to the
tote.
• « * *
Maoriland, too, is beginning to feel bad in its
wickedness, and meetings of men with the monkey-shave,
and old ladies of both sexes, are discussing the advis-
ability of wiping out the totalisator. These dear, old,
ignorant souls are shocked to find that investments on
the totalisator have swelled from £506,078 in 1892 to
£1,437,431 in 1905. The explanation is, of course, that
Maoriland is, in the first place, about five times better
off than it was in 1892, when its population was pour-
ing out of the country because the anti-Socialistic,
private enterprise-worshipping Atkinson Government
had brought the country to the verge of ruin
Now it is very prosperous. Another reason for the
increase is that under its improved rules the totalisator
gamble is much fairer and better than anything that the
surreptitious bookmaker can offer, so that private
betting is on the decrease. If the totalisator is abolished
it means the bookmaker will return, and the State will
lose £21,571 revenue yielded for charitable purposes by
the totalisator-tax. A curious thing is that while the
investments have increased so enormously, ohe number
of licenses issued has dropped from 234 in 1893 to 156 in
1905. Another curious fact is that nobody has so far
thought of trying to abolish gambling on horse-racing by
prohibiting the sport on which the gambling takes place.
Why this laxity on the part of the sacred Josephs?
* * * *
Maoriland Premier Ward has been threatening
to do something desperate in order to cope with the
“gambling evil.” He says he is going to amend the law
in a manner which will make it impossible to send money
to any place where a race meeting is in progress,
to prohibit the publication of betting news within
a month, to prevent the circulation of leaflets and bills
from Tattersall and other sweep promoters, to isolate
racecourses from telegraphic and telephonic communi-
cation, and, in fact, to do everything to suppress
betting on horse-racing, barring suppressing the
racing itself. Now, why doesn’t Joseph Ward
go the whole hog and make horse-racing a penal
offence? Just imagine the state of affairs when Ward has
done all these things, and when a big city like Christ-
church cannot receive any money orders for half a week
because the Canterbury Jockey Club is holding a three-
days meeting ! Only a man whose Christian name is
Joseph could propose such lunacy. This unreasoning
desire to inconvenience everybody in order to prevent a
few wasters betting on horse-racing has only broken out
since Australasia became afflicted with two Premiers
whose front names are Joseph.
, * * * *
Attorney-General Wade will need to remodel
his Anti-Gambling pill and provide machinery for the
limitation of rabbit-skinning contests. This new and
charming pastime is now booming in the trapping dis-
tricts, and so fierce is the gambling on the events that
many of the trappers and their families have been reduced
to beggary by backing Bill in the Kitten Handicap. Will
Wade please do something?
* * * *
The Q. Cricket Association has held its
opening meeting. Amongst other things, it has put
its foot upon peregrinating Trumpers and other indivi-
duals who exploit the game for gate. Even clubs must
submit their proposal for visits from clubs outside the
State. Country cricket is to receive more attention.
* * * *
Said that Charlie White, perhaps the most
brilliant, and certainly the unluckiest, three-quarter
N.S.W. has produced, could be in a much better condi-
tion physically and financially. It seems to be “upto ”
the Rugby Union to start an insurance or benefit fund for
injured players. Players like White have earned thou-
sands in gate-money, but their share so far has been
more kicks than ha’pence.
* * * *
The Vic. Amateur Billiard Championship
games, which have more interest for the great army of
amateurs than any professional match, are to be played
at Alcock and Co.’s Hall (Melb.) during this week. Some
preliminary test heats resulted in four men being left to
meet one another on American tournament terms, and
the one who wins the most heats will have to try conclu-
sions with Tricks, who captured the championship last
year. Jacoby, a previous winner, is likely to be the
survivor of the quartette, and on form he should just
about beat Tricks in the final.
* * * *
The Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Animals is, for once in a way, deserving its title in
Maoriland by an energetic canvass against pigeon-
butchering matches. A petition of great tonnage is to be
presently rolled before the Government.
• * * *
The New South Wales-Victoria baseball match
appears to have been a howling farce. It ended in a deci-
sive defeat of the Cornstalks by 19 to nine. Of the 16
matches played, N.S.W. has got away with nine, and the
Victorians with seven.
#■*■**■
The Bulletin gets two portraits of O’Hagen-
O’Brien, who is coming to Australia to fight Squires.
One represents gentleman O’Hagen in his immaculate
evening dress, and the other is Jack O’Brien in his
bruising war-paint. The Bulletin never saw a prize-
fighter who looks more like a dude than O’Brien, nor a
gentleman who looks more like a prize-fighter than
O’Hagen.
Word has been received in Sydney from Cana-
dian sculler Durnan, Hanlan’s nephew, that he will leave
home on October Ist for Australia, in order to row Towns
on the Parramatta. He suggested £lOO as a reasonable
amount for expenses, and requested that the race should
take place not later than April, but would prefer an
earlier date if possible. He is bringing his own boat with
him but will have a training shell built in this country.
Towns immediately wired an acceptance, agreeing to the
allowance of £lOO as expenses, and offering to row in
March. So presumably the race will now come off.
Concerning the men who will officially represent
the various States in the Dunlop Warrnambool-Melbourne
road race. Westralia’s man is A. F. Wright, who streaked
over the 116 miles between Beverley and Perth in 6hr.
lOmin. 3Ssec., 2Ssec. better than the previous best for the
same road. Tasmania’s man, J. Edwards, rode from
Launceston to Hobart in 7hr. 51min., which was 58min.
9 ‘2-ssec. worse than the time in which E. Hardman rode
from Hobart to Launceston in 1904. The change of
direction might affect the riding to an enormous extent.
O. H. Brook was to have represented N.S.W., but there
is trouble in the camp, and at time of writing it is under-
stood that neither Brook, Uagney nor Larcombe will go
across to take part in the great race. Which is a pity.
Maoriland used a 112-mile course, from Timaru to
Christchurch, and out of 220 entrants 176 faced the
starter. Of these 42 arrived within half an hour of one
another. In the struggle for fastest time four riders
finished within 34 seconds of each other, big Jack Arnst
being the third. IL Mehrtens, who was on the 25-minute
start, secured fastest time, covering the distance in
6 hours 17min. 16sec., beating J. Arnst by 25 seconds.
But Maoriland is sending across four official representa-
tives, H. Mehrtens, H. Henderson, G. Smith, and J.
Arnst, with the threat that the formidable combination
may be strengthened. The Warrnambool-Melbourne
course is 165 miles long. The great race will be run on
Saturday, 22nd. The entry, 290, is a world’s record for a
long-distance road event.
# * * *
The confidence of Larcombe ! Transpired while
the committee sat to consider a frivolous protest, that
latest Bathurst to Sydney road race winner had, before-
hand, sold the two bikes to be presented to the first man
home and the one accomplishing fastest time, so sure was
he of success. Generally such pride goes before a fall;
in this case it went before a financial rise.
The Bulletin notices with cold disgust that
Queensland Minister for Works O’Sullivan is encouraging
the hare-torturing game in his electorate (Warwick). At
a recent hare-murdering contest there, he gave a silver
cup, valued at eight guineas, as first prize. It suggests
a new item for the Democratic programme—no Demo-
cratic vote to be given to any pigeon-slayer, hare-chaser,
dog-fight person, or any follower, aider, or supporter of
any coarse and beastly sport whatever.
Since the demise of “Donald ” M‘Leod, at the
hands of sundry “welshed,” at Flemington racecourse
(Melb.), much limelight has been shed on welter “ Ted ”
Nelson, the “’ero” who was said to have tried to avert
the tragedy. His presence in the ring at the Cyclorama
building, as a challenger, has been a regular weekly
occurrence and the signal for a certain quantity of ap-
plause. Ted’s hungering for a chance to display his
ability came along, t’other Monday night, when Tom
Dunn, a Westralian, of advertised punching fame, stripped
to administer an awful knock-out that had previously
settled gentleman Jack M'Gowan and other notable
experts. The crowd waited patiently, but the long-
expected “ outer” never came, except in the shape of a
wild, misdirected, ill-timed swing that would have shifted
several Nelsons, provided it had reached the desired
spot. The ’ero gave an artistic display and a superfluity
of style, the work being voted a “boshter” exhibition by
the knowing ones, and blooming flashness by the humble
patrons on the shelves. Ted pranced about like a
ballet girl, dabbing a right hook here and a left
swing there, so that Mister Dunn became a perplexed
pug., and for considerable space occupied the floor in
ungraceful attitudes. Nelson went down early in the
second round from what appeared to writer a recoil,
and remained on the boards for an unrequired nine
seconds. At times the Westralian received urgent re-
quests to take some of the flashness out of his ’ero op-
ponent, but poor Thomas required all his energy to keep
himself on the defensive. The contest devolved into a
sportive spar for Nelson, who merely made things un-
comfortable for his raw opponent instead of closing the
performance. The audience called on referee Basto to
call them off in the tenth round, but as the go never
went within coo-ee of stoush, Basto allowed further pro-
ceedings. In the v following round Ted fairly went for
his man, raining in a shower of bewildering blows that
caused his victim to turn and sprint. Basto, in biled-shirt
attire, declared without hesitation the ’ero winner,
amidst groans, cheers and unpolite remarks. Dunn, in
excuse for his defeat, stated that his valet administered
smelling salts as a thirst quencher (early in the fray),
which bestirred his little Mary.
* * * *
Jim Griffin, the Maoriland middleweight, who
reached Melbourne via Sydney, where he has accounted
for a few of the best, added another win to his list at the
Olympic Club’s Hall, Eastern Hill, last Thursday, meet-
ing Bob Fraser, the well-known local footballer and
pugilist. Fraser is a man hard to punish, but Griffin
punished him by reason of dogged persistence and
unusual power. Fraser is much the cleverer boxer, and
if he had been content to use the footwork that was a
distinguishing feature of his earlier fights, he might have
lasted to get in a finishing punch ; as it was, he had the
Maorilander down for 9sec. on three occasions—in the
first, second, and third rounds —and once Griffin was
saved by the gong. Griffin had the advantage of size,
weight, youth, and strength. He is a smaller edition of
Willie Squires, and fights in very much the same way.
It takes him a very little time to recover from a dizzying
punch, and then he wades in again as gaily as ever. He
was giving Fraser soggy body punches, and in the fifth
the Melbournian began to go to bits. By cleverness in
evasion he kept on end till the eighth, after which his
people handed in the linen, which was wise, for although
Griffin had lost much ginger, he still had a rib-bender in
his right hand.
* * » *
The moral and refined Bent has announced that
his little Bill for the suppression of boxing is now ready
for submission to the Assembly. As this is the day of
the Holy Joe, the Assembly will probably swallow the
thing whole in the long run, but some of the fervent
admirers of citizen John Wren will put up a stiff fight.
There is little doubt that, but for Wren having imported
Mike Williams and O’Hagen-O’Brien from Africa and
U.S.A. respectively, the Holy Joes would not have been
so disturbed in their religious feelings.
* * * *
“ Squidge ” (in protest): For frank, unalloyed
brutality the finals of the Vie. amateur boxing champion-
ships would be hard to beat. Yet Rev. Ernie Hughes was
on the platform, surrounded by dozens of people who
wouldn’t be seen at a go between professionals, because
professional pugilism is so demoralising. All the same,
professional pugs turned up their eyes in astonishment
at the feast of gore and smash and fight served up
by the amateur brigade. One man got his ankle
fractured, struggled to his feet, and then, broken ankle
and all, was bashed back to the floor again. Another
man was woodened out so sickeningly that even ex-
perienced seconds thought for a few minutes that he had
been killed. Those were only the two main incidents of
an orgy of stoush. Because the men were amateurs they
were asked to box only' three rounds, so that the referee
might decide which was the more skilful. That sounded
nice and lady’like, but it worked out the other way. Be-
cause the men had only to last out three rounds they
went at it with all the devil they had in them. The con-
tests were not boxing matches, they were wild whirling
fights in which the men clawed and punched and banged
each other all they knew. Every competitor was ob-
viously trying to knock-out his man. Yet this was the
show that the Amateur Athletic Association implored the
parsons and anti-boxing crowd to come and see. It
turned the stomachs of old seasoned followers of the
game.
* * * *
Crawford Vaughan, M.L.A., whose Bill,
prohibiting pigeon-murder matches, was talked but in
S.A. Assembly last session, is again on the warpath. He
proposes to move for the insertion in the Cruelty to
Animals Protection Bill, now before Parliament, of a
clause making it an offence to shoot pigeons or other
birds at matches, to promote birdicide competitions, or
to supply fowl for such events. Premier Price is whole-
heartedly sympathetic, so the clause has a good chance
of going through. If it does, there will be one decent
State in the Commonwealth, anyhow.
» * * *
The Sydney Ferries, which recently purchased
Chowder Bay old pleasure resort, lately 7 known as Clifton
Gardens, proposes to spend a lot of money 7 in order to
make the place a profitable resort for steamer traffic. A
large swimming bath of novel design is to be built, far
superior to anything else in Australia. Mixed bathing is
not the idea —the Sydney Ferries directorate is too respec-
table to bathe mixed, and never in its life has it plunged
into the water with only 7 a frill on ; but it is intended to
make Chowder the scene of all the local championship
events and other big swimming fixtures. Any way the
voice of the push is to be heard thereabouts no more.

The demand for the best class of blood sires
seems to be pretty active on the other side of the world.
On July 20th tile mailboat Nile left England with over
£lOO,OOO worth of blood horses on board, hound for
Buenos Ayres. First of the sensational cargo was Diamond
Jubilee, winner of the 2000 gns. Derby 7 and St. Leger,
sold by the King to an Argentine breeder for 30,000 gns.
There were also Val d’Or, Jardy and Pietermaritzburg.

“ S.R.”: In view of the weakness of the Q.
Opposition, no wonder Kidston is getting some-
what careless, and bit by bit widening the gap
between his Government and the Labor party.
I cannot shake off the impression that Kidston is
no longer the staunch Democrat, the fighting
leader of the Labor party. Is it prosperity which
is ruining the man’s nature? 1 have watched
Kidston for the past 12 months, and he is drift-
ing, drifting, drifting ; and as he drifts the breach
widens.
Minister Bill Lyne threatened twice last
week to “spank” Willie Kelly. It isn’t politics,
that sort of thing, but the mosquito tactics of
Kelly invite it; and, one day, the tragedy will
happen, and some big politician will administer
correction to Willie in a strictly fatherly way.
Dr. John Quick seems in danger of
making “a King Charles head” of the Tariff
Commission reports. He wishes not only to
establish them as a sort of sacrosanct table of
the law, not to be touched without blasphemy,
but to argue that every political question, and
not alone the tariff, should be decided in the light
of their wisdom. The Tariff Commission has
done good work, and its chairman should have
much credit therefor ; but the fact is likely to be
lost sight of if Dr. Quick persists in an attitude of
querulous devotion to every comma of his reports.
The shooting of Constable Guilfoyle some
six years ago by a criminal named Shaw, in com-
pany with another of the tvpe, named Skidmore,
was recalled last week by the death of Constable
Maher, who, at the time of Guilfoyle’s murder,
was shot in the shoulder by Skidmore. Shaw
subsequently shot a policeman at St. Kilda (Vic.),
and then shot himself. Skidmore, it is shrewdly
surmised, was murdered by Shaw, fearing that he
would be “given away.” Anyhow, Skidmore
has not been captured.

trumpets, and the effect is the same as with the phonograph,
an immensely increased volume of sound. In addition to
this, the Bass Notes are the deepest it is possible to use, and
the Tenor Chords are very full and powerful. It is no
exaggeration to state that the Resonator Trombone Accordeon
mor*- nearly resembles an organ in rhe power and depth of
its tone than an Accordeon. This effect is produced ny the
extra large and heavy reeds, all of which are screwed (not
glued i into position, and the very powerful Eleven-fold Triple
Bellows supply sufficient wind to operate these superior reeds
to the utmost limit of their capacity. The Bellows are strongly
protected by patent metallic corners, ami this, as accordeon
players well know, overcomes the weakness that ruins most
accordeons. The body of the instrument is beautifully ebon-
ised and the metal fittings are well plated and highly bur-

HOLDER is the greatest blessing
gver devised. We all know, alas ! to our
sorrow, the cramped and tired feeling
w<* experience, which makes writing a
disagreeable task instead of a pleasure,
5>nd we know, too, how impossible it is
to get a Penholder to suit the hand.
Now, the “ BERTILLION ” REFORM
PENHOLDER does away with all this—
makes writing a comfort. Everybody can have a pen “ MADE TO ORDER” and to fit their hand like a glove. The end of this penholder is made of a material which,
when dippedlnto hot water, softens." All you have to do, then, is to catch hold of the Pen in the same manner as you are accustomed to write with the most convenience,
and the Pen moulds itself, under the gentle pressure exercised in holding it, into the shape of your fingers, just exactly as you see in the illustration. Now, dip it in cold
water, and it will immediately harden again, leaving the impression or mould of your fingers still on the Pen. Not only will the “ BERTILLION ” PENHOLDER be a
<>-reat convenience, but it will ensure it against being stolen, or readily used by anyone else.

From a man who has been wandering
through the Northern Territory:—
All the talk about Asiatic labor to develop the Terri-
tory is moonshine. Intense cultivation will never be
carried on there. What is required is mixed farming'.
A man with 10,000 or 20,000 acres on the Daly, Katherine
or Adelaide Rivers could rear cattle, Angora goats, pigs,
and fowls, and grow fruit, maize, rice, tobacco,
hemp, cotton, and grain for hay. Aborigines are plenti-
ful, and he does not need to look for Chows or Japs for
labor. The Territory is essentially a country for small
holdings, and to talk like Cole, of the bookshop, about
swamping it with colored people, is rubbish. The Chows
in this land are drones; they only work while you are
watching them, and they get 7s. a day, too. The N.T.
is very different from North Queensland, where there are
hundreds of miles of rich jungle country with splendid
soil fertilized by decayed vegetable matter for centuries.
What they call jungle in the Territory is only scrub.
Develop the mineral resources, and build the railways ;
other things will come after.
The best country in the Territory, from a pastoral
point of view, is the Victoria River. Some few years ago
Captain Bradshaw formed a syndicate with an eye to meat
extract works at the Depot, believing cattle could be sup-
plied to the works at 30s. per head. A chemist with his
wife and family, and an engineer with plant went to the
Depot to make a start, but found cattle could not be had
for less than £4 ; so the affair collapsed.
The runs are immense. Victoria Downs is 12,000
square miles. Talk about breaking up large estates in
the South with a graduated land-tax—what about these
estates, which pay Is. per square mile? Yet the
lot of them don’t employ a score of white men ;
and by penny-wise and pound-foolish policy they lose
hundreds of beasts yearly. If this country were thrown
open for small selectors—say, 50,000 acres—it ought to be
a prosperous district. Every station has a garden, where
fruit and vegetables grow luxuriantly; Mitchell and
Flinders grasses abound, and herbage and scrub are
plentiful. . But even this country can make no headway
when one individual or syndicate holds an area the size of
Ireland.
A true bill from Codville (Vic.). Jock
and Scotty met after many years. Half a century
agone they were cronies in the land o’ oats, and
they had cruised together round the shearing
sheds of sunny Loan-land—hence their effusive-
ness on meeting. Jock was the wealthiest landed
proprietor in the district. Scottie was on the
wallaby, chasing a crust at a slow gait. “Weel,
hoo’re ye gettin’ on, Jock?” queried Scottie.
*‘ Preetty weel, considerin’ the land-tax, and the
income-tax, and the ither Socialistic taxes. Hoo’s
yersel’ ?” “ Ba-ad, mon, ba-ad ! ” quoth Scottie ;
“can ye help me, mon?” Jock could, but not
with money, that might be spent on the barley
bree ; so he took Scottie to the butcher’s and
bought him the understanding of a prime wether !
An hour later Jock sat down to carve that self-
same understanding, for'the astute Scottie had
sold the limb to Jock’s wife for the price of four
tanglefeet! The.date of the crime was August 18.
“ Chiffonier ” wants to know :
I’ve used up the few brains a rather niggardly Nature
has thrown at me in an endeavor to solve subjoined :
(1) Why Greid, M.H.R., still clings to the dinginess and
hopelessness of a political life ? (Said man could, I’m
convinced, have the refusal of some real clinking jobs
such as High Commissioner, Judge of the Appellate
Court, Police Magistrate, King of Papua, &c.) He’ll never
get down again on the right of Frederick Holder at the
table in the Reps.
(2) Why W. M. Hughes, Tom Brown, Page of Maranoa
(or is it Artesia?), Fowler, &c., are Foreign-traders?
(3) Why an allegedly sane and democratic community
votes to seat in “the Legislative halls of the nation ”
people like Cook, “ Johnsing,” Wilks, &e. ?
(4) What, in the name of High Heaven, has this coun-
try done to be burdened with obsolete, useless, expensive
tomfoolery like Legislative Councils, imported State
Governors (whose “ trousis " turn up automatieallv when
tis raining in “ Rotting ” Row), Ushers of the Black Rod, '
Clerks of the Greasy Pole, and such bosh ?
(5) Who invented Kelly, M.H.R., and why?
Two items from the Rand Daily Mail,
same date:—
The output for May, totalling 461,2020 z., is worth
within £40,938 of two millions sterling, and it looks as if
in another two months that colossal sum will have been
exceeded. . . .
It is pitiable to see so many able-bodied and intelligent
workmen quitting the country. An endless procession
wanders up and down the Reef looking for free meals and
stray shillings.
Cohen gets the gold. The British population, to
help whom the war against the Boers was said to
have been waged, gets the .depression, and the
fresh air, and the wandering, and the eternal
search for the meal that isn’t there and the bob
that is absent.

“T.E.D.”: In the arid districts of South
Australia through which runs the railway line
from Petersburg to Broken Hill, where the
abundance of mineral outcrop and lack of rain
preclude the possibility of agriculture, the woolly
caterpillar just now may be found in millions.
A month ago the crawlers, in strings of varying
length, might have been seen wending their way
towards Broken Hill, but. at present they are all
travelling to West. Whether they are gifted
trith a knowledge of Euclid I know not, but they
are evidently aware that the shortest distance
between two points is a straight (or railway)
line, and as they like a smooth track on which to
travel, they mount the rails, and thereby become
a nuisance to the engine-drivers, as their squashed,
in-easy bodies prevent the wheels getting a
grip of the rails, and in consequence trains
daily lose a considerable amount of time.
I travelled from Paratoo to Oulnina last week by
goods train, and we lost half-an-hour, the wheels
revolving at a great pace as they skidded round.
The trainmen’s language was a revelation. The
drivers fix straw envelopes (the bottle, of course,
having been carefully extracted) to the sand-pipes
of their engines, and try to brush the caterpillars
off that way, but, as a rule, the straw wears out
before the end of the caterpillar line (sometimes
over a mile in length) is reached. Thank good-
ness, the pest is confined to practically barren
country. It would be anything but a cheerful
prospect for the unfortunate cocky if, in addition
to four horses, three cows, a pig, and half-a-dozen
children, he had to find grub for a few million
hungry caterpillars. However, we live in an
enlightened age, and I expect in a few small years
we shall see a company started to shear the insects,
and caterpillar-wool clothes will be the fashion.
Certain it is that an acre of this land can keep more
caterpillars than it can sheep.

Australasian Branch, Melbourne.
The Inky Way.
The Brisbane Telegraph and Observer recently set a
trap for the Daily Mail, which they seem to suspect of
stealing their heavy matter. They printed under bold
headlines the story that one Van Dooyen, or Van Dogged,
or something had arrived from the Sandwich Islands to
pursue his industry of Angora goat farming ; being driven
from his old haunts by the presence in the Islands of a
poisonous grass “ botanically known as raravis dala-
malii, called by the natives red-hot food.” Ex-
tensive details were given of the proposed removal
of large quantities of goat to Queensland. The Mail
however, did not fall into the trap and rush into print
with more details about the goats and the grass. There
was a wooden humorousness and a labored mirth about
the Angora that warned it off. But it came out next
morning with a scathing notice as to contemporary
reliability, and concluded : “ The paragraph, to place the
most charitable construction upon it, furnishes an
instance of how bogus information may be disseminated
through ignorance, though, it must be confessed, it says
very little for the value at which those responsible rate
the intelligence of their readers.” Goats are a painful
subject just now.
* * * *
A curiosity of journalism is the Mahly Daily. It is a
single sheet, printed on both sides, and its contents run
to six columns, ten inches long. It is issued gratis, and is
crowded up with small advts. The remainder is mostly
paragraphic tribute to the glory of “ The Village.”
* * * *
The just-published autobiography of Bussey, a London
journalist, states that when Bright said in Parliament
that Lowe and others had formed a party of the Cave of
Adullatn, the editor of a leading paper said to the chief
of staff : “ Our reporter does not seem to have understood
the allusion.” “Oh, yes,” says the chief, “everyone
knows the ‘ Arabian Nights’.’-’ A country editor sent to
another in the same town for a Virgil to verify the
reference.
* * * *
A while back that humorous publication, Melbourne
Herald, used to have stock headlines which had to be
used under pain of dismissal. For instance, the Divorce
Court was headed, “ Breaking the Bonds,” and the Agri-
cultural Show had to be called “Agricola” to avert the
heavens falling. Now a new order has been instituted,
and apparently the cross-head department has been en-
trusted to a poet, for the headlines tell a story in a few
brief words placed wrong end on. A murder is an-
nounced “ Killed,” and below it “ A Man Was,” while the
story of a crime has above it, “ Three Storey House,”
with underneath, “ Burglars Break Into,” and the poli-
tical situation is set forth, “ Not Resign ” “ Mr. Bent
Will.” It is understood that this innovation is regarded
as stimulating to the readers’ imagination, like beginning
a novel at the last chapter, and working backwards.
* * * *
“M.M.”: “Ropata” must have been counting up
Filchitt’s adjectives same time as I was adding up how
many original cartoons and caricatures a certain copy of
Life contained. Found forty-nine illustrated jokes and
other things, every one of which was “ lifted ” from some
other publication. Six of them had the honor of being
ticketed “ The Bulletin.” “ Bleeds ” clips articles from
all and sundry, and the flattering acknowledgments
are at times amusing. For instance: “The San
Francisco Argonaut collects the best of the world’s
stories and redresses them in interesting form.
Here are three samples.” Another: “ A month
or two back we noted ” (how kind of Filchitt to
note) “ some short stories running in M'Clcre’s under
the title of ‘ Arizona Nights,’ S. E. White. The writer
has collected a number of ranchmen’s yarns, cleverly
garnished in his own inimitable style.” Then follows the
story holus-bolus, and not a penny to pay. The climax
is reached in the Answers to Corres. page, where the
paper tries to fire off second-hand “ hostile notices.”
* * * *
“O’F.”: Burrowing among newspaper files of the far-
back year 1806,1 find that ‘ 1 rash act ” was the regular
journalistic term even then for the deed of the person
who hanged himself with deliberation or gunned the top
of his head off. It is surely time to give “rash act” a
decent pension as an overworked centenarian, and to fine
anyone who in future arouses it from its sleep in the
Home for Worn-out Phrases.
• * * •
“G.,” writing from Tas.: Ido not regard the labors
of your correspondents, “Kappa” (B. 28/6/’O6) and
“Gravedigger” (B. 2/8/’O6), as particularly herculean.
Some years ago I threw up a clerical billet to enter
journalism, taking on the editorship and management of
a 24-page weekly at £4 a week. My duties consisted of
writing four columns of leader matter, four columns of
random drivel under a pen-name, eight columns of pars,
scissoring suitable fill-up stuff, proof-reading, etc. The
boss being an ambitious M.P., I had to initiate and sus-
tain periodical controversies in the daily papers, calling
attention to his great and unobtrusive public services.
When nobody took any notice of the first letter—which
was usually the case —I had to fake replies in a disguised
hand. In addition I had to keep the books, canvass for
ads.—no ads. no pay ; address wrappers (fortunately, few
were .required, and I did not try to push the circulation),
and publish.' At election time I hadto act as secretary,
committee, organiser of meetings, and sometimes as an
enthusiastic and unanimous audience. The paper is now
dead, although the politician lives and thrives.
Dear Bulletin.— You were mistaken in
asserting that the Chilian earthquake was not due
to a protest of Providence against the rise of
Socialistic legislation. The Chilian Government
has for some years past held a monopoly of the
coastal shipping trade along Chili’s protracted
shore. That’s up against your duckhouse.— Anti-
Sosii.
THE PEOPLE WHO MAKE THE NEWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I will sing you a short, paradoxical song—
A ballad of devious ways,
A ballad of people who all go wrong
And never get word of praise ;
A ballad of people who can’t go right
Whatever the path they choose,
Of people who murder, and steal, and fight,
The people who make the news.
When Tummy, the King, has forgotten to sneeze,
And the Queen has no ailment at all ;
When the Kaiser is cussedly taking his ease,
And the birth-rate’s forgotten to fall;
When there isn’t a really new lie to relate,
Nor a truth to distort to a lie ;
When all the principal men of the State
Most firmly refuse to die ;
When Parliament’s over and members at rest
From the wearisome waggle of chin ;
When the Cups have been run, and there isn’t a “test,”
And the war declines to begin ;
When there’s never a myth or a “ hatter” a-near,
And the Freetraders’ bogies have fled ;
When the tariff, like most of the industries here,
Is sleeping the sleep of the dead ;
When elections are over, and war-fevers gone,
And the markets are stable at last;
When shipwrecks are “ off,” and no accidents “ on,”
And the drought is a thing of the past;
It is then that the scribe who has nothing to write—
Nor much in this world to lose—
Will hail, with a hail of great delight,
The people who make the news.
Sing ho 1 for a gruesome murderer, then,
And ho ! for his gory deed,
When never a one of the world’s great men
Is good for the price of a feed ;
For it’s cake and wine in the corpse they see,
And the hanging means good brown booze.
For sovereigns grow on the gallows-tree
For the people who write the news.
N.S.W.
ERN. R. EMERSON.

The German law against unfair competi-
tion is rigorously enforced, and recent Teutonic
legal decisions should help to stiffen Bill Lyne’s
backbone. Take these instances
For offering certain goods for sale under cost price in
order to attract customers, the court condemned the
defendant as being guilty of an offence against the law.
To sell at cost price means charging customers the
price which tlie seller paid at the factory.
Bill should look Australian shoddy men squarely
in the eye and say, “ That’s the way I’m going.”
\nd here is how the German law deals with the
petty swindle of the smart little party, whose
business letters display the photograph of a mag-
nificent, many-storeyed establishment, whereas
in reality he carries on work in a sort of enlarged
hencoop, with the aid of a few undersized assis-
tants ?
Views on business papers of factories and showrooms
must represent those places as they actually are, seeing
that they serve as advertisements.
Bill Lyne has merely started on the road to fraud
suppression, and instead of growing timid when
Shoddy howls, he should set himself for another
onward stride.
Thus the Eastern World (Yokohama) :
We believe that Japan is preparing for another war
with China. Japan must have money, much money,
many hundred millions of money, and a war with China,
that'would not only pay for itself, but which would also
pay off the present crushing war debt and make all
China the treasury of Japan, would be popular from one
end of the country to the other.

Anyone who has studied the history of medicine knows there is a vast
difference between the diseases of to-day and of centuries ago. How
is it, then, that in this scientific age, Consumption (the white man’s
scourge) and other dread lung and throat diseases daily kill thousands
of the world’s best citizens ?
The cause lies in ineffective treatment. People regard too lightly
“a slight cold” or “persistent cough”; they do not view them as
heralds of deadly lung-destroying diseases, insidiously undermining
the bodily health.
Observation proves that no person is so immune from lung and throat
affections than the Swiss Shepherd tending his sheep on the slopes of the
Alps, breathing hourly into his lungs the rich resinous vitalising
atmosphere of the Pine Forests in which he dwells.
It is a study of this fact that has led to the invention of the
Peps tablet as an entirely novel treatment for pulmonary troubles-
As it slowly dissolves on the tongue it impregnates the whole of
the delicate membranes lining mouth, throat and lungs with its
healing and antiseptic fumes.
It is a bactericide of uncommon power, and annihilates the
worst disease by first annihilating the germs that have been the
cause of the trouble.
Peps help to repair the damage wrought upon the delicate
tissues by the wheezing and barking in bronchitis or by the
hacking cough of consumption. They cure the worst cold or cough.
Miss Marie Narelle, the popular Australian Soprano, says of Peps : —“ I know of
nothing better to relieve huskiness and prevent sore throat.”
Peps for Children*
Peps, because of their purity, potency, and pleasant
taste, are excellent for children. They thoroughly disinfect
the mouth and nasal organs; and clear the throat when
breathing is heavy or impeded. It does the lungs good to
bathe them in the fumes from a dissolving Pep.
When there is whooping cough in the house, Peps will
be found simply indispensable. A liberal supply of Peps
will ease the nasty cough, and prevent other members of the
family from catching the same complaint. Peps are an
insurance for health and strength.
eps
An Unique Family Medicine,
PEPS are the latest and best family medicine for coughs, colds,
chill, influenza, lung-weakness, bronchitis, difficult breathing,
wheezing, tonsilitis, asthma, consumption’s hacking cough, cold, croup,
children’s colds, whooping cough, sore throat, huskiness, and loss of
voice. They are invaluable to clergymen, teachers, lawyers, singers,
and public speakers.

Business, Robbery, etc.
A prominent American citizen, to the British
juvenile Smoking Committee : —“ Worse than Chicago
; ;aned meat is the American cigarette—the worst thing
jur country sends to you. You ought to prohibit the
sale of cigarettes imported from America on account of
the exceedingly cheap quality of the tobacco ordinarily
employed, the filthy surroundings from which it was
gathered, and the drugs used. The American cigarette-
maker devises most ingenious concoctions to please the
jialate, such as cocaine and laudanum.”
* * * *
Australian clothing people and button users
can now help along a new Australian industry, while
lowering the cost to themselves, by co-operating in the
proposed establishment of a factory of pearl buttons and
M.O.P. goods. The Torres Straits pearlers are the people
who propose it. The only chance of the pearl industry
continuing payable for white men is the wiping out of
the buyers at this end, and the buyers in London, and
the manufacturers in Belgium, and all the army of middle-
men, who take away Australian shell and bring Australia
buttons. Pearlers and clothiers and fancy goodsmen
should be able to make a co-operative factory among
them, and once it is a success, the boundaries of the
Commonwealth wouldn’t hold them long. There would
be an export trade pretty soon, as Torres shell is the
finest in all the world.
* * * *
Much, if not most, of the silky-oak furniture
that the Chow cabinet-makers in Melbourne and Sydney
turn out is made of fruit-cases. The finest silky oaks in
Australia grow back of Mourilyan and Geraldton, where
the Queensland bananas come from ; and when he packs
his fruit at all, the local 'Ah Hang, who has the business
in his own paw, packs it in silky-oak cases. It involves a
cruel waste of a very fine wood ; but it saves freight on
furniture timber, and Ah Hang makes his cases pay for
themselves.
* * * *
Some of the tricks of the opal trade came out
during the hearing of a case of assault between a White
Cliffs opal gouger and a buyer. The chief trick consists
in manipulating the poorer opal—in the local vernacular,
“painting”it. “Runners” are then employed to sell it
to rival buyers for more than its value. It was alleged by
the battered gouger that the buyer accused him of run-
ning “ painted ” opal for another buyer, and that this led
to someone being “dealt a hand.” Evidence was con-
flicting enough for the P.M. to dismiss the case ; but, for
all that, opal must now be added to the list of possible
fakes. At least it is susceptible to “ doctoring ” in the
rough state.
* * * »
Queer advt. from the Opal Miner (White
Cliffs, N.S.W.): —
NOTICE.
TT having come to my knowledge that miners are
afraid of being brutally assaulted when offering
their opal for sale, I beg to assure them that when
they enter my office with opal for sale that I.will ex-
tend unto them ever,v business courtesy, and if I
cannot agree with them on a price for the parcel, I
will guarantee that no miner will receive from me a
punch in the eye or a split ear for offering his goods
to me for sale.
HY. ABOTOMEY.
It appears as though there has been some unpleasantness
at White Cliffs.
• » * *
“Carvossa” : Over here in S. Australia we are
a porridge-eating people, and used to favor the imported
article. But the product of the oatmeal mills at Mt.
Gambier is gradually driving the foreign-made stuff out.
Besides being quite as good, it is cheaper and fresher. As
a result of its sales falling off, the representatives of one
of the much-boomed and extensively-advertised freak
foods found themselves with an enormous stock in bond.
To get rid of it, they' opened the cases, took out the car-
toons, and made a heap of the contents (about 30 tons).
Then they added 15 per cent, of crushed maize, to make
it unusable as a breakfast food and to save the bulk of
the duty ; and the compound was sold as pigs’ dinners.
“Mullalyup”: Next to the jabbering Asiatic, the
Illicit pearl-buyer is the curse of Broome (W.A.). He is as
plenteous as leaves in Vallombrosa (wherever that may
be). Probably a fourth of the pearl output goes
into his side pocket. There is no danger as far
as the law is concerned, and no one ever hears of a
“ snide ” pearl-buyer being captured. The only thing to
be feared is Asia’s kris or jiu-jitsu strangle-hold. It was
while trying to buy a stolen pearl that bagman Liebglid
met his fate, for which three men swung; He was be-
guiled on board a pearling-boat ostensibly to look at and
chaffer for a dishonestly-acquired gem, the simple inten-
tion of Asia being to make him a dead remains, and then
go through him for the pearls it suspected him of having
dishonestly acquired from other murky sons of Sheitan.
And so far as the remains were concerned Asia was very
successful.
In the course of its bustling life, Sydney Fire
Brigade has groped together a lot of facts about the way
the trouble starts. In the last two years, in city and
suburbs, the men have been called out 1030 times —not
counting the times when they couldn’t find anything
after they had got there ; and they only own to not
knowing the cause of the blaze in 81 cases. By far the
most common is the match that was thrown down and
allowed to take its chances ; 327 times this casual habit is
entered up. Candle-flames stand next in the brigade’s
black books; after that rubbish-fires and the accident
that happens to the baby with the box of matches to
play with. The brigade hasn’t got any confidence worth
mentioning in the discredited rat that got at the
matches, though he is allowed to have done five lots of
mischief in 1905 ; and there is only one recorded case of a
Sydney or suburban fire caused by lightning in the past
four years.
# * * »
The most costly section of railway owned by
N.S. Wales is the mile and a half that carries the squeal-
ing pig and the other great natural resources down to
Darling Harbor. It ran to £527,690 a mile. For a long
section, that one from Milson’s Point to Hornsby has
made the largest hole in Uncle Cohen’s pocket—
£4B,699 per mile. The very cheapest has been the
Tarrawingee line, which runs from Broken Hill
to nowhere, and hasn’t quite enough energy to
get there. A syndicate owned it once, but tired of the
profitless job, and sold the whole outfit to the State for a
price which, with renewals, &c., only stands in at £BOB
per mile. Roundly, comparing railways and tramways,
it costs half as much again to lay the average tramway
line as the other. The Ocean-street line cost just under
£60,000 a mile ; and, outside the city, the Bondi and
Coogee lines averaged over £25,000.

“C.R.” : The competition between butter fac-
tories in this district (S. Gippsland) is very keen, and
some of the means used to secure the custom of the
honest cow-man are remarkable. For instance, Jones,
who is a shareholder and supplier of the Yamyam butter
factory, missed the carrier one day last summer, and his
can of cream, containing the collection of a fortnight,
stood out on the road for several warm days,
simmering and stewing, with increasing froth. When
discovered, the contents had arrived at the loud
stage of fermentation. The lid of the can was blown
off, and angry smells rushed out, and many strange
objects, like little whales, rolled and plunged in the
bubbling slop which once was cream. Something had to
be done. So Jones—who is an emergency man every
time—rushed off home, and brought back a bucketful of
new milk, which he emptied into the can. And, as he
stirred the mixture about with his whip-handle, he
remarked that it would sweeten it up a bit, and make
up for what had “biled over.” Just then the Claypool
cream waggon came along, and the can was loaded
on, for Jones didn’t care to send it to his own
factory. And the Claypool butter factory, wishing to
secure a new customer, paid Jones for a full can of first-
class cream on a record butter percentage. But, in spite
of this generous treatment, Jones was ungrateful ; for
he went around amongst his neighbors and told them of
his experience with many joyful and sardonic bellowings,
and his remarks created the impression that Claypool
butter couldn’t amount to much when it was made of
slop such as Jones had sold to it. At the annual meeting
of the Claypool factory the directors spoke bitterly about
the disloyalty of both suppliers and shareholders !
A Born Actress.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gwendoline jones walked slowly
down the street. Her dress was of the
deepest black ; her pale, pretty face wove
a look of sad resignation, mingled with import-
ance. Both expressions deepened as a girl of her
acquaintance crossed the road and greeted her
with a brusquely sympathetic “Hello, Gwen!
What’s up? Nothing wrong at home, I hope ! ”
Gwendoline lifted pathetic eyes that seemed
heavy with unshed tears. “ Haven’t you heard?”
she sighed. “It’s my young man. He died of
typhoid last week. We only got the news
yesterday.”
“Didn’t know you had a young man,” said the
other girl, staring.
The mourner stiffened slightly. “ The engage-
ment has been kep’ secret,” she explained with
dignity, “but we have been attached to one
another for more’n a year. He was a doctor in
South Australia, and we was to have been married
next June. It’s hard. I try to bear up at ’ome
for pore mother’s sake, but if you only knew the
aching void—the blight that has fallen and—and
crushed the sunshine out of my life —the ”
“Yes, yes,” interrupted her companion, writh-
ing uneasily under this deluge of sentiment. “I’m
real sorry for you, Gwen, and I didn’t mean to
suspicion that you hadn’t got a young man—
though ’ow you come to mike the acquaintance of
a doctor beats me.”
“It was when I was bad with fever,” said
Gwen, hastily. “Reggie (that’s his name) was
over here for a ’oliday, and' Dr. Wise was
consulting with him, an’ he told me that the
first time he set eyes on me he felt I was the
only girl in the world for him.” ,
She dabbed at her eyes with a dainty black-
edged handkerchief. “I don’t suppose I’ll ever
get over it,” she whimpered. “I’m not as
strong as most, and I’ve got more heart than
some—and I feel as I can’t bear it. I’ve always
liked you, Fan. When I’m gone, you’ll see
that there’s flowers on my grave every Sunday.
I couldn’t bear to feel as I was forgotten ! ”
Kind-hearted Fanny fidgeted unhappily, and
attempted clumsy consolation. “ Look here,”
she said, “don’t you be so down. There’s as
good fish in the sea as ever was caught—l mean
you’ll forget him after a bit—leastways, time
will soften the blow like. Oh, well, I must be
going—goodbye.” She beat a retreat, troubled
by after-thoughts of consolations she might have
offered.
The story caused much gossip in the work-room,
where Gwendoline was not a favorite. Her
manner copied from those members of the
British aristocracy who “sweep disdainfully”
through the pages of cheap fiction had shut
her off from her fellow-workers. Still it was felt
to be “ hard luck on Gwen,” and, though two or
three girls said it served her right for putting on
airs, the general feeling was sympathetic.
One of the girls had met her, looking pale and
pathetic in her black frock, and learned from her
that she would not be back for a month, as she was
just leaving for South Australia, having been sent
for “ to settle up poor Reggie’s affairs.”
“ Queer start that,” said the girls.
Some weeks later, as Gwendoline’s brother
Tom was piling a miscellaneous lot of furniture
into a delivery cart, he was accosted by Fanny.
“ Is your sister back ? ” she inquired.
“ Never been away as I’ve heard of,” said Tom,
grumpily, straightening the kink in his back.
“ Been mooning round the house in a black dress
this month past, and if you know what’s been up
with the gel, you’d better out with it.”
“Don’t tell me you didn’t know about her
young man she was in mourning for! ” said
Fanny, reproachfully; “the dootor as died in
South Australia ; and there was papers and things
that she had to go and settle up.”
Tom whistled. “My word!—she has been
pullin’ yer leg,” said he, succinctly. "She didn’t
know no doctor in South Australia, and,” he
added, thoughtfully, “there’s times when I’m
blowed if she knows whether she’s herself or a
character out of one of them fool books that she’s
always porin’ over. Anyway, she’s right out o’
mourning now, there’s no doubt about that. I see
her this afternoon with a sorter red dress and
yaller roses in her hat, going to meet young
Tompkins from the oookshop. He don’t care
about these die-away notions, young Tompkins
don’t.”
NOR M.
It is evident that, in helping Japan to
secure “the open door” for the trade of all
nations in Manchuria, Britain has again fallen in
heavily. This is what an English journal, the
Field, prints, from a man who saw what he writes
about:—
What has occurred is partly due to the complacent
belief that the departure of the Russian and the entry of
the Japanese . . . marked the inauguration of free and
unrestricted trade for the future. But. . . the astute
Japanese are straining every nerve to obtain as tight a
grip upon the trade of Manchuria as upon that of Korea.
Such enterprise was never a feature of the Russian occu-
pation of Manchuria ; and, if the question is regarded
from that point of view, it must be acknowledged that
the state of things existing at that time was better than
it is at present for the British merchant. . . . The control
of Russian military and civil law, disagreeable as it may
have been . . . did not at any rate, introduce the cheap
competition into their businesses which the re-advent of
the Japanese has done, and which will increase in severity
year by year. . . . With all these new forces at work, it
would be miraculous if British interests on the spot did
not suffer in the long run.. . . Reliance upon Govern-
mental assistance will not avail them in the days to come,
for British political influence in that part of the Far East
is now a very minor quantity.
There is not the slightest doubt now, that
Japan, in negotiating the famous treaty with
Britain, was really working a gigantic confi-
dence trick upon the Ma Country. The Tory
Government, when it signed that treaty, bought
the biggest political gold-brick of the century.
“ H.” digs up an interesting point: —
A fearsome press roar in the Amurkan papers concern-
ing the destruction of the municipal titles office of’Frisco
by the recent fire discloses the fact that whenever the
Federal Government of the U.S.A. erects a building it
secures a clear space round it. When the fire swept over
’Frisco the whole of the Federal buildings escaped. The
State and municipal buildings had also been originally
put up in open spaces, but corrupt Boards of Supervisors
had alienated the clear ground, and all the State and
municipal buildings, with their contents, were destroyed.
As the result, it seems that the man who swears hardest
will get the best allotments, and the most of them, in the
re-arrangement of things, and the man who isn’t a good
perjurer will get none.

The Nomad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The curse of Ishmael was on him. He was destined
to be a wanderer on God’s earth, and all things apper-
taining-to the wild life of the forests were music in his
ears.” — Setox Merriman.
HE journeyed north by the burning plains
and south by the ranges dim.
Seeking ever the unknown land that his
soul had described to him ;
Seeking the land of his youthful dreams that ever
was faint and far,
And setting his course by the noonday sun, or at
night by the northern star.
It seemed that his heart was light and leal to the
dreams of his callow days ;
It seemed that his path was bright with blooms in
the sweet of the sunlit ways ;
It seemed that the tale of his boy’s romance
would ever be coming true
When he rode in the sun, and slept in the stars,
as out to the north he drew.
But the sun went by in a splendid train and the
drifting days went by ;
The long plain-grasses sprouted and grew, and the
autumns burned them dry.
The great plains whispered across the night, and
the great hills glowed by day,
And the moons came out of the mystic east and
hurried the hours away.
The wild dog called to him out of the night with
a human fear and pain,
When fire and drought swept over the hills and
wasted the fruitful plain ;
And many a homestead flamed and fell, leaving
its chimneys bare,
Like tombstones marking the graves of hopes of
those that had labored there.
He passed by cities enwrapt in gloom and the
griefs of a thousand souls ;
He heard from afar the moaning seas as they bit
on the ragged shoals ;
He found it writ in the fairest place that pain is
the one thing real,
And saw in the eyes of the toiling man the grave
of the lost ideal.
And so by freshets that roared in spate, and the
downs where the breeze was bland,
He came at length to that great lone place, the
heart of his native land ;
Then did the brave soul fail at last, the thread of
his life was cut.
The ghosts of his hopes saluted him, and the book
of his days was shut.
The hot sun stared on his upturned face with a
fierce, unwinking eye ;
The cold moon softened the features grim as she
rode in her tranquil sky ;
Till the years have made him one with the dust,
and hid him with drifts of sand,
And taken him into that great lone heart, the
heart of his native land.
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.
Mr. Barnes, Matchmaker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I NEVER yet see the genuine bushman
as could get past a good ’orse, a bad pub,
or any woman, an’ I don’t wanter,” said
Mr. Barnes, defiantly.
“Mind yer, though, you chaps ain’t like wot
-we uster be in my young days. A wild lot we wuz—
no mistake. There wuz me mate, Jerry Lane, wot
you’ve ’eard me tell ov time an’ agen. ’E wuz
•alius in trouble some’ow. ’E’d get trackin’ round
wi’ a gal makin’ fierce love to ’er, an’ they’d fix
ther weddin’ day—an’ then Jerry’d ferget an’ go
away, or ’e’d see sum thin’ ’e’d like better, or ’e’d
be shorter cash, or summit. It wuz real ’umerous
the way ’e finally did get spliced.”
The old man chuckled softly to himself, took a
huge drink of beer, and began his yarn, reflec-
tively scratching his head with his pipe-stem.
“Jerry an’ me wuz in that Gaskar Creek gold
rush—when fivfc ’undred bloomin’ fools swarmed,
like flies, over sand ’ills where there weren’t
-enough metal for to make a respectible-sized ter-
baccer tag. One night I wuz cornin’ ’ome late
from ther claim, thinkin’ on ’ow all ther fun an’
beer an’ wimmin’ ’ad gorn from life, when I ’ears
16ud, angry voices cornin’ from our ’ut. I ’urried
•up to ther door an’ near broke me neck over a
whole ’eap ov portmanties, an’ boxes an’ things.
I struck a light, an’ on one of the boxes wuz ther
name ‘Mrs. Martha Whistle.’ ‘Ho! ho!’
thought I, ‘’ere’s trouble.’
“I opens ther door, an’ there wuz a strange
sight! In the farthest corner, ’uddled on ’is box,
wuz Jerry, lookin’ mortal scared an’ ’shamed.
Between ’ini an’ ther door wuz a stout ole lady,
•dressed out in a terrible big ’at wif a terrible big
feather. Very red in ther face she wuz, an’ she
•stood there in front o’ Jerry with ’er ’ands on ’er
’ips, lookin’ like ther Queen ov Ingland on
washin’ day. _ Seated on my box, wot stud
beneath the winder, wuz a gal. She wuz weepin’
bitter inter a ’ankerchif ’bout the size ov ther ace
•o’ diamonds. I couldn’t see much ov ’er face, but,
judgin’ by t’other parts ov ’er wot wuz showin’,'
she wuz orlright. The ole lady, ’oo from ther
name on the portmanter I reckoned wuz Mrs.
Martha Whistle, wuz so tuk up wi’ wot she ’ad to
to Jerry that she tuk no ’eed o’ my entrance.
The gal did, though. She guv a sly look at me,
•an then she wiped ’er eyes and straightened ’er
air with ’er ’ands. I could see she wuz struck on
™ e - J wuz a ’andsurn young feller in those days,
■though, o’ course, I’ve lost some ov me good looks
mow.
“ ‘ So, now, wot are y’u goin’ to do about it? ’
■the ole lady sed to Jerry, very stern, foldin’ ’er
arms acrost er ample bosom, as they say in ther
story books. Ther sigh ov relief that Jerry give
when ’e seed me would ’ave ruptured the spine ov
a snake. ‘ This is a welcum surprise,’ sed I, with
a cough.
~ ; ‘ Me mate,’sed Jerry, sulkily, wi’a wave ov
* by way ov interduction.
1 Ow are yer, mum ? ’ sed I, grabbin’ ’er paw
ln ‘ Y’u’ll surely be Mrs. Martha Whistle,
wot Mr. Lane ’ere ’as so often spoke ov to me very
affectsnunate.’
“ She seemed a bit took aback at my knowin’
’er name, an’ I could see she wuz flattered. She
scraped the clay orf ’er ’and wot she ’ad got orfer
mine. Then she started in ter explain the facts
ov ther case to me, an’ wot ’ad led to this visit.
‘ I’m a respectible married woman,’ she sed,
tappin’ ’er foot on ther floor, ‘ an’ I defy anyone
ter prove it, an’ wot I wants ter know is, wot is
’e goin’ ter do about it ? ’E comes along an’ sneaks
the young affeetshuns ov me ”
Say no more, mum,’ interrupted I, soothin’ly.
‘ Sav no more, mum. Mr. Lane ’as tole me all.’
“Jerry sat up an’ gasped, an’ the gal took the
rag frutn ’er eyes. ‘Yus, mum, me poor ole mate
’ere’—Jerry sniffed sorrowful—“’as tole me all
about the deep love ’e feels fer yer beautcheous
dorter, Mrs. Whistle ’
“ I knew then there wuz surmnat wrong. Jerry
groaned, an’ the gal give a snarly sorter larf, like
ther bark ov a disappointed dog, while, as fer the
ole wuman, she drew a circle in the air wi’ ’er nose
an’ remarked, freezin’ like : ‘ Then ’e lied ; she’s
me niece! ’
“ ‘O’ course, o’ course, yer niece—l remember
distinctly it wuz yer niece as ’e sed ’e wanted ter
marry, only ’
“ ‘Wot ther ’ell ’ sed Jerry, jumpin’ up.
““’Old yer jore,’ sed I, givin’ ’im a punch in
ther chest an’ knockin’ ’im back over ’is bunk
where ’e lay an’ cursed softly to ’isself— ‘ wanted
to marry only she wouldn’t ’ave ’im, so ’e tole me,’
I resoomed.
“‘Oh, Jerry!’ sed the gal, sittin’ up very
straight an’ lookin’ at ’im reproachful wi’ ’er big,
brown, bedroom eyes.
“ ‘Don’t believe a word ’e sez,’ shouted Lane.
I took no ’eed ov ’im.
“ “’E’s bin dyin’ to see’er. It’s bin Mabel this
an’Mabel that, day an’night, drunk or sober.’
That surprised even Jerry—my knowin’ ’er front
name so pat—but ’adn’t I spelt it out careful orfer
the big brassy broach wot she wore, stuck over
’er bows? ‘Now that y’u’re ’ere, where are y’u
thinkin’ ov sleepin’ ? It’s mortal cold in ther pad-
docks these nights.’
“ l ’ow dare you talk to me like that, young
man,’ the ole gal snarled ; ‘ I’ll ’ave y’u know I’m
a respectible married wuman, an’ defy enyone to
prove it ! We’ll stay at the best ’otel ’ere, o’
course ! ’
“ ‘ The best ’otel! Oh lor ! ’ Even Jerry larfed
—a thin ghost ov a larf. As fer me, I fairly
squelched onter ther floor. ‘ Ther best ’otel !
Sly Mick’s—oh, lor ! Y’u couldn’t stay at Sly
Mick’s, an’ it’s ther only place o’ ther kind ’ere;
why, they’d eat yer.’
“ ‘Yus,’ sed Jerry, spitefully, ‘they’d eat yer—
to ther last stay lace.’
“The poor young gal grew pale an’ began to
whimper. * Don’t cry, pretty one,’ said I, in me
best style. ‘ Take no notice of ’im. I’ll perfect
yer. I’ll turn all ’arm from yer.’
“‘Yer face ’d turn anything,’ sed Jerry. ’E
wuz gettin’ spiteful, so 1 decided to ’elp ’im no
more.
“I took ’er ’and an’ rubbed it gently. ‘Y’u
kin sleep in my bunk, mum,’ sed I to Mrs. Martha
Whistle.
“ ‘Sir, I’ll ’ave y’u know I’m a respect’
“‘An’ Mr. Lane an’ me’ll go over to Sly
Mick’s.’
“‘Oh, thank y’u so very much, Mr. Barnes,’
exclaimed Mabel. ‘lt is so ref reshin’ter meet a
gen’leman—there’s so few about! ’ An’ she cast
a cold eye over Jerry.
“ ‘ Oh, dam ! ’ sed ’e, an’ kicked the dorg.
“ Then ’e went out an’ slammed the door be’ind
’im, an’ the languige ’e used as ’e fell over the
portmantoos made the lamp flicker. Arter that,
it took me three-quarters ov a ’our to reassure ’em
as they wuz quite safe. Then, arter showin’ ’em
where they could find the damper an’ drippin’,
an’ sich ’ome comforts, an’ a drop ov gin (what
the ole lady swore she never touched, but wouldn’t
let me move fear the roomatics cum on suddint in
ther night), I left ’em, havin’ squeezed the gal’s
’and, an’ told ’er she wuz to call me Jim.
“ Jerry wuz waiting fer me outside. ’E come up
an’ shoved ’is face in mine very fierce. ‘ See
’erei!’ ’e snarled thro’ ’is clenched teeth, like the
villin in ther the-ater play I seed once ; ‘ see ’ere,
I’m agoin’ to punch y’u fair in the eyeball ! ’
“Now there’s no man livin’ ’as kin say Jim
Barnes ever refused a fight or a drink, but jist
then it wouldn’t ’ave suited me ter fall out wi’
Jerry, so I soothed ’im over.
“ Well, we wuz ’avin’ a drink inter Sly Mick’s,
when I sez to Jerry: ‘Are yer goin’ter marry
’er ?’
“‘No, certintly not! Wot about that girl
over Sand’urst way ? ’
“ ‘ That’s all right, then,’ sed I.
“ ‘ Wad’yer mean ? ’ ’e arst, suspicious.
“ ‘ Oh, nothin’,’ I tole ’im.
“’E kept quiet fer a long time. Then ’e
breathed ’ard an’ sed slowly, ‘ I s’pose you’ll be
goin’ ter marry ’er yerself, eh ? ’
“ ‘ Of course,’ I sed, ‘if you don’t want ’er I’ll
take ’er on.’
“ ‘ I’ll be damned if you do ! ’ shrieked he, real
savage.
“ ‘ Jist please yerself,’ I answered back. ‘ She’s
real shook on me.’
“ ‘ Grrrrh !!! ’ said Jerry, way down in ’is
throat. Then, arter finishin’ my beer, e’ wenter
bed.
“That night I explained ther sitwashun to Sly
Mick, an’ we called a select meetin’ ov ther boys.
You must know thet a coach called only once
a fortnight at Gaskar Creek, so that the wimmin
’ad no charnst ov gettin’ away, even if they
wanted to. But the fuss that ther boys made ov
Mabel, and the attenshun she got, muster made
’er satisfied to stay fer ever. Why, arter the
second day every man on ther field, married an’
single, ’ad proposed to ’er, bar meself an’
Jerry. Ole Stringy Steve even went so far as to
propose to Mrs. Whistle, ’im bein’ drunk at ther
time. Why didn’t I propose to the gal meself ?
Well, d’yer see, although she wuz so shook on
me, Lane ’ad played a low-down game an’ put me
pot on by tellin’ ’er a lie about me bein’ married
to a widdy an’ family, so thet when one night I
tole ’er ’ow I wuz a orphin, alone in ther wurld,
she up an’ tole me I wuz a liar wi’ six child’en,
an’ all ther satisfackshun left me wuz to go ’ome
an’ swipe Jerry in ther jore, which I did. Me
bein’ outer ther ’unt, so ter speak, I could see
thet Mabel’s affeckshuns centred onter Jerry
agen, though she treated ’im cold an’ crool an’
didn’t seem to wanter ’ave naught ter do wid ’im
no ’ow. Jerry ’imself wuz not eggsackly a plea-
sant companion, as you might say, those times.
’E’d sit by ’isself an’ sulk an’, wot’s worse, ’e’d
drink by ’isself an’ shout for no one. ’E threat-
ened to kill eny man wot wuz seen talkin’ to ’er,
but as every lad at ther Creek left orf work to
fetch an’ carry for ’er, ’e would ’ave ’ad a big eon-
track. ’E did start in ter keep ’is word, but ’ad
grew discouraged by the time Bobby M'Lean, a
heavyweight from the Hunter, ’ad finished wif
’im.
“ I’d give a pint ter know what Jerry’s feelin’s
wuz when, ther next Sunday mornin’, ’earin’ a
great trampin’ ov feet under ’is winder, ’e got up,
and, lookin’ out, saw ther ’ole population o’
Gaskar Creek lined up. ’E wuz a picter as ’e stud
there wi’ ’is face ajar, an’ takin’ us fer jim-jams.
We ’ad fifty chaps lined up like sodjers in a row^
big lumps ov clematis stuck in their ’ats an’ coats,
while their boots an’ clothes wuz brushed as clean
as a magsman’s. Some ’ad even gone ther length
ov ’avin’ a shave 1 The rest ov ther boys wuz
grouped round lookin’ on, while all ther dorgs in
ther wurld seemed to be strewed about the lands-
cape, fightin’ an’ arguin’ as to wot wuz ’appenin’.
“’Ullo there,’ sed Jerry, tryin’ to appear sif
’e wuz amoosed, ‘’oo’sded? Where’s ther gard-
ing-party ? ’
“No one tuk no notice ov’im. Not a word
wuz spoke. Then me an’ Mick posted ourselves
at th’ ’ead ov ther colum’, an’ give th’ order to
march. Slow an’ steady, wi’ never a word, we
filed down frum ther pub onter our ’ut in ther
hollow, where ther two wimmin still lived. Ther
dorgs wuz on a’ead, an’ ther rest ov ther popu-
lashun, except Jerry Lane, ’oo wuz frantically
dressin’, brought up ther rear. The ladies cum
outer ther ’ut when they ’eard us, an’, while
Mabel dashed back inside to tidy ’erself, Mrs.
Whistle stood wif ’er ’ands on ’er ’ips an’ ’er jore
set tight, eyein’ us wi’ disfavor. I lined ther
fifty up in fronter ther ’ut, while ther others all
formed a semi-circle round. Jist as Mabel come
out all a-blushin’, but lookin’ so neat that it
woulder made a Chinese idol glad, down the ’ill,
wif ’is braces flyin’ in ther wind, rushes Jerry. ’E
’ad ’is trousers on back to front, on’y one boot, no
’at nor coat, an’ ’is ’air thet wild ’e looked like a
yard-broom. ’E dashed up cussin’ an’ swearin’
an’ vowin’ thet someone ’ud pay for all this, an’
tried to break ’is way through the crowd to where
we wuz lined up. But, as nobody’d budge an’
let ’im through, all ’e could do wuz to dance up
an’ down on the outside tearin’ ’is ’air.
“ By this time Mick ’ad mounted on a couple o’
kerosene tins an’ begun to address ther meetin’.
4 Ladies, an’ yous blokes,’ ’e sed ; 4 we is gathered
’ere on a very solem’ occashun. A bright ray ov
sunshine ’as come an’ fastened onto our ’ard, cold,
beer-stained lives, an’ ’as brought more joy an’
pleasure to our midst than ther holdin’ ov four
dooces agen a king full. The name ov ther ray
o’ sunshine is Mabel! ’ Arter the cheerin’ ’ad
stopped 'e resoomed : 4 There is not a man among
us’ (’e pointed to Jerry, an’ added, ‘I don’t
inclood thet !’)
44 ‘Let me at ther ,’ screamed me mate, an’
it took six ov ’em to ’old ’im down.
—“‘As wouldn’t like to ’elp ’er change ’er
name.’
4 4 4 Hoobloomin’ ray ! ’ yelled everyone.
“ ‘But ther lady don’t seem to be able to make
up ’er little mind. Theffefore, we are ’ere to
decide for ’er. There are 50 young coves ’ere—
good-lookin’, too, is some ov ’em, if y’u don’t
examine ’em too close—wot is sober, domesticated
an’ sanitary, wot would make eny wuman ’appy,
so long as she weren’t too perticler. I’ll back ’em to
drink, eat or swap lies agen anything their own
weight. They can ready ther cyards agen any-
one. Hell can show ’em nothin’. Yet, kind
frens, there’s one thing they can’t do. They can’t
all marry Mabel ! There is only one way then.
They must draw lots fer ’er ! ’
“The angry protests ov ther wimmin and
Jerry’s violent language wuz drowned in ther
cheers wot followed. Then, arter everyone ’ad
’elped to kick ther dorgs into silence, Mick pro-
duced a long list ov names, an’ began foldin’ ’em
up an’ puttin’ ’em into a hat.
‘ 4 4 Does enyone else wan ter enter ? ’ he inquired,
lookin’ round.
44 ‘Better put Jerry Lane down,’ sed someone.
4 ’E might like to take a charnst.’
4 4 4 Seems hardly fair to ther gal,’ sed I, an’
although there wuz ’arf a ton ov miner sittin’ all
over’im, Jerry managed to mention wot ’e’d do
for me when ’e got me to ’isself. As fer ther
wimmin, they wuz so speechless wi’ surprise an’
fury, they couldn’t talk. The ole lady wuz red in
ther face, an’ ther way she kept openin’ an’
shettin’ ’er mouth like a Murray cod, I thought
she wus agoin’ to throw a fit for sure. Well, ther
names wuz all counted inter ther ’at, an’ Mick,
wif a perlite bow, arst Mabel would she pick one
out fer luck. She jist give him ther cold eye, an
turned ’er back on ’im.
44 4 Oh, well,’sed ’e, ‘someone else pick.’ A’
young feller named Leeds stepped out, an’ spittin’
on ’is ’ands, picks out a paper an’ opins it up very
slow. They'ad let Jerry up an’ ’e ’ad made’is
way through the crowd, an’ stood in front ov
Leeds. ’ls hands wuz openin’ an’ closin’, an’
seemed to be keepin’ time to ther ole gal’s jores.
Leeds ’ad jist got the slip ov paper unfolded, an’
wuz holdin’ it under ’is eye, when Jerry grabs it
from ’is and. ”E jist takes one glance at it, an’
then ’e throws ’isself in ther air, like ’e wuz shot,

44 ’E shoves ther paper into my ’and, an’ there

44 ‘Let’s see ’oo woulder come second,’ sed Mick,
