SYDNEY TESTIMONY.
FROM WATSON’S BAY. [
Mr. James Fotheringham, Watson’s Bay, late
6th Imperial Bushmen, writes : “Whilst in South
Africa with the 6th Imperial Bushmen, through
constant exposure to all sorts of weather, sleeping
in wet clothes, etc., I got a very severe attack of
lumbago. I was so bad that I could not get up off
the ground—let alone mount my horse. Two
military doctors had a go at me. One blistered
my back until all the skin was off ; and the other
gave me medicine, but neither of them did me
any good. Fortunately, a comrade came along
with a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and
before I had finished the box I was as right as ever
I was in my life. Since then I always keep a box
of the pills handy in case of the lumbago return-
ing, but, so far, I am glad to say, I have had no
necessity to take them. You may make whatever
use you like of this, as I consider Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills a boon to humanity, and I shall
always recommend them.”

Crusoe of Hull.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The Island of Juan Fernandez has been partially des-
troyed by the Chilian earthquake.”— News item.
I HAVE voyaged up and down,
Since I grew to be a man ;
Trough of sea and billow’s crown,
Since my roving first began ;
But my best of ships was when
I was a gay youth of ten.
Then —on lands in splendid seas—
Where now missionaries go,
Scents of tuberose in the trees,
Crystal streams of tinkling flow j
Never ice nor snow was nigh ;
Softly blew the winds on high.
In Romance’s caravel
Sailed before the zephyr soft;
And the ventecello swell
Brought the scents of field and toft;
And the captain of the show
Was the Admiral Defoe.
Sailed we forth from ancient Hull,
Freighted but with silks and gold
Silver that would never dull
All our men were strong and bold ;
Crusoe came with us to sea
And we dealt in piracy.
Crusoe this would not admit—
Frightened of the gallows tree ;
Now the danger’s past, I wit,
All our trade was piracy ;
Else, how is it that our freight
Was moidores and coins of eight ?
Else how was it that we left
All the higher, ship-hot seas ?
Else how could we be bereft
All those years of homes and ease ?
Hot were Eastern seas awhile,
We sailed wrest to Juan’s isle.
God, or Fate, or Providence
Knows dramatic unities ;
Our piratical offence
Called for vengeance of the seas ;
Wrecked were we on reef and sand ;
I, with Crusoe, swam a-land.
Helped him fell the mighty tree—
Burn it to the galley shape ;
Helped him struggle with the sea
Boiling round the craggy cape ;
And, with mattock and with spade,
Helped him build the strong stockade.
When his mem’ries bade him weep
I was near, and dried his tears ;
Bre xthed hope to him in his sleep,
Helped him through the lonely years ;
And, at last, my sympathy
Brought Man Friday from the sea.
Crusoe, and his Poll, and I
Walked about upon our isle;
And a many deaths did die,
Fearing myths and savage guile;
And, dumbfounded more and more,
Saw the footprints on the shore.
For the island was like youth
Dreaming in its solitude ;
Sky blue, sea blue, great as truth,
And our isle was but a rood—•
Glad, afraid, to see the sand—
As when Love first comes a-land.
Crusoe, Friday, Dreams and I
Frightened of the pirate ship—
In our hiding near the sky
Steeled the heart and firmed the lip ;
And one day we justice did
Bravely, though at first we hid.
Hanged the leaders of the plot;
Flogged Will Atkins to the bone ;
Quelled some mutineers with shot,
Made the rest to ache and groan ;
Taught these pirates manners—then
Treated them as gentlemen.
Force seismic swift may break—
Sink our island in the brine ;
But from Crusoe cannot take.
Nor from me, or his, or mine !
Crusoe’s island still is there,
Belted with divinest air.
For the billions yet to be—
For the boys who’ll read and know—
Crusoe’s Island decks the sea,
Palm above and wreck below;
Crusoe’s Island gems her bays
In the soft sub-tropic haze.
And, full often, ere my fate
Sends me downward to the sand ;
With moidore or'piece of eight
Shall our wreck strike on the land ;
And its cargoes then enhance
With the glamor of Romance.
I shall see the beach again !
See the coral-trash and weed !
See the red hibiscus stain !
They do bloom as if they bleed—
Spears ! and cocoanuts ! and then !
Be a dreaming boy of ten.
By the cape and jutting reef,
I’ll with Crusoe walk unseen ;
I will keep him through his grief,
In the stars’ soft velvet sheen—
And, when tired, be found, and go
Home with Admiral Defoe !
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
WHITSUNDAY PASSAGE.
A Tragedy of Respectability.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lA.M thirty-five and respectable—hor-
ribly indecently respectable. I arrived in
the world cursed with a conventional dis-
position and a long, straight nose (it is impos-
sible to be naughty with a long, drooping nose).
“Jane has never been silly,” mother says,
proudly, but oh, if she only knew how passion-
ately Jane has wanted to be silly.
Yes, I am thirty-five, and no one has ever even
put an arm about'my limp waist or tried to kiss
me. No one has ever written me a love-letter or
sent me flowers. Certainly, they are hard to get
in the bush, but that would not stop a lover in
Bookland.
The other night I stood and watched my cousin,
Nerida, and the jaclceroo, down at the slip-rails,
They had just got to the rub-head stage, when
mother came and stood beside me. “Good
gracious !” she said. “ You were never silly, like
Nerida is, Jane,” and I choked down my bitter-
ness, and said meekly, “ I hope not, mother.”
Ah, if only someone wanted me to be silly, and
would call me his “star,” or his “flower,” as the
jackeroo calls Nerida !
I tried to copy Nerida once to see if that would
make a difference. I sat on the verandah railings,
and swung my legs as Nerida does, and even tried
to smoke a cigarette one day when mother was
out and Mr. Brown was in. But he only looked
disgusted-though he laughs when Nerida does it.
, . , ~ . „ ,
Mother says the girls the men want are useful,
, ■, , ■ ? rm . ~ , ,
strong, sensible girls. I hey may want them, but
they don’t make love to them, unless they are
finished off with an attractive face. Nerida isn’t
sensible, and she can’t make anything but sponge-
cakes and chocolate-creams; but she has curly
hair, and even I admire her ankles.
It is really dreadful to be cursed with respecta-
bility. People say it is hard to be virtuous when
one is beautiful, but it is far harder to be wicked
when one is plain. I went down to Svdney to
stay with my aunt, and I made up my mind I
was going to be really wicked for once, just to see
what it felt like. I told aunt I was going to stay
with a friend for one night, and, instead, I took
all my money, and engaged a room in a hotel on
my own. It was the first big lie I ever told, and I
did feel dreadfully bad. When I took the room the
clerk smiled at me, and I felt beautifully worldly,
for it was the first time I had ever been smiled at
in an unpitying kind of way. I curled my hair,
put on my best white dress and my evening shoes,
and put a red bow in my hat, and then I went
out with delicious thrills of deliberate wanton
naughtiness running down my spine.
I went down the street, hoping someone would
come and speak to me, and invite me to go to the
theatre and supper as they do in books, so that I
mi S ht repudiate the offer—but no one did. I
showed my feet and my best white petticoat, but
no one took any notice. My feet grew tired, and
a lump rose in my throat as I wondered why it
was that I was cursed with respectability. At
last a boy walked by, and I nearly died of fright,
but he only said, “Does yer mother know yer
out?” and went on.
Then a little, red-haired rat of about 15 called out
“Good night, Cis,” and so I went to bed. It is-
impossible to be naughty when one is called “Cis.”
Mother and auntie think the theatre is im-
moral, and only just decent when one has a box
with curtains, so I made up my mind to go aIL
alone, and to the top gallery. At first I felt
as if everyone in the theatre was staring at me-
with accusing eyes, as I sat there, growing hotter
every minute. Then I found I was just a nothing
™ the crowd, and felt injured, for at home, in the
bush even the goannas know me I sat next to
old man with a cold in his head, and a woman
m a plush coat, who smelt of peppermint, and 1
was so miserable. I don’t know what went on
stage-wards. The orchestra squeaked doleful
music, and a lot of silly people ran about singing;
and dancing until my eyes ached and my head
whirled.
It was the audience that interested me. I
envied the smiling girls, with their beautifuliy-
dressed heads and pretty, low evening dresses,
the gleam of their white arms, the sparkle of
jewels on their rounded necks and heaving bosoms,,
the play of eyes and lashes as they talked to their
companions-for it all belonged to another world,
far, far awav from the sordid, drought-stricken.
T , , * . .
one I had lived in.
1 grudged them their pleasure, tlieir chocolates
and fine, V< and ’ most of all ’. the whispers that
brought the smiles to their lips, for t len ives
had held so much fun, and mine has alwavs been
full of worr y and drought.
sat bere with a hard ball of feeble cynicism
growing somewhere about the place one’s heart is
supposed to occur. Even the boy selling souvenir
fans and chocolates did not speak to me, and I
went out Wlth tbe crowd > y et feelm S utterl y
l° ne ly- Some were going to supper, some rushing
to cabs and carriages, others collecting in groups,
bld ab ba PPy and joyous. J was just a speck of
nothingness left behind them in the gaily-lighted
vestibule.
The crowd melted away like fat in the sun, and
a man who was shutting up came to me and said,,
<l re y° u waiting for anyone, Miss ? ” Then I
hurried away, for the pain of it all is that I have nq
one to wait for in the vestibule we call Life. My
P art is the invisible and useful one of keeping the
floors clean. It is so hard to be born colorless,,
domesticated and good, with the passion and
capacity for love and beauty that is the heritage
ever y woman, clever or rich, plain or beautiful,
